Attributes Enabling Action: Power, Will, and Appetite
The author enumerates the enabling action attributes and notes a dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of attributes that enable action according to the author, and the scholarly dispute regarding appetite/repulsion versus will/aversion.

Core Idea
The attributes that enable action are power, will/aversion, and appetite/repulsion—but do appetite and repulsion truly differ from will?

In this passage, the author moves to the second division of accidents—those that require life—and enumerates the attributes that enable action. These are power, will/aversion, and appetite/repulsion. However, a key question arises: are appetite and repulsion distinct from will and aversion, or the same? This brief text sets up an important theological and psychological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the three types listed and the mention of disagreement.
The Arabic is concise.
2
Identify the three categories
Pick out the three types: power (qudra), will and aversion (irada wa karaha), appetite and repulsion (shahwa wa nufra).
Note that will and aversion are paired, as are appetite and repulsion.
3
Understand the dispute
The passage highlights a disagreement: are appetite and repulsion the same as will and aversion, or different genera? Reflect on why this matters.
This dispute touches on whether appetites are a separate category of psychological states.
4
Connect to the broader context
The author concludes this is a brief indication of the types of accidents. Consider how this fits into the larger classification of attributes.
This passage completes the discussion of accidents that require life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الصفات التي تفيد المكنة من الفعل، فهي أنواع: الأول : القدرة ، والثاني : الإرادة والكراهية : والثالث : الشهوة والنفرة . وفيه خلاف في أن الشهوة والنفرة هل هما نفس الإرادة والكراهة ، أو جنسان مغايران للإرادة والكراهة ؟ فهذا هو الإشارة المختصرة إلى نوع الأعراض
As for the attributes that convey ability (imkan) for action, they are types: First: power (qudra), Second: will (irada) and aversion (karaha), Third: appetite (shahwa) and repulsion (nufra). There is disagreement about whether appetite and repulsion are identical to will and aversion or two genera different from will and aversion. So this is a brief indication of the types of accidents.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion of the second division of accidents (those that require life) by presenting the attributes that enable action. The author lists three types: qudra (power), irada and karaha (will and aversion), and shahwa and nufra (appetite and repulsion). A dispute is noted: are appetite and repulsion identical to will and aversion, or are they separate genera? The author concludes by saying this is a brief overview of the types of accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Appetite and will are identical and there is no difference.
Correction: The text notes a scholarly disagreement: some say appetite/repulsion are distinct from will/aversion, while others equate them. This is not settled in this passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed as an enabling action attribute?
A) Power
B) Will
C) Knowledge
D) Appetite

Key Takeaway
The attributes that enable action are power, will/aversion, and appetite/repulsion, with a dispute over whether the latter two categories are distinct from will/aversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you strongly desired something (appetite) but your will (irada) told you it was wrong. How did you choose, and what does that tell you about the relationship between your desires and your will? Will you now be more intentional about distinguishing them before acting?
?
The passage notes a dispute over whether appetite and repulsion are the same as will and aversion. In your own life, do you ever find yourself 'willing' something you don't actually want, or 'wanting' something you don't will? How does this internal conflict affect your sincerity in worship?
?
You have been given power (qudra) to act—yet your actions are often driven by appetite or aversion. Are you using your power to obey Allah, or are you letting your appetites command you? What will you change today to ensure your power serves your will for righteousness?

Creation as Predestination: The 'Kun Fayakun' Argument
Analysis of 'Kun Fayakun' to argue that 'khalq' means predestination, not bringing essences into existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'Kun Fayakun' is used to argue that 'khalq' (creation) means predestination (taqdīr) rather than bringing essences into existence from nothing.

Core Idea
Khalq means predestination, not ex nihilo creation.

This passage analyzes the Qur'anic expression 'Kun Fayakun' (Be, and it is) to argue that the term 'khalq' (creation) does not necessarily mean bringing essences into existence from nothing. The author presents a nuanced interpretation: 'khalq' actually refers to God's predestination and planning, not the origination of substances. Let's break down this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the key terms like 'khalq' and 'taqdīr'.
2
Identify the main claim about 'khalq'
Find the sentence that defines 'khalq' as 'taqdīr'. This is the central thesis.
The passage states: 'فثبت: أن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير'.
3
Trace the logical progression
The author starts from the apparent meaning of 'Kun Fayakun', then shows that creation must precede coming into being, and identifies that prior creation as God's knowledge.
Follow the sequence: 'كن فيكون' → 'يخلقه ثم يكونه' → 'الخلق المتقدم هو علمه'.
4
Consider the hypothetical of pre-existing atoms
The author supposes atoms existed eternally and God only arranged them. Even then, God is still called 'creator' because He determined the arrangement.
This hypothetical strengthens the argument that 'khalq' is about predestination.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence explicitly concludes that 'khalq' does not imply God is the originator of essences or entities.
This challenges the common understanding of creation as bringing something from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كن فيكون. ولا شك أن المراد بقوله: كن فيكون: التكوين والإيجاد والإحداث. فظاهر الآية يقتضي أنه يخلقه، ثم بعد الخلق يكونه، والخلق الذي يتقدم على التكوين، ليس إلا علمه تعالى، بأنه يجب تكوينه على الصفة الفلانية، وعلى الصورة الفلانية، حتى يكون أقرب إلى الصواب والصلاح. فثبت: أن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن بتقدير أن تكون الأجزاء موجودة في الأزل، ثم إنه تعالى ركبها على التأليف أصوب، ونظمها على التركيب الأصح، فحينئذ يكون هذا العالم واقعاً بتقديره وتركيبه، فيكون خالقاً لها. فيثبت: أن لفظ الخلق لا يفيد كونه تعالى محدثاً لذواتها وموجداً لأعيانها.
Be, and it is. There is no doubt that what is meant by His saying 'Be, and it is' is: bringing into being, creating, and originating. The apparent meaning of the verse implies that He creates it, then after creation He makes it be. The creation that precedes bringing into being is nothing but His knowledge, exalted, that it must be brought into being in such and such a manner and in such and such a form, so that it is most conducive to correctness and rectitude. Thus it is established that 'creation' (khalq) is [a term for] predestination (taqdīr). And when this is established, we say: On the supposition that the atoms existed in pre-eternity, and then the Exalted composed them in the most correct arrangement and organized them in the most sound structure, then this world occurs by His predestination and composition, so He is its creator. Thus it is established that the word 'creation' does not imply that He, exalted, is the originator of their essences or the bringer into existence of their entities.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage analyzes the Qur'anic expression 'Kun Fayakun' (Be, and it is) to argue that the term 'khalq' (creation) does not necessarily mean bringing essences into existence from nothing. The author begins by stating the apparent meaning: 'Kun Fayakun' signifies bringing into being and originating. However, he notes that the verse implies a sequence: He creates (yakhluquhu) and then after creation He makes it be (yukawwinuhu). The creation that precedes bringing into being is identified as God's knowledge of how it must be made in the best way. Therefore, 'khalq' is actually 'taqdīr' (predestination or planning). The author then supposes that the atoms (ajzā') existed eternally, and God merely arranged them in the most correct composition. In that case, He would still be the creator because He caused the world to occur through His predestination and composition. The conclusion is that the word 'khalq' does not imply that God is the originator (muhdith) of the essences or the bringer into existence (mūjid) of the entities. This argument aims to show that the Qur'anic language does not necessarily support the view that God creates the very substances of things ex nihilo, but could be understood as God organizing pre-existing matter.

Key Terms
كن فيكون
التكوين
الإيجاد
الإحداث
الخلق
التقدير
الأجزاء
ذوات
أعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'khalq' (creation) always means bringing something into existence from nothing (ex nihilo).
Correction: This passage shows that 'khalq' can mean predestination and arrangement, as evidenced by the 'Kun Fayakun' verse and the hypothetical of pre-existing atoms.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the word 'khalq' (creation) primarily signify when applied to God?
A) Bringing essences into existence from nothing
B) Predestination and planning (taqdīr)
C) Making something out of nothing
D) Giving form to pre-existing matter

Key Takeaway
The term 'khalq' in the Qur'an, as used in 'Kun Fayakun', refers to God's predestination and arrangement rather than bringing essences from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God created everything,' do you truly believe that He predestined every detail—including your failures and hardships—or do you secretly think you create your own outcomes? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If 'khalq' means God's perfect planning and arrangement, how should that change your response when life feels chaotic or unfair? Are you willing to submit to His predestination even when you don't understand?
?
The passage argues that God's 'creation' is His knowledge and predestination. Does your daily life reflect trust in that knowledge, or do you act as if you need to arrange things yourself? What step can you take today to live out this truth?

The Argument from 'Fātir': Splitting as Shaping, Not Originating
Linguistic analysis of 'Fātir' showing it implies splitting and shaping pre-existing matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'Fātir' in the Qur'an indicates splitting and shaping pre-existing matter, not creating essences from nothing.

Core Idea
'Fātir' means splitting, not creating from nothing — it shapes pre-existing matter.

The divine name 'Fātir' is often translated as 'Originator', but its linguistic root reveals a different nuance. This passage examines the literal meaning of 'fāṭara' as splitting, and shows how it supports the idea that God's act of creation involves shaping pre-existing atoms rather than bringing essences into being ex nihilo. By analyzing a Quranic verse, a Bedouin idiom, and an anecdote from Ibn Abbas, the author demonstrates that 'Fātir' does not imply creating the essences themselves.

Guided Path
1
Examine the literal meaning of 'Fātir'
The passage begins by defining 'fāṭara' as 'splitting' in the Arabic language, citing Quranic and idiomatic evidence. Read the definition and supporting examples carefully.
Note the derived word 'futūr' (cracks) in the Quranic verse.
2
Consider the hypothetical scenario
The author presents a scenario: if atoms were pre-eternal and dark, and God created light and assembled those atoms into the world, then 'Fātir' applies to the act of shaping, not originating the atoms.
This scenario is a thought experiment to illustrate the point.
3
Reflect on the anecdote from Ibn Abbas
Ibn Abbas admits he didn't know the meaning of 'Fātir' until two Bedouins used 'fāṭara' to mean initiating a well. This shows the word's usage in Arabic for bringing about a new form without creating the matter.
The anecdote reinforces that 'fāṭara' can mean to initiate or create in a particular way.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that 'Fātir' does not imply God creates essences; it only indicates He gives shape and form to pre-existing atoms. This supports the theological view that God works on pre-existing matter.
This conclusion is central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ الثالث: لفظ الفاطر. قال تعالى: (الحمد الله فاطر السموات والأرض) فالفطر في أصل اللغة: هو الشق، قال تعالى: ﴿فارجع البصر، هل ترى من فطور﴾؟ أي: هل ترى فيه انشقاقاً؟ ويقال: فطر ناب البعير، أي انشق الموضع، عن ذلك الناب. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: بتقدير أن تكون الأجزاء، كانت أزلية، وكانت الصفات، كانت لا محالة ظلمانية، لأنه لا معنى للظلمة إلا عدم عارية عن النور، عما من شأنه أن يقبل الضوء. فإذا خلق الله الضوء، وأخرج طائفة من تلك الأجزاء، وركب منها هذا العالم، كان هذا المعنى في اطلاق لفظ الفاطر عليه. قال ابن عباس: «ما كنت أعرف أن الفاطر، ما هو؟ حتى اختصم أعرابيان في بئر، فقال أحدهما: أنا فطرتها، أي: أنا أحدثتها.» إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إنه كان فاطراً لتلك البئر، لا لأجل أنه أحدث تلك الأجزاء، بل لأنه أحدث ذلك الشكل والهيئة في تلك الأجزاء. فهذا يدل على أن لفظ الفاطر، لا يدل على كونه تعالى محدثاً للذوات.
And the third word: the word 'al-Fātir'. He, exalted, said: 'Praise be to Allah, the Originator (Fātir) of the heavens and the earth.' 'Fitr' in the origin of the language is: splitting. He, exalted, said: 'Then return the sight; do you see any fissures (futūr)?' That is: do you see any split in it? And it is said: 'A young camel's tusk split (faṭara),' meaning the place split from that tusk. If you understand this, we say: On the supposition that the atoms were pre-eternal, and they were attributes, they were necessarily dark, because there is no meaning for darkness except the absence of light, devoid of that which is capable of receiving light. Then when God created light and brought out a group of those atoms and composed from them this world, this meaning applies to the word 'Fātir' to Him. Ibn Abbas said: 'I did not know what 'al-Fātir' was until two Bedouins disputed over a well, and one of them said: 'I fataṙtuhā (I originated it),' meaning I initiated it.' If you understand this, we say: He was Fātir of that well, not because He originated those atoms, but because He originated that shape and form in those atoms. So this indicates that the word 'Fātir' does not imply that He, exalted, is the originator of essences.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage analyzes the divine name 'Fātir' (often translated as Originator or Creator) from the verse 'Praise be to Allah, Fātir of the heavens and the earth.' The author first gives the literal meaning: 'fāṭir' in Arabic means splitting or cleaving, as supported by a Qur'anic usage about 'fisūr' (cracks) and a Bedouin idiom about a well. He then constructs a hypothetical scenario: if atoms (parts) were pre-eternal and dark (since darkness is merely absence of light), and God created light and extracted some of those atoms and assembled them into this world, then the word 'Fātir' can be applied to God, but only in the sense of bringing about the form and structure, not the atoms themselves. The anecdote of Ibn Abbas and the Bedouins reinforces that 'fāṭara' can mean to initiate something new without necessarily creating its constituent matter. Thus, the conclusion is that the term 'Fātir' does not necessitate that God is the originator (muḥdith) of the essences themselves. This argument supports the view that God works upon pre-existing matter rather than creating ex nihilo.

Key Terms
الفاطر
الفطر
شق
الظلمة
النور
الأجزاء
الذوات
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that 'Fātir' (often translated as 'Originator') means creating from nothing, ex nihilo.
Correction: The passage shows that 'Fātir' literally means 'splitter' and refers to shaping pre-existing matter, not originating essences.

Checkpoint
What is the core linguistic meaning of 'fāṭir' as given in the passage?
A) Creating from nothing
B) Splitting or cleaving
C) Bringing forth
D) Giving life

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute 'Fātir' indicates that God shapes and forms pre-existing matter, not that He creates essences from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'al-Fātir' in your prayers, do you feel that God is actively shaping your present circumstances, or is it a distant title? What specific situation today can you submit to His 'fatr' - His shaping hand - instead of trying to control it yourself?
?
Ibn Abbas learned the true meaning from a concrete dispute between Bedouins. Are you satisfied with abstract theological labels, or do you seek to understand God's names through real-life encounters? How can you let an experience this week deepen your understanding of al-Fātir, and what would that require of you?

The Argument from 'al-Ghanī': Need for Attributes
Showing that God's self-sufficiency and man's need refer to attributes, not to essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'anic expression 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor' indicates the world's need for specific attributes and descriptions, not for the essences themselves.

Core Idea
Poverty means need for attributes, not for existence.

This lesson examines the fourth Qur'anic expression used in theological arguments about creation. The verse 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor' might seem to imply that creatures are inherently needy for their very existence, but the author clarifies that the need is for specific attributes and descriptions. We will explore the two reasons the author provides to support this interpretation, including a philosophical principle about the nature of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice how the author refutes a potential interpretation of the verse.
2
Identify the author's main claim
What is the author trying to disprove? Find the phrase 'لا يدل على هذا المطلوب'.
The author is arguing against the idea that the verse proves God creates essences.
3
Examine the two supporting reasons
The author gives two reasons: (1) the world needs the Creator for specific attributes, and (2) essences are possible in themselves and necessary by cause. Understand each one.
Note the transition word 'وأيضاً' which introduces the second reason.
4
Consider the philosophical context
The phrase 'الذوات عند القوم ممكنة لذاتها واجبة بسبب وجوب علتها' refers to a key principle in Islamic philosophy. Reflect on how this view of essences relates to the concept of need.
There is a variant reading with 'وجود' instead of 'وجوب', but the main text uses 'وجوب'.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Summarize why the verse does not support the claim that God creates essences. How does the author's analysis protect divine transcendence?
The verse highlights need for attributes, preserving God's role as the bestower of qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ الرابع: قوله تعالى: والله الغني وأنتم الفقراء. وهذا أيضاً لا يدل على هذا المطلوب، لأن العالم يحتاج إلى البارىء، في حصول الصفات المخصوصة، والنعوت المخصوصة. وهذا القدر يكفي في حصول الحاجة. وأيضاً: فالذوات عند القوم ممكنة لذواتها، واجبة بسبب وجوب علتها، وهذا يكفي في حصول معنى الحاجة.
And the fourth word: His saying, exalted: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor.' This also does not indicate the desired conclusion, because the world needs the Creator for the attainment of specific attributes and specific descriptions. And this amount suffices for the occurrence of need. Also: the essences according to the people are possible in themselves, necessary due to the necessity of their cause, and this suffices for the meaning of need.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage examines the verse 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor' to argue that it does not prove God's creation of essences. The author explains that the world's need for God is for specific attributes and descriptions, not for their very existence. Additionally, according to the author's opponents (the 'people,' likely philosophers or theologians who hold that the world is possible in itself), the essences are possible in themselves and become necessary due to the necessity (or existence) of their cause. This understanding of need (need for a cause to confer necessity) is sufficient to explain the poverty or need mentioned in the verse, without implying that God creates the essences from nothing. Thus, this Qur'anic expression does not support the claim that God originates the substances of things.

Key Terms
الغني
الفقراء
الحاجة
الصفات
النعوت
الذوات
الممكن
الواجب
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'poverty' in the verse implies that creatures need God for their very existence (essence).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the need is for specific attributes and descriptions, not for the essence itself. The need is for the perfection of qualities, not for the existence of the substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the world need from the Creator?
A) Its very existence
B) Specific attributes and descriptions
C) Its essence
D) Its material substance

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor' shows that creation's need is for specific attributes, not for their essences; the essences themselves are possible in themselves and become necessary through the Creator's necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every good attribute you possess is a gift from Allah? What specific step can you take today to acknowledge your poverty before Him?
?
If your very essence is possible in itself and only becomes necessary through Allah, how does that reshape your understanding of your own independence and accomplishments? Are you holding onto any pride that belongs only to Him?
?
The verse says 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient and you are the poor.' Have you truly felt the weight of that poverty, or do you live as though you own your attributes? How would your daily worship change if you constantly remembered that your need is for every single perfect description?

The Argument from 'al-Awwal': Being First Does Not Require Temporal Priority
Author argues that 'al-Awwal' does not imply temporal priority to everything.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attribute 'al-Awwal' (the First) can be interpreted as causal priority rather than temporal priority, and that this interpretation refutes a specific theological claim about creation.

Core Idea
Being 'First' means causal priority, not necessarily being first in time for everything.

In this passage, the author tackles a common theological argument that uses the Qur'anic name 'al-Awwal' (the First) to claim that nothing else existed with God in pre-eternity. The author shows that this conclusion does not necessarily follow, because the word 'first' can be satisfied by being first in one aspect, such as causality, without requiring temporal priority over all things. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'because' (لأن) and 'also' (وأيضاً).
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice that the opponents argue that 'al-Awwal' means 'the singular precursor', implying nothing else existed with God in pre-eternity. The author rejects this conclusion.
This is a common interpretation that the author aims to refute.
3
Understand the first counter-argument
The author states that being 'First' does not require being first of everything; an unqualified term can be satisfied by a single instance. Find this key sentence.
The Arabic phrase 'ليس من شرط كونه أولاً، أن يكون أولاً لكل شيء' is central.
4
Examine the concessive argument
Even if 'first' meant first of everything, the author reports that opponents say the world is possible in itself and necessary by its cause, with causal and essential priority. This interpretation fits 'first' as causal priority.
This shows the argument works even under the stronger assumption.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this matters: it prevents using 'al-Awwal' to prove that God created essences from nothing before time. The passage shows flexibility in scriptural interpretation.
This is part of a larger debate about creation and the relationship between God and the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ الخامس: قوله تعالى: هو الأول. قالوا: والأول هو الفرد السابق. وهذا يدل على أنه لم يوجد في الأزل مع الله غيره. وهذا أيضاً لا يفيد هذا المقصود، لأنه ليس من شرط كونه أولاً، أن يكون أولاً لكل شيء، لأن اللفظ المهمل في جانب الثبوت، يكفي في حصوله حصول مسماه في فرد واحد. وأيضاً: فبتقدير أنه يجب أن يكون أولاً لكل ما سواه، لكن القوم يقولون: العالم ممكن لذاته، واجب بوجوب علته، والعلة سابقة على المعلول بالعلية وبالذات، فيكون أولاً لكل ما سواه بهذا التفسير.
And the fifth word: His saying, exalted: 'He is the First.' They said: 'The First is the singular precursor.' This indicates that nothing else existed with God in pre-eternity. This also does not serve the intended purpose, because it is not a condition of His being 'First' that He be first of everything, since an unqualified word in the affirmative side suffices for its occurrence by the realization of its referent in a single instance. Also: on the supposition that He must be first of everything other than Himself, the people (the opponents) say: 'The world is possible in itself, necessary by the necessity (or existence) of its cause, and the cause is prior to the effect in causality and in essence; thus He is first of everything other than Himself in this interpretation.'
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses the divine name 'al-Awwal' (the First). The author first notes that some claim this shows nothing existed with God in pre-eternity. He then argues that it does not prove the desired conclusion (that God creates essences). The reason is that the word 'first' when unqualified can be satisfied by being first in one particular aspect, not necessarily first in everything. Even if we assume it must mean first in everything, the author reports the view of his opponents: the world is possible in itself and becomes necessary by its cause's necessity (or existence). The cause is prior to the effect in causality (bi-al-‘illiyya) and in essence (bi-al-dhāt). So God is 'first' with respect to everything else in the sense of causal priority, not necessarily temporal priority. Thus, this attribute does not force the conclusion that God created essences from nothing.

الفرد
السابق
الأزل
المعلول
العلة
الذات
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'al-Awwal' (the First) necessarily means that God is temporally prior to all things, implying nothing else existed before creation.
Correction: The passage shows that 'first' can be understood as causal priority (the cause is prior to the effect in causality and essence), not temporal priority, so the attribute does not force the conclusion that nothing existed with God in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given that 'al-Awwal' does not prove God creates all essences ex nihilo?
A) The word 'First' is ambiguous
B) It is not a condition of being first that He is first of everything
C) The world is eternal
D) God is only first in name

Key Takeaway
The attribute 'al-Awwal' does not force temporal priority over all things; it can be interpreted as causal priority, leaving room for the possibility that the world is caused without being temporally preceded by nothingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the world is always in need of its Cause every moment, does that make you more conscious of Allah's continuous creation? What would change if you started each day acknowledging that you are literally supported by His causal priority?

The Argument from 'Kun Fayakun': Power and Will, Not Origination
Reinterpretation of 'Kun Fayakun' as effectiveness of power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Kun Fayakun' does not prove that God creates essences from nothing, but rather indicates the effectiveness of His power and will.

Core Idea
'Kun' does not create matter; it activates power and will.

In this passage, the author addresses the sixth verbal argument from the famous verse 'Kun Fayakun'. While many assume this verse shows God's direct creation of essences, the author clarifies that 'Kun' means the effectiveness of divine power and will. This distinction is crucial for understanding the limits of scriptural evidence on the nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the sixth word (اللفظ السادس) carefully. Notice the key terms: كن, القدرة, الإرادة, النفاذ, التكوين.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the reinterpretation
The author states that 'كن' (Be) means the effectiveness of power and will, not a creative command that brings essences from nothing. This is a crucial shift in interpretation.
This interpretation leaves open the possibility of pre-existing matter.
3
Recognize the argument's failure
The author concludes that the verse still does not prove the desired conclusion (that God creates essences). It only describes how things occur when God wills them.
The verse is about the mode of action, not the origin of substances.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Take the multiple-choice quiz to confirm your understanding of what 'Kun' means in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ السادس: قوله تعالى: (إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه: أن نقول له: كن. فيكون). والمراد بقوله: كن: نفاذ القدرة والإرادة، فدل على أنه تعالى كون الأشياء بقدرته، وهذا أيضاً لا يدل على هذا المطلوب، لأن هذه الآية تدل على أن كل ما أراد الله تكوينه، فإنه يكون بهذا الطريق.
And the sixth word: His saying, exalted: 'Our word to a thing when We intend it is only that We say to it: Be, and it is.' And the intended meaning of His saying 'Be' is: the effectiveness of power and will. So it indicates that He, exalted, brings things into being by His power. But this also does not indicate the desired conclusion, because this verse indicates that whatever God wills to bring into being occurs by this way.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents yet another linguistic argument from the same verse 'Kun Fayakun', but this time focusing on the interpretation of 'Be' (كن). The author states that 'Be' here means the effectiveness (nufūdh) of God's power (qudra) and will (irāda). Thus the verse means that when God intends something, it comes to be through His power and will. The author reiterates that this does not prove the desired conclusion (that God creates essences from nothing), because the verse only describes the manner in which things occur: whatever God wills to bring into being happens in this way. It does not specify whether the essences themselves are brought into existence or merely composed from pre-existing material. So this argument, like the previous ones, fails to establish that God is the originator of the essences.

Correction: The passage explains that 'Kun' means the effectiveness of power and will, not a creative command that brings about the very substance. This interpretation allows for the possibility that the thing already existed in some form.

C) A literal speech act

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Kun Fayakun' describes God's action through power and will, but does not prove that He creates essences from nothing.

Objection and Refutation on the Possibility of the World's Eternity
Author presents an objection and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if possibility has no beginning then the world could be eternal, and the author's refutation based on the condition of being preceded by non-existence.

Core Idea
Possibility without beginning does not imply ability to be eternal if conditioned by non-existence.

This passage presents a challenging objection against those who deny the world's eternity. The opponent argues that if the possibility of the world's existence and God's power have no beginning, then there is no obstacle to eternal creation. The author admits the difficulty and provides a subtle refutation from his work Al-Arba'in, showing a logical flaw in the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'As for those who affirm a beginning...' to '...combining them is impossible.' Understand the opponent's premises: no beginning for possibility, no beginning for God's power to affect, therefore no impossibility in eternal creation, and if that is so, saying the world cannot be eternal would be contradictory.
Notice the opponent's reasoning is based on the idea that possibility and power are eternal.
2
Identify the refutation's key move
Read the refutation from 'Know that this speech is very problematic...' to the end. The author shifts the focus to the condition 'preceded by non-existence' and argues that this condition itself prevents the thing from being eternal, even if its possibility has no beginning.
The condition is crucial: a thing that comes into existence after non-existence cannot be eternal by definition.
3
Understand the logical gap
The opponent infers from 'no beginning for the validity of existence' to 'it is valid to be eternal'. The author denies this inference because the validity is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence, which is incompatible with eternity.
The inference is invalid because the condition changes the meaning of 'no beginning'.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This passage illustrates the precision required in kalam arguments. A small difference in conditions can block apparently valid inferences. It also shows how Muslim theologians handled difficult objections by carefully analyzing underlying conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بإثبات الأول لهذا الإمكان. فقد إحتجوا عليه، وقالوا : إنه [إذا كان] لا أول لإمكان وجود العالم في نفسه، ولا أول أيضاً لإمكان تأثير قدرة الله تعالى فيه، فعلى هذا التقدير، لا امتناع في تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم من الأزل إلى الأبد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لزم [القطع] بأنـه امتناع في كون العالم موجوداً في الأزل، ولا امتناع في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم في الأزل . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان القول بأن العالم يمتنع أن يكون قديماً ذلك القول، يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال . وأيضاً : فالفعل ما له أول، والأزل ما لا أول له، والجمع بينهما محال . واعلم أن هذا الكلام مشكل جداً، وقد أجبنا عنه في كتاب « الأربعين » فقلنا : إنا إذا وجدنا الشيء المعين، بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم، فإنه مع هذا الشرط لا أول لصحة وجوده، ثم لا يلزم من قولنا : لا أول لصحة وجوده مع هذا الشرط، صدق قولنا : إنه أن يكون أزلياً، لأنه مع شرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، يمتنع أن يكون أزلياً فيثبت أنه لا يلزم من قولنا : إنه لا أول لصحة وجوده، قولنا : إنه يصح أن يكون أزلياً .
As for those who affirm a beginning for this possibility, they have argued against it, saying: If there is no beginning for the possibility of the world's existence in itself, and no beginning for the possibility of the effect of God's power in it, then on this estimation there is no impossibility in the effect of God's power on the existence of the world from eternity to eternity. And if the matter is so, it necessarily follows that there is an impossibility in the world's being existent in eternity, and no impossibility in His being the one who brings the world into existence in eternity. And if the matter is so, then the statement that the world cannot be eternal—that statement necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. And also: the act has a beginning, and eternity has no beginning, and combining them is impossible. Know that this speech is very problematic, and we have answered it in the book 'Al-Arba'in,' saying: Indeed, when we consider a specific thing, under the condition of its being preceded by non-existence, then with this condition there is no beginning for the validity of its existence. Then, it does not follow from our statement 'there is no beginning for the validity of its existence with this condition' that our statement 'it can be eternal' is true, because with the condition of its being preceded by non-existence, it is impossible for it to be eternal. So it is established that it does not follow from our statement 'there is no beginning for the validity of its existence' our statement 'it is valid for it to be eternal.'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from opponents who affirm a beginning for possibility (i.e., they think possibility had a starting point), and then the author's refutation. The objection: If there is no beginning for the possibility of the world's existence (i.e., it was always possible) and no beginning for the possibility of God's power to affect it, then there is no obstacle to God's power creating the world from eternity. If that is so, then it would be contradictory to say the world cannot be eternal, because that would require that the possibility of its existence is not eternal (contradicting the premise) and also that God's power is not eternal. Additionally, they argue that an 'act' has a beginning while 'eternity' has no beginning, so combining them (an eternal act) is impossible. The author admits this objection is difficult. His response from his book Al-Arba'in: When we consider a specific thing under the condition that it is preceded by non-existence (i.e., it comes into being after not existing), then under that condition there is no beginning for the validity of its existence (i.e., it could have existed at any time). However, from the fact that there is no beginning for the validity of its existence under that condition, it does not follow that it can actually be eternal (i.e., exist without beginning). Why? Because the condition of being preceded by non-existence itself prevents it from being eternal; an eternal thing cannot be preceded by non-existence. So the inference from 'no beginning for the validity of existence' to 'it is valid to be eternal' is invalid. This refutes the objection by showing a logical gap.

Key Terms
إمكان (possibility)
قدرة الله (divine power)
أزل (eternity)
جمع النقيضين (contradiction)
مسبوق بالعدم (preceded by non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is always possible (no beginning for its possibility), then it must be capable of being eternal.
Correction: The author shows that this inference fails when the thing is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence, because that condition prevents it from being eternal, regardless of the possibility's beginninglessness.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why does the statement 'there is no beginning for the validity of existence' not imply that something can be eternal?
A) Because possibility itself has a beginning.
B) Because the thing is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence, which is incompatible with eternity.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because eternity is not a valid concept.

Key Takeaway
The condition of being preceded by non-existence prevents a thing from being eternal, even if the possibility of its existence has no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, you were preceded by non-existence. Does that condition humble you? How does it shape your understanding of your own permanence?
?
You might have thought that just because something is always possible, it must eventually happen. In what area of your life have you assumed this, and how does this lesson correct that assumption?
?
The condition of being preceded by non-existence makes eternity impossible. How does this truth affect your relationship with the Eternal One? What does it mean for you to be a created being dependent on Him for every moment?

The Paradox of Eternal Possibility
Author reflects on the paradox of eternal possibility and temporal actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the paradox that arises when the possibility of the world's existence is considered eternal while its actuality is not.

Core Idea
The possibility of existence seems both eternal and non-eternal—a true paradox.

The author has just presented an objection against the view that the world is created. Now he reflects on the objection itself, acknowledging its strength and the profound difficulty it poses. He reveals a paradox at the heart of the issue: if possibility is eternal, but the actual world is not, then we seem to be asserting contradictory statements. This lesson explores that intellectual struggle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses 'Wa i'lam' (And know) to draw attention.
2
Identify the two contradictory statements
Find where the author says: (1) possibility is eternal, and (2) possibility is not eternal. Understand why he sees them as opposites.
The contradiction is between 'there is no beginning for possibility' and 'the world cannot be eternal'.
3
Understand the difficulty
The author says the objection is 'very strong and real' because the issue itself is problematic. Grasp why reconciling the two statements is difficult.
The difficulty is that both statements seem necessary yet incompatible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with 'And God knows best.' Consider what this implies about human reasoning on this matter.
This is not a defeat but an admission of intellectual humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه المعارضة قوية واقعة جداً، لأن المسألة في نفسها : مشكلة . فإنا لما سلمنا أنه لا أول لإمكان وجودها، كان معناه : أن إمكان وجودها حاصل في الأزل، فإذا قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونه أزلياً، كان معناه : أن إمكان وجوده غير حاصل في الأزل، فهذا يقتضي الجمع بين الضدين . وهب أن هذه المسألة، صعبت على الكل في الصورة المذكورة، لكن كيف اجتمع فيها صدق القولين مع كونها متناقضين ؟ ولا شك أنه في غاية الصعوبة [ والله أعلم ]
And know that this objection is very strong and real, because the issue itself is problematic. For when we conceded that there is no beginning for the possibility of its existence, its meaning is that the possibility of its existence occurs in eternity. Then if we say that it is impossible for it to be eternal, its meaning is that the possibility of its existence does not occur in eternity. Thus this necessitates combining two opposites. Granted that this issue is difficult for everyone in the mentioned form, but how can two statements be simultaneously true while they are contradictory? And there is no doubt that it is extremely difficult, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a reflection on the objection just discussed. The author acknowledges that the objection is very strong and real because the problem itself is inherently difficult. He formulates the apparent paradox: if we admit that there is no beginning for the possibility of the world's existence (meaning that possibility is eternal), but then we say that the world cannot be eternal (meaning that its possibility is not eternal, since an eternal thing cannot be preceded by non-existence), then we seem to be saying two contradictory things: possibility is eternal and possibility is not eternal. This would be a combination of opposites. The author wonders how two seemingly contradictory statements can both be true in this case. He admits the extreme difficulty of the issue and concludes with 'God knows best,' indicating that the resolution may be beyond human intellect. This passage shows the author's intellectual honesty and the complexity of the theological problem of the eternity of the world.

Key Terms
معارضة (objection)
إمكان الوجود (possibility of existence)
أزل (eternity)
ضدان (opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is giving up or admitting defeat on the issue.
Correction: The author is humbly acknowledging the profound difficulty and relying on divine knowledge, not abandoning the argument.

Checkpoint

A) The definition of possibility.
B) The apparent contradiction between the possibility of existence being eternal and the impossibility of the world being eternal.
C) The limitation of God's power.
D) The inability to prove the existence of the world.

Key Takeaway
The paradox of eternal possibility and temporal actuality is a profound difficulty that even great scholars acknowledge, often deferring to divine knowledge.

Objection and Two Answers on God's Will and Creation Time
Objection and two responses about God's will specifying the time of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam theologians defend God's eternal will specifying the time of creation against an objection, and the two answers based on the nature of the divine will.

Core Idea
God's will specifies creation time by its very essence, blocking infinite regress of 'why'.

After presenting a proof for the eternity of the world, the author now addresses an objection: if all necessary conditions for God's agency are eternal, why is the world not eternal? The objector argues that one condition is God's will to create at a specific time, which delays the effect. This lesson presents two answers defending the view that the will's specification is intrinsic and not subject to further questioning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read how the objector argues that God's eternal will is a condition that requires creation at a specific future time, not immediately. This challenges the proof that the world must be eternal if all conditions are eternal.
The objector introduces God's will (irada) as an eternal attribute that specifies a future time for creation.
2
Explore the first answer
The first answer states that the divine will by its particular essence (haqiqatiha al-makhsusa) necessitates the specification; no further 'why' is possible because it's a concomitant of the essence. This blocks the infinite regress of 'why'.
This answer relies on the principle that essences have necessary properties (lawazim al-mahiya) that cannot be explained further.
3
Examine the second answer (incomplete)
The second answer begins by suggesting that the will attaches to that time for a reason (e.g., wisdom). The text cuts off, but it implies a teleological explanation.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the full second answer is not available in this chunk.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these answers avoid infinite regress and uphold God's freedom. The first answer makes the specification intrinsic to the will, while the second appeals to divine wisdom.
These are typical kalam strategies to reconcile God's eternal will with temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الكلام على هذه الحجة من وجهين : تارة يذكر بحسب الجواب ، وتارة بحسب إيراد المعارضات . أما المقام الأول . وهو ذكر الجواب : فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً وموجداً ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل كان من جملة تلك الأمور : كونه تعالى مريداً لإحداث العالم ، وأنه تعالى كان يريد إحداث العالم في وقت معين ، فيما لا يزال . فلهذا السبب اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت . فإن قلتم فلم اقتضت إرادة الله تعالى إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ فنقول الجواب عنه من وجوه : الوجه الأول في الجواب : أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن إرادة الله تعالى أوجبت بحقيقتها المخصوصة تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، وتلك الإرادة ليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط قول القائل : لم تعلقت تلك الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ لأن تلك الإرادة لما كانت بحقيقتها المخصوصة ، يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وامتنع تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ، وكان ذلك الوجوب المخصوص ، والامتناع المخصوص ، من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة ، وامتنع قول القائل : لم وقعت تلك الإرادة على ذلك الوجه المعين ، دون سائر الوجوه ؟ الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، لأن إحداث العالم في ذلك
For it is said: The discussion of this proof is from two aspects: sometimes according to the answer, and sometimes according to the presentation of objections. As for the first station – that is, mentioning the answer – we say: Why is it not permissible to say that everything necessary for Him – exalted is He – to be an agent and creator, what was obtained in eternity was from the totality of those things: His – exalted is He – being willing to create the world, and that He – exalted is He – was willing to create the world at a specific time, in the future. For this reason the temporality of the world was specified to that time. For if you say: Why then did the will of God – exalted is He – necessitate the creation of the world at that specific time rather than before it or after it? We say the answer to it from aspects: The first aspect of the answer: that we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God – exalted is He – necessitated, by its particular essence, the specification of the creation of the world to that specific time, and that will does not have the capacity to necessitate the creation of the world at another time. On this supposition, the statement of the one who says: Why did that will attach to the creation of the world at that specific time and not attach to its creation at other times? collapses. Because that will, since it is by its particular essence, its attachment to the creation of the world at that specific time is necessary, and its attachment to the creation of the world at other than that time is impossible, and that particular necessity and particular impossibility are from the concomitants of the essence of that will, and the statement of the one who says: Why did that will occur upon that particular manner, rather than other manners? is impossible. The second aspect of the answer: it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: The will of God – exalted is He – attached to the creation of the world at that specific time because the creation of the world at that ...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof and two responses. The objector argues that the necessary conditions for God's agency include His eternal will (irada) to create the world at a specific future time. This will is an eternal attribute that determines the time of creation, so the effect is not immediate. The proof had argued that if all conditions are eternal, the effect must be eternal. The objector says: But one of those conditions is the will to create at a specific time, not immediately. The two answers are given: First answer: The divine will by its very particular essence (haqiqatiha al-makhsusa) necessitates the specification of the time; one cannot ask 'why' because it is a primitive fact about that will. This answer blocks the infinite regress by making the will's specification a necessary concomitant of its essence. Second answer (incomplete in the text): The will is attached to that time because the creation at that time is better or more appropriate (probably due to wisdom). The text cuts off. These answers are typical of kalam debates on how God's eternal will relates to temporal events.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
حقيقة مخصوصة (haqiqa makhsusa)
تخصيص (takhsis)
لم؟ (lima)
لوازم الماهية (lawazim al-mahiya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first answer is a 'brute fact' cop-out that avoids explanation.
Correction: It relies on the well-established principle that essences have necessary properties (lawazim al-mahiya) that cannot be further reduced; the specification is intrinsic to the will's nature.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what is the reason for God's will being attached to a specific time?
A) God chose it arbitrarily.
B) The will by its nature necessitates that time.
C) The creation at that time is more beneficial.
D) It is due to an external cause.

Key Takeaway
The specification of God's will to a particular time is a necessary concomitant of its essence, not an arbitrary choice open to further questioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded from Allah a reason for why something happened to you at a particular time? How does the lesson’s teaching that God’s will specifies by its essence challenge your need for an explanation beyond 'He willed it'?
?
Think of a trial you are currently facing. Do you believe that the timing of this trial is arbitrary, or does it flow from the very nature of God’s wisdom and will? How would your heart respond if you truly accepted that no further 'why' is needed?
?
The passage stops the infinite regress of 'why' by grounding it in God’s will. Is there an area in your life where you are stuck asking 'why' without submitting to the reality that some things are simply the way God’s will is? What would it take for you to stop and trust that His will is sufficient?

The Problem of the Specific Time of Creation
Razi presents the objection and its presupposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theological objection to why God created the world at a particular moment and the importance of this question in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The question 'Why this time?' challenges the apparent arbitrariness of the eternal will.

Before presenting his six responses, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi sets up the problem: if God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at one specific moment rather than any other? This question probes the nature of divine will and wisdom. The objection is not merely rhetorical; it touches on fundamental issues of divine attributes, causality, and the relationship between God's knowledge and action. In this lesson, you will read the objection in Razi's own words and understand its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the premise that God's will is eternal.
The objection flows from that premise.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Razi states that God's will was always directed at the specific time. The objector asks why that time.
3
Understand the challenge
Reflect on why this question is seen as a challenge: if the will is simple and eternal, why one time over another?
This sets the stage for the six responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً وموجداً ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل كان من جملة تلك الأمور : كونه تعالى مريداً لإحداث العالم ، وأنه تعالى كان يريد إحداث العالم في وقت معين ، فيما لا يزال . فلهذا السبب اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت . فإن قلتم فلم اقتضت إرادة الله تعالى إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ فنقول الجواب عنه من وجوه : الوجه الأول في الجواب : أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن إرادة الله تعالى أوجبت بحقيقتها المخصوصة تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، وتلك الإرادة ليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط قول القائل : لم تعلقت تلك الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ لأن تلك الإرادة لما كانت بحقيقتها المخصوصة ، يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وامتنع تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ، وكان ذلك الوجوب المخصوص ، والامتناع المخصوص ، من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة ، وامتنع قول القائل : لم وقعت تلك الإرادة على ذلك الوجه المعين، دون سائر الوجوه ؟ الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، لأن إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت أصلح للمكلفين من إحداثه في سائر الأوقات فلأجل هذا المعنى اختص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنا لا نعرف لذلك الوقت المعين خاصية معينة ، إلا أنا نقول : عدم علمنا بذلك لا يقدح في حصوله في ذلك الوقت . الوجه الثالث في الجواب : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات ، وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن العالم يحدث في الوقت الفلاني بعينه ومعلوم الله تعالى واجب الوقوع ، ممتنع التغير . والإرادة لا تعلق لها بالمحالات ، فلا جرم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، ولم تتعلق بإيقاعه في سائر الأوقات . الوجه الرابع في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما لم تتعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في الأزل ، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الذي سبقه العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية ، والجمع بينهما محال ، والمحال غير مقدور ولا مراد . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن حصول الأزل مانع من الحدوث ، فكان الحدوث في الأزل محالاً ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة العالم في الأزل . الوجه الخامس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العالم قبل أن حدث ، كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه قبل الوقت الذي أحدثه فيه . الوجه السادس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن قول القائل لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في هذا الوقت : سؤال باطل ، لأن تلك الإرادة لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، لا في هذا الوقت ، لكان ذلك السؤال المذكور عائداً ، ولما كان ذلك السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات كان باطلا ؟
Everything that is necessary for Him, exalted, to be an agent and creator—whatever was present in pre-eternity—was among those matters: His being, exalted, willing to bring about the world, and that He, exalted, was willing to bring about the world at a specific time, in what never ceases. For this reason the coming-to-be of the world was specified to that time. If you say: Why did the will of God, exalted, necessitate bringing about the world at that specific time rather than before it or after it? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: The first aspect in the answer: That we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, necessitated, by its specific reality, the specification of bringing about the world at that specific time, and that will does not have the capacity to necessitate bringing about the world at another time? And on this assumption, the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will attach to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times?' falls away. Because that will, since it is by its specific reality, its attachment to bringing about the world at that specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about the world at other than that time is impossible, and that specific necessity and that specific impossibility are necessary concomitants of the quiddity of that will, and the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will occur in that specific manner, rather than other manners?' is impossible. The second aspect in the answer: That it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time only because bringing about the world at that time is more beneficial for the morally responsible than bringing it about at other times? And for this reason the bringing about of the world was specified to that specific time? The utmost in the matter is that it is said: We do not know for that specific time a specific property, but we say: Our lack of knowledge of that does not detract from its occurrence at that time. The third aspect in the answer: That He, exalted, is knower of all particulars, and this necessitates that He, exalted, knew in pre-eternity that the world would come to be at exactly such-and-such a time, and what God knows is necessarily occurring, impossible to change. And the will has no attachment to impossible things, so necessarily the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times. The fourth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The will did not attach to bringing about the world in pre-eternity only because coming-to-be (ḥudūth) is an existence preceded by non-existence, and pre-eternity (azal) is the negation of beginningness, and combining them is impossible, and the impossible is neither within power nor will. And when this is established, it is clear that the occurrence of pre-eternity is a hindrance to coming-to-be, so coming-to-be in pre-eternity would be impossible; for this reason the will of the world did not attach in pre-eternity. The fifth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The world, before it came to be, was impossible of existence in itself and by its essence, then it became possible of existence in itself and by its essence; for this reason the will of God, exalted, did not attach to bringing it about before the time in which He brought it about. The sixth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says 'Why did the will of God, exalted, attach to bringing about the world at this time?' is an invalid question, because if that will attached to bringing about the world at another time, not at this time, that same question would recur, and since that question would recur in all cases, it is invalid?
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six responses (wujūh) to a classical theological objection: If God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. First, the passage sets up the premise that God's will is eternal and that He always willed the creation of the world at a specific time. The objector asks: Why that time specifically? The first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) appeals to the specificity of the divine will itself: God's will has a unique 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to that exact time, and it lacks the capacity to attach to another time. Thus, the question 'why this time?' is improper because the will's nature makes it necessarily specific. The second answer invokes divine wisdom: that time was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents (mukallafūn). Even if we do not know the specific benefit, our ignorance does not disprove its existence. The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. Since will cannot attach to what is impossible (the object of knowledge must occur), the will attached necessarily to that time. The fourth answer argues from the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (azal): 'coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' negates beginning—they are contradictory, so creation in pre-eternity is impossible and thus not an object of will. The fifth answer posits a change in the world's essence: before creation, the world was inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi), then it became inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi), so the will could not attach before that change. The sixth answer argues that the question is inherently invalid (bāṭil) because if the will had attached to a different time, the same question would arise, showing that it is not a genuine objection but a universalizable demand that cannot be satisfied.

Key Terms
إرادة
حدوث
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objection presented in this passage?
A) Why did God create the world at all?
B) Why did God create the world at a specific time rather than earlier or later?
C) Why is God's will eternal?
D) Why did God create humans?

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the rationality of the eternal will's specific attachment to one moment, a problem Razi seeks to resolve with six responses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's timing in your life was arbitrary or without reason? Write down one specific instance and consider how the objection in this passage echoes that feeling.
?
The passage asks why God chose this specific time for creation. If you cannot answer that, does it unsettle you? How does that challenge your trust in God's wisdom?
?
When you face a waiting period in life, do you demand a reason from God for the specific timing? What would it mean for you to surrender that demand, as Razi will argue?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Response 1: The Specific Nature of Divine Will
Razi argues that the will's specific reality is its own sufficient reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first response: that the divine will has a unique reality (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to a specific time and cannot attach to another.

Core Idea
The will's specific reality is its own sufficient reason; the question 'why this time?' is impossible.

Razi's first answer is the most philosophically dense: he posits that the divine will has a 'specific reality' (ḥaqīqa makhṣūṣa) that by its very nature necessitates attachment to that particular time and precludes attachment to any other. This means the will is not generic but highly particular. In this lesson, you will see how Razi uses the concept of necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of quiddity (māhīya) to argue that the question 'why this time?' is itself invalid when applied to the will's intrinsic nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the first response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key phrase 'bi-ḥaqīqatihā al-makhṣūṣa'.
2
Identify the key claim
The claim is that the will's specific reality necessitates attachment to that exact time and makes other times impossible.
This is a strong metaphysical claim.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi argues that since the necessity and impossibility are intrinsic to the will's essence, asking 'why' is like asking why a triangle has three sides.
The question is therefore invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six responses (wujūh) to a classical theological objection: If God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. First, the passage sets up the premise that God's will is eternal and that He always willed the creation of the world at a specific time. The objector asks: Why that time specifically? The first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) appeals to the specificity of the divine will itself: God's will has a unique 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to that exact time, and it lacks the capacity to attach to another time. Thus, the question 'why this time?' is improper because the will's nature makes it necessarily specific. The second answer invokes divine wisdom: that time was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents (mukallafūn). Even if we do not know the specific benefit, our ignorance does not disprove its existence. The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. Since will cannot attach to what is impossible (the object of knowledge must occur), the will attached necessarily to that time. The fourth answer argues from the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (azal): 'coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' negates beginning—they are contradictory, so creation in pre-eternity is impossible and thus not an object of will. The fifth answer posits a change in the world's essence: before creation, the world was inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi), then it became inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi), so the will could not attach before that change. The sixth answer argues that the question is inherently invalid (bāṭil) because if the will had attached to a different time, the same question would arise, showing that it is not a genuine objection but a universalizable demand that cannot be satisfied.

Key Terms
إرادة
حقيقة
ماهية
صلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this response makes God's will arbitrary or reduces it to blind necessity.
Correction: Actually, it asserts that the will's nature is intrinsically particular, not that it lacks reason.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, why does the question 'why this time?' fall away?
A) Because God's will is all-powerful.
B) Because the will's specific reality necessitates that particular time.
C) Because the time is known to be the best.
D) Because the will is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first response argues that the divine will's specific reality is its own sufficient reason, making further questioning invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation in your life that you do not understand, do you find yourself demanding from Allah an explanation of 'why now?' or 'why this way?'? How does Razi's argument about the divine will's specific reality being its own sufficient reason challenge you to replace that demand with trust?
?
If Allah's will is inherently specific and not subject to external questioning, what does that mean for how you approach the things in your life that seem arbitrary or painful? Are you willing to accept that His will has a necessary wisdom you cannot grasp, and to submit without requiring a reason?
?
Razi says that asking 'why' about the will's attachment is like asking why a triangle has three sides—a category mistake. Is there an area of your faith where you have been making a similar category mistake, questioning something that is actually intrinsic to Allah's perfection? What would it look like to stop that questioning and instead accept the specific reality of His will?

Response 2: The Time of Creation as Most Beneficial
Razi argues that the specific time was most beneficial for moral agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second response: that God chose the specific time because it was most beneficial for moral agents, even if we do not know the specific benefit.

Core Idea
Ignorance of the benefit does not negate its existence.

The second response appeals to divine wisdom (ḥikma). Razi suggests that the time of creation was chosen because it was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents. This is a teleological explanation: God acts with purpose, and the specific time had a unique advantage. Razi acknowledges that we may not know that advantage, but that does not undermine the explanation. In this lesson, you will see how Razi defends the rationality of God's choice by referring to an unknown but real benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the second response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the term 'aṣlaḥ' (most beneficial).
2
Grasp the concept
Understand that the chosen time is claimed to have a unique benefit for creation's moral development.
This is a common theme in Islamic theology.
3
Address the objection
Razi admits we may not know the specific property, but our ignorance does not disprove the benefit.
This is a defense of divine wisdom.
