كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً وموجداً ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل كان من جملة تلك الأمور : كونه تعالى مريداً لإحداث العالم ، وأنه تعالى كان يريد إحداث العالم في وقت معين ، فيما لا يزال . فلهذا السبب اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت . فإن قلتم فلم اقتضت إرادة الله تعالى إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ فنقول الجواب عنه من وجوه : الوجه الأول في الجواب : أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن إرادة الله تعالى أوجبت بحقيقتها المخصوصة تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، وتلك الإرادة ليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط قول القائل : لم تعلقت تلك الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ لأن تلك الإرادة لما كانت بحقيقتها المخصوصة ، يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وامتنع تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ، وكان ذلك الوجوب المخصوص ، والامتناع المخصوص ، من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة ، وامتنع قول القائل : لم وقعت تلك الإرادة على ذلك الوجه المعين، دون سائر الوجوه ؟ الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، لأن إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت أصلح للمكلفين من إحداثه في سائر الأوقات فلأجل هذا المعنى اختص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنا لا نعرف لذلك الوقت المعين خاصية معينة ، إلا أنا نقول : عدم علمنا بذلك لا يقدح في حصوله في ذلك الوقت . الوجه الثالث في الجواب : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات ، وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن العالم يحدث في الوقت الفلاني بعينه ومعلوم الله تعالى واجب الوقوع ، ممتنع التغير . والإرادة لا تعلق لها بالمحالات ، فلا جرم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، ولم تتعلق بإيقاعه في سائر الأوقات . الوجه الرابع في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما لم تتعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في الأزل ، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الذي سبقه العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية ، والجمع بينهما محال ، والمحال غير مقدور ولا مراد . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن حصول الأزل مانع من الحدوث ، فكان الحدوث في الأزل محالاً ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة العالم في الأزل . الوجه الخامس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العالم قبل أن حدث ، كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه قبل الوقت الذي أحدثه فيه . الوجه السادس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن قول القائل لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في هذا الوقت : سؤال باطل ، لأن تلك الإرادة لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، لا في هذا الوقت ، لكان ذلك السؤال المذكور عائداً ، ولما كان ذلك السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات كان باطلا ؟
Everything that is necessary for Him, exalted, to be an agent and creator—whatever was present in pre-eternity—was among those matters: His being, exalted, willing to bring about the world, and that He, exalted, was willing to bring about the world at a specific time, in what never ceases. For this reason the coming-to-be of the world was specified to that time. If you say: Why did the will of God, exalted, necessitate bringing about the world at that specific time rather than before it or after it? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: The first aspect in the answer: That we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, necessitated, by its specific reality, the specification of bringing about the world at that specific time, and that will does not have the capacity to necessitate bringing about the world at another time? And on this assumption, the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will attach to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times?' falls away. Because that will, since it is by its specific reality, its attachment to bringing about the world at that specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about the world at other than that time is impossible, and that specific necessity and that specific impossibility are necessary concomitants of the quiddity of that will, and the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will occur in that specific manner, rather than other manners?' is impossible. The second aspect in the answer: That it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time only because bringing about the world at that time is more beneficial for the morally responsible than bringing it about at other times? And for this reason the bringing about of the world was specified to that specific time? The utmost in the matter is that it is said: We do not know for that specific time a specific property, but we say: Our lack of knowledge of that does not detract from its occurrence at that time. The third aspect in the answer: That He, exalted, is knower of all particulars, and this necessitates that He, exalted, knew in pre-eternity that the world would come to be at exactly such-and-such a time, and what God knows is necessarily occurring, impossible to change. And the will has no attachment to impossible things, so necessarily the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times. The fourth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The will did not attach to bringing about the world in pre-eternity only because coming-to-be (ḥudūth) is an existence preceded by non-existence, and pre-eternity (azal) is the negation of beginningness, and combining them is impossible, and the impossible is neither within power nor will. And when this is established, it is clear that the occurrence of pre-eternity is a hindrance to coming-to-be, so coming-to-be in pre-eternity would be impossible; for this reason the will of the world did not attach in pre-eternity. The fifth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The world, before it came to be, was impossible of existence in itself and by its essence, then it became possible of existence in itself and by its essence; for this reason the will of God, exalted, did not attach to bringing it about before the time in which He brought it about. The sixth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says 'Why did the will of God, exalted, attach to bringing about the world at this time?' is an invalid question, because if that will attached to bringing about the world at another time, not at this time, that same question would recur, and since that question would recur in all cases, it is invalid?

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six responses (wujūh) to a classical theological objection: If God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Response 3: The Necessity of Divine Foreknowledge
Razi argues that God's eternal knowledge of the specific time determines the will's attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third response: that God's eternal knowledge necessitates the specific time of creation, and the will necessarily follows that knowledge.

Core Idea
What God knows must occur; the will cannot attach to the impossible.

The third response connects the specific time to God's eternal knowledge. Since God knows all particulars, He knew in pre-eternity that the world would occur at that exact time. And what God knows is necessarily occurring and immutable. Therefore, the will could not attach to any other time, because to will otherwise would be to will contrary to knowledge, which is impossible. In this lesson, you will see the tight link between divine knowledge and will in Razi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the chain: God's knowledge → necessity of occurrence → will attaches.
2
Trace the logic
Follow: God knows all particulars → He knew the exact time → His knowledge is immutable → the will cannot attach to what is impossible (the contrary).
This shows the will's attachment is a necessary consequence of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the relationship
Consider how divine knowledge and will are coordinated in this argument.
They are not independent; the will follows knowledge.

The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. First, the passage sets up the premise that God's will is eternal and that He always willed the creation of the world at a specific time. The objector asks: Why that time specifically? The first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) appeals to the specificity of the divine will itself: God's will has a unique 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to that exact time, and it lacks the capacity to attach to another time. Thus, the question 'why this time?' is improper because the will's nature makes it necessarily specific. The second answer invokes divine wisdom: that time was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents (mukallafūn). Even if we do not know the specific benefit, our ignorance does not disprove its existence. The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. Since will cannot attach to what is impossible (the object of knowledge must occur), the will attached necessarily to that time. The fourth answer argues from the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (azal): 'coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' negates beginning—they are contradictory, so creation in pre-eternity is impossible and thus not an object of will. The fifth answer posits a change in the world's essence: before creation, the world was inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi), then it became inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi), so the will could not attach before that change. The sixth answer argues that the question is inherently invalid (bāṭil) because if the will had attached to a different time, the same question would arise, showing that it is not a genuine objection but a universalizable demand that cannot be satisfied.

Key Terms
عالم
معلوم الله
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this makes God determined, but in kalam, God's knowledge is identical with His essence, and the necessity is logical, not external.
Correction: The necessity here is logical, not compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the third response, why did God's will attach to the specific time?
A) Because the time was most beneficial.
B) Because God knew the world would occur at that time.
C) Because the will's nature required it.
D) Because the time was the only possible one.

Key Takeaway
The third response grounds the specific time in God's eternal and immutable foreknowledge, which necessitates the will's attachment to that time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge of every detail of your life is unchangeable and determines what happens, would you worry less about the future? Do you live as if you can change God's decree by your own effort? Consider one area where you fret unnecessarily and ask yourself: does my anxiety reflect a lack of trust in God's perfect knowledge?
?
This passage argues that God's will necessarily attaches to what He knows. Do you ever find yourself wishing that God would will something different for you than what He has decreed? How does acknowledging the harmony of God's knowledge and will change the way you accept His decree in your life?
?
The argument says the will cannot attach to impossibilities. Are there things you are chasing that are impossible because they contradict God's knowledge? What would it mean for you to align your will with what God knows and wills for you?

Response 4: Why Creation Could Not Occur in Pre-eternity
Razi argues that creation in pre-eternity is logically impossible, so the will had to attach to a temporal moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth response: that coming-to-be (ḥudūth) and pre-eternity (azal) are contradictory, making creation in pre-eternity impossible, so the will could not attach to it.

Core Idea
Coming-to-be and pre-eternity cannot coexist; the impossible is not an object of will.

Razi's fourth response tackles the question from the perspective of the definitions of key terms. 'Coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' (azal) means the negation of a beginning. These two concepts are mutually exclusive. Therefore, it is logically impossible for the world to have come to be in pre-eternity. Since the will cannot attach to the impossible, it could not have willed creation in pre-eternity. In this lesson, you will learn how Razi uses conceptual analysis to block the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the definitions of ḥudūth and azal.
2
Understand the contradiction
Coming-to-be implies a beginning, pre-eternity negates a beginning. They cannot be combined.
3
Apply to the objection
Since creation in pre-eternity is impossible, the will could not have attached to it. Therefore, the specific time is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً وموجداً ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل كان من جملة تلك الأمور : كونه تعالى مريداً لإحداث العالم ، وأنه تعالى كان يريد إحداث العالم في وقت معين ، فيما لا يزال . فلهذا السبب اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت . فإن قلتم فلم اقتضت إرادة الله تعالى إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ فنقول الجواب عنه من وجوه : الوجه الأول في الجواب : أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن إرادة الله تعالى أوجبت بحقيقتها المخصوصة تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، وتلك الإرادة ليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط قول القائل : لم تعلقت تلك الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ لأن تلك الإرادة لما كانت بحقيقتها المخصوصة ، يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وامتنع تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ، وكان ذلك الوجوب المخصوص ، والامتناع المخصوص ، من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة ، وامتنع قول القائل : لم وقعت تلك الإرادة على ذلك الوجه المعين، دون سائر الوجوه ؟ الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، لأن إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت أصلح للمكلفين من إحداثه في سائر الأوقات فلأجل هذا المعنى اختص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنا لا نعرف لذلك الوقت المعين خاصية معينة ، إلا أنا نقول : عدم علمنا بذلك لا يقدح في حصوله في ذلك الوقت . الوجه الثالث في الجواب : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات ، وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن العالم يحدث في الوقت الفلاني بعينه ومعلوم الله تعالى واجب الوقوع ، ممتنع التغير . والإرادة لا تعلق لها بالمحالات ، فلا جرم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، ولم تتعلق بإيقاعه في سائر الأوقات . الوجه الرابع في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما لم تتعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في الأزل ، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الذي سبقه العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية ، والجمع بينهما محال ، والمحال غير مقدور ولا مراد . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن حصول الأزل مانع من الحدوث ، فكان الحدوث في الأزل محالاً ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة العالم في الأزل . الوجه الخامس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العالم قبل أن حدث ، كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه قبل الوقت الذي أحدثه فيه . الوجه السادس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن قول القائل لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في هذا الوقت : سؤال باطل ، لأن تلك الإرادة لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، لا في هذا الوقت ، لكان ذلك السؤال المذكور عائداً ، ولما كان ذلك السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات كان باطلا ؟
Everything that is necessary for Him, exalted, to be an agent and creator—whatever was present in pre-eternity—was among those matters: His being, exalted, willing to bring about the world, and that He, exalted, was willing to bring about the world at a specific time, in what never ceases. For this reason the coming-to-be of the world was specified to that time. If you say: Why did the will of God, exalted, necessitate bringing about the world at that specific time rather than before it or after it? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: The first aspect in the answer: That we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, necessitated, by its specific reality, the specification of bringing about the world at that specific time, and that will does not have the capacity to necessitate bringing about the world at another time? And on this assumption, the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will attach to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times?' falls away. Because that will, since it is by its specific reality, its attachment to bringing about the world at that specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about the world at other than that time is impossible, and that specific necessity and that specific impossibility are necessary concomitants of the quiddity of that will, and the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will occur in that specific manner, rather than other manners?' is impossible. The second aspect in the answer: That it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time only because bringing about the world at that time is more beneficial for the morally responsible than bringing it about at other times? And for this reason the bringing about of the world was specified to that specific time? The utmost in the matter is that it is said: We do not know for that specific time a specific property, but we say: Our lack of knowledge of that does not detract from its occurrence at that time. The third aspect in the answer: That He, exalted, is knower of all particulars, and this necessitates that He, exalted, knew in pre-eternity that the world would come to be at exactly such-and-such a time, and what God knows is necessarily occurring, impossible to change. And the will has no attachment to impossible things, so necessarily the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times. The fourth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The will did not attach to bringing about the world in pre-eternity only because coming-to-be (ḥudūth) is an existence preceded by non-existence, and pre-eternity (azal) is the negation of beginningness, and combining them is impossible, and the impossible is neither within power nor will. And when this is established, it is clear that the occurrence of pre-eternity is a hindrance to coming-to-be, so coming-to-be in pre-eternity would be impossible; for this reason the will of the world did not attach in pre-eternity. The fifth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The world, before it came to be, was impossible of existence in itself and by its essence, then it became possible of existence in itself and by its essence; for this reason the will of God, exalted, did not attach to bringing it about before the time in which He brought it about. The sixth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says 'Why did the will of God, exalted, attach to bringing about the world at this time?' is an invalid question, because if that will attached to bringing about the world at another time, not at this time, that same question would recur, and since that question would recur in all cases, it is invalid?
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six responses (wujūh) to a classical theological objection: If God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. First, the passage sets up the premise that God's will is eternal and that He always willed the creation of the world at a specific time. The objector asks: Why that time specifically? The first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) appeals to the specificity of the divine will itself: God's will has a unique 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to that exact time, and it lacks the capacity to attach to another time. Thus, the question 'why this time?' is improper because the will's nature makes it necessarily specific. The second answer invokes divine wisdom: that time was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents (mukallafūn). Even if we do not know the specific benefit, our ignorance does not disprove its existence. The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. Since will cannot attach to what is impossible (the object of knowledge must occur), the will attached necessarily to that time. The fourth answer argues from the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (azal): 'coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' negates beginning—they are contradictory, so creation in pre-eternity is impossible and thus not an object of will. The fifth answer posits a change in the world's essence: before creation, the world was inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi), then it became inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi), so the will could not attach before that change. The sixth answer argues that the question is inherently invalid (bāṭil) because if the will had attached to a different time, the same question would arise, showing that it is not a genuine objection but a universalizable demand that cannot be satisfied.

أزل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think pre-eternity is simply a very long time, but it is a logical category of 'no beginning'.
Correction: Pre-eternity is not a temporal duration but the negation of beginning.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the will could not have attached to creation in pre-eternity?
A) Because God's will is eternal.
B) Because pre-eternity and coming-to-be are contradictory.
C) Because God did not know that time.
D) Because pre-eternity is not a specific time.

Key Takeaway
The fourth response argues that the very definition of 'coming-to-be' prevents it from occurring in pre-eternity, so the will's attachment to the actual time is logically necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God delayed something in your life? This lesson shows that attributing 'delay' to God is a failure to understand that timing is necessary for the nature of creation. Ask yourself: Do I truly trust that God's will attached at the perfect moment, or do I harbor dissatisfaction with the timing of my own existence? What can you do today to surrender to God's timing?
?
The impossible is not within God's power. Does that shock you? How does this affect your understanding of God's power? Are there things you ask God for that are actually impossible by definition—like wanting a created thing to be eternal without beginning? What does this reveal about your assumptions regarding God's actions?
?
This passage forces you to reckon: Creation had to be preceded by non-existence. Your very existence came from nothing. How does that humbling fact change the way you view your own life? Will you live today as if you came from nothing, or as if you have always been?

Response 5: The World's Change from Impossibility to Possibility
Razi argues that the world's modal status changed from impossible to possible, explaining the timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth response: that the world, before its creation, was impossible in essence, and then became possible, so the will could only attach after that change.

Core Idea
Before creation, the world was impossible; the impossible cannot be willed.

The fifth response introduces a metaphysical transformation: the world's own essence changed from being inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi) to being inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi). Before this change, the world's existence was impossible, so God's will could not have attached to it. Once it became possible, the will attached at the earliest feasible moment. In this lesson, you will explore this intriguing idea of an essence 'flipping' from impossibility to possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the terms 'mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi' and 'mumkin li-ʿaynihi'.
2
Understand the modal shift
The world's essence was at first impossible, then became possible. This change is not external but in the essence itself.
This is a unique argument within kalam.
3
Apply to the objection
Since the will cannot attach to what is impossible, it had to wait until the world became possible.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents a series of six responses (wujūh) to a classical theological objection: If God's will is eternal and unchanging, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection implies arbitrariness in the divine will. The text is structured as an objection-and-answer, with the objection stated concisely and then six distinct lines of answer given. First, the passage sets up the premise that God's will is eternal and that He always willed the creation of the world at a specific time. The objector asks: Why that time specifically? The first answer (al-wajh al-awwal) appeals to the specificity of the divine will itself: God's will has a unique 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) that necessitates its attachment to that exact time, and it lacks the capacity to attach to another time. Thus, the question 'why this time?' is improper because the will's nature makes it necessarily specific. The second answer invokes divine wisdom: that time was the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for moral agents (mukallafūn). Even if we do not know the specific benefit, our ignorance does not disprove its existence. The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. Since will cannot attach to what is impossible (the object of knowledge must occur), the will attached necessarily to that time. The fourth answer argues from the impossibility of creation in pre-eternity (azal): 'coming-to-be' (ḥudūth) means existence preceded by non-existence, while 'pre-eternity' negates beginning—they are contradictory, so creation in pre-eternity is impossible and thus not an object of will. The fifth answer posits a change in the world's essence: before creation, the world was inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ li-ʿaynihi), then it became inherently possible (mumkin li-ʿaynihi), so the will could not attach before that change. The sixth answer argues that the question is inherently invalid (bāṭil) because if the will had attached to a different time, the same question would arise, showing that it is not a genuine objection but a universalizable demand that cannot be satisfied.

Key Terms
ممتنع
ممكن
عين
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may object that change in essence is contradictory; but in kalam, essences are not necessarily fixed.
Correction: The response asserts that essences can change in modal status.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth response, why did the will not attach before the actual time?
A) Because the world was impossible in essence before that time.
B) Because God did not will it.
C) Because the world did not exist.
D) Because the time was not beneficial.

Key Takeaway
The fifth response claims the world's own essence transitioned from impossible to possible, and the will could only attach after that transition.

?
This passage says the world's own essence caused its modal change. Do you believe that God can change the very nature of your heart, or do you limit His power to what seems possible now? What step will you take to seek that change?

Response 6: The Question is Invalid on Its Own Terms
Razi argues that the objection is self-defeating because it can be raised for any possible time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth response: that the question 'why this time?' is inherently invalid because it would arise for any time chosen.

Core Idea
Every possible answer is open to the same question; thus the question is flawed.

Razi's final response is a meta-argument: the question itself is invalid because it can be applied equally to any alternative. If the will had attached to another time, the same question 'why that time?' would be asked. Therefore, the question does not pinpoint a genuine flaw but reflects a general human tendency to demand reasons for contingent facts. In this lesson, you will learn how Razi uses the principle of the recurring question to show the objection's impotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the argument that the question recurs for any time.
2
Grasp the logic
If the will had attached to another time, the same 'why' would apply. So the question is not specific to this time.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the question always applies, it cannot be a genuine objection; it is invalid (bāṭil).

كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً وموجداً ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل كان من جملة تلك الأمور : كونه تعالى مريداً لإحداث العالم ، وأنه تعالى كان يريد إحداث العالم في وقت معين ، فيما لا يزال . فلهذا السبب اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت . فإن قلتم فلم اقتضت إرادة الله تعالى إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله أو ما بعده ؟ فنقول الجواب عنه من وجوه : الوجه الأول في الجواب : أن نقول : لم لا تعلقت تلك الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ لأن تلك الإرادة لما كانت بحقيقتها المخصوصة ، يجب تعلقها بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وامتنع تعلقها بإحداث الجواب : أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، لأن إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت أصلح للمكلفين من إحداثه في سائر الأوقات فلأجل هذا المعنى اختص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنا لا نعرف لذلك الوقت المعين خاصية معينة ، إلا أنا نقول : عدم علمنا بذلك لا يقدح في حصوله في ذلك الوقت . الوجه الثالث في الجواب : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات ، وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل ، بأن العالم يحدث في الوقت الفلاني بعينه ومعلوم الله تعالى واجب الوقوع ، ممتنع التغير . والإرادة لا تعلق لها بالمحالات ، فلا جرم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، ولم تتعلق بإيقاعه في سائر الأوقات . الوجه الرابع في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنما لم تتعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في الأزل ، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الذي سبقه العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية ، والجمع بينهما محال ، والمحال غير مقدور ولا مراد . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن حصول الأزل مانع من الحدوث ، فكان الحدوث في الأزل محالاً ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة العالم في الأزل . الوجه الخامس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العالم قبل أن حدث ، كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لعينه ولذاته ، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه قبل الوقت الذي أحدثه فيه . الوجه السادس في الجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن قول القائل لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في هذا الوقت : سؤال باطل ، لأن تلك الإرادة لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، لا في هذا الوقت ، لكان ذلك السؤال المذكور عائداً ، ولما كان ذلك السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات كان باطلا ؟
Everything that is necessary for Him, exalted, to be an agent and creator—whatever was present in pre-eternity—was among those matters: His being, exalted, willing to bring about the world, and that He, exalted, was willing to bring about the world at a specific time, in what never ceases. For this reason the coming-to-be of the world was specified to that time. If you say: Why did the will of God, exalted, necessitate bringing about the world at that specific time rather than before it or after it? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: The first aspect in the answer: That we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, necessitated, by its specific reality, the specification of bringing about the world at that specific time, and that will does not have the capacity to necessitate bringing about the world at another time? And on this assumption, the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will attach to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times?' falls away. Because that will, since it is by its specific reality, its attachment to bringing about the world at that specific time is necessary, and its attachment to bringing about the world at other than that time is impossible, and that specific necessity and that specific impossibility are necessary concomitants of the quiddity of that will, and the statement of the one who says: 'Why did that will occur in that specific manner, rather than other manners?' is impossible. The second aspect in the answer: That it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time only because bringing about the world at that time is more beneficial for the morally responsible than bringing it about at other times? And for this reason the bringing about of the world was specified to that specific time? The utmost in the matter is that it is said: We do not know for that specific time a specific property, but we say: Our lack of knowledge of that does not detract from its occurrence at that time. The third aspect in the answer: That He, exalted, is knower of all particulars, and this necessitates that He, exalted, knew in pre-eternity that the world would come to be at exactly such-and-such a time, and what God knows is necessarily occurring, impossible to change. And the will has no attachment to impossible things, so necessarily the will of God, exalted, attached to bringing about the world at that specific time, and did not attach to bringing it about at other times. The fourth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The will did not attach to bringing about the world in pre-eternity only because coming-to-be (ḥudūth) is an existence preceded by non-existence, and pre-eternity (azal) is the negation of beginningness, and combining them is impossible, and the impossible is neither within power nor will. And when this is established, it is clear that the occurrence of pre-eternity is a hindrance to coming-to-be, so coming-to-be in pre-eternity would be impossible; for this reason the will of the world did not attach in pre-eternity. The fifth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The world, before it came to be, was impossible of existence in itself and by its essence, then it became possible of existence in itself and by its essence; for this reason the will of God, exalted, did not attach to bringing it about before the time in which He brought it about. The sixth aspect in the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says 'Why did the will of God, exalted, attach to bringing about the world at this time?' is an invalid question, because if that will attached to bringing about the world at another time, not at this time, that same question would recur, and since that question would recur in all cases, it is invalid?

The third answer ties the time to divine foreknowledge: God knew eternally that the world would occur at that exact moment, and His knowledge is immutable and necessitates occurrence. God's Eternal Knowledge Determines the Time of Creation
Third argument linking God's knowledge to specific creation time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's eternal knowledge of all particulars determines that the world was created at a specific time, and how His will follows His knowledge without contradiction.

Core Idea
God's perfect knowledge sets the time, making His will inevitable, not arbitrary.

In this lesson, we explore the third argument answering why God created the world at a particular moment. The argument builds on God's eternal and all-encompassing knowledge: because He knows everything from eternity, He knows exactly when the world will occur. His will then necessarily attaches to that moment, as attaching to any other would contradict His knowledge—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: God's knowledge leads to a specific known time, which then binds His will.
The passage is concise; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with the premise that God knows all particulars. Ask yourself: why is this premise crucial for the conclusion?
It establishes the foundation: God's knowledge is eternal and infallible.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the sequence: God's eternal knowledge → specific time known → that known time must occur → His will attaches only to what is possible → thus, His will attaches to that time and not others.
The chain links knowledge, necessity, and will.
4
Reflect on the role of impossibility
Notice the statement: 'will has no attachment to impossibilities.' Why is this important? It explains why God's will does not attach to other times—because that would contradict His knowledge.
This prevents the misconception that God's will is arbitrary.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the MCQ. This will reinforce the core reasoning.
The question directly asks why the will attaches to that specific time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في الجواب: أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات، وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل، بأن العالم يحدث في الوقت الفلاني بعينه، ومعلوم الله تعالى واجب الوقوع، ممتنع التغير. والإرادة لا تعلق لها بالمحالات، فلا جرم تعلقت إرادة الله بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ولم تتعلق بإيقاعه في سائر الأوقات.
The third aspect of the answer: That He, exalted, is Knower of all particulars, and this necessitates that He, exalted, was eternally knowing that the world occurs at that specific time, and what is known by God, exalted, is necessarily occurring, impossible to change. And the will has no attachment to impossibilities. So inevitably, God's will attached to causing the world to occur at that specific time, and did not attach to causing it at other times.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the third argument in answer to the question why God created the world at a particular time. The argument is based on God's eternal knowledge. Since God knows all particulars eternally, He knows that the world will occur at that specific time. God's knowledge is infallible and what He knows must occur. Therefore, His will attaches to that time because attaching to another time would contradict His knowledge, which is impossible. The argument emphasizes that God's will does not attach to impossibilities.

The Impossibility of Origination in Eternity
The fourth argument defines huduth and azal as contradictory, showing the impossibility of creation in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining temporal origination (huduth) and eternity (azal) is logically impossible, and that God's will does not attach to impossibilities.

Core Idea
Huduth and azal are mutually exclusive; God does not will the impossible.

This lesson presents the fourth argument against the possibility of creation in eternity. It is a logical argument based on the definitions of 'huduth' (temporal origination) and 'azal' (eternity, no beginning). By clearly defining these terms, the author shows that they cannot coexist, and thus God's will—which only pertains to possible things—could not have attached to creating the world in eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it starts with an objection in the form of a question, then provides an answer with definitions and logical deduction.
Pay attention to the definitions of huduth and azal—they are the key premises.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify the definitions: huduth means 'existence preceded by non-existence'; azal means 'negation of a beginning'. Write these down in your own words.
These definitions are taken directly from the text.
3
Recognize the contradiction
See why the combination of huduth and azal is impossible: something cannot both have a beginning (since it is preceded by non-existence) and have no beginning (since azal negates a beginning). Thus, the conjunction is logically impossible.
The text calls this combination 'muhal' (impossible).
4
Apply the rule about God's will
Note that the impossible is not within God's power (qudra) nor object of His will (irada). Therefore, the will of God did not attach to creating the world in eternity because that would be an impossibility.
This concludes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع في الجواب: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنما لم تتعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في الأزل، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن الوجود الذي سبقه العدم، والأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية، والجمع بينهما محال، والمحال غير مقدور ولا مراد. وإذا ثبت هذا، ظهر أن حصول الأزل مانع من الحدوث، فكان الحدوث في الأزل محالاً، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة العالم في الأزل.
The fourth aspect of the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The will did not attach to bringing about the world in eternity (al-azal) because 'coming into existence' (al-huduth) is existence preceded by non-existence, and 'eternity' (al-azal) is negation of beginning; combining them is impossible, and the impossible is not within power nor object of will. And when this is established, it becomes clear that the occurrence of eternity prevents coming into existence, so coming into existence in eternity is impossible; for this reason the will of the world did not attach in eternity.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument, which uses the logical impossibility of combining 'huduth' (temporal origination) and 'azal' (eternity, i.e., without beginning). Since 'huduth' implies prior non-existence, and 'azal' negates any beginning, the world cannot be both originated and eternal at the same time. God's will does not attach to impossibilities, so the will did not attach to creating the world in eternity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could create an eternal world because He is all-powerful.
Correction: This argument shows that 'eternal world' is a contradictory concept because 'world' implies temporal origination (huduth) which requires a beginning, while 'eternal' (azal) negates a beginning. The impossible is not an object of power or will.

Key Takeaway
Creation in eternity is impossible because huduth and azal are logically contradictory; thus God's will does not attach to it.

Ninth Response: Creation as Manifestation of Divine Power
Al-Razi gives the ninth response: creation aims at manifesting divine power; timing is discretionary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's ninth response to the objection against temporal creation, based on the Qur'anic verse and the principle of beneficence.

Core Idea
Beneficence frees the Creator from needing a reason for the timing of creation.

This lesson presents the ninth and final response in Al-Razi's series of answers to the argument against temporal creation. Al-Razi grounds his response in a clear Qur'anic verse and a rational principle: if God's motive is beneficence, He may choose any time for creation without needing an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without breaking it down. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the passage starts by stating the response and then quotes the Quran.
2
Identify the scriptural basis
Focus on the Quranic verse (65:12) cited by Al-Razi. Understand how it supports the idea that creation reveals God's power and divinity.
The verse explicitly says 'so that you may know that Allah is over all things powerful.'
3
Grasp the key argument about time
Al-Razi argues that the specific time of creation is not necessary because the motive (beneficence) allows free choice. The benefactor is not constrained.
This refutes the objection that creation must be eternal or have a specific cause for its timing.
4
Understand how this concludes the nine responses
The final sentence indicates that these nine aspects together form the complete answer to the objection. Reflect on the cumulative force of the responses.

الوجه التاسع في الجواب : أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق العالم إظهاراً لقدرته ، وليستدل به على إلهيته . قالوا : وهذا هو الجواب المذكور في القرآن وهو قول الله تعالى ( الله الذي خلق سبع سموات ، ومن الأرض مثلهن ، يتنزل الأمر بينهن ، لتعلموا : أن الله على كل شيء قدير ، وأن الله قد أحاط بكل شيء علما ) فبين : أنه تعالى إنما خلق هذه الأشياء ليستدل بها على كمال إلهيته ، وكمال قدرته . وأما تعيين الوقت ، فغير لازم . لأنه لما كان الداعي له إلى خلق هذه الأشياء : هو الإحسان والتفضل . فالمحسن والمتفضل مختار في تعيين الوقت . فلم يكن هذا التعيين محتاجاً إلى سبب زائد . فهذه الوجوه التسعة هي الأجوبة المذكورة عن الحجة المذكورة .
The ninth aspect of the answer: It is said: He Most High created the world as a manifestation of His power, and so that evidence for His divinity may be derived from it. They said: This is the answer mentioned in the Qur'an, namely the saying of God Most High: 'Allah is the One who created seven heavens and of the earth the like of them. The command descends among them so that you may know that Allah is over all things powerful and that Allah encompasses all things in knowledge.' (65:12) So He made clear that He Most High only created these things so that evidence for the perfection of His divinity and the perfection of His power may be derived from them. As for the determination of the time, it is not necessary. Because since the motive for creating these things is beneficence and grace, the beneficent and gracious one is free in determining the time. Thus this determination does not need an additional cause. These nine aspects are the answers mentioned against the mentioned argument.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an answer (jawab) that provides a scriptural and rational argument. It cites Qur'an 65:12 to show that God created the world so that people may know His complete power and knowledge. The specific time of creation is not necessary because the motive (da'i) is beneficence (ihsan) and grace (tafaddul), and the benefactor is free to choose the time without requiring an external cause. This concludes the nine responses to the objection.

The Dilemma of Divine Will: General or Specific
Razi presents the second proof, arguing that the will must be either general or specific, leading to either indeterminism or necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof against the first answer, which forces a choice between two options for God's will, each leading to a contradiction with divine freedom.

Core Idea
God's will must be either general or specific; both options destroy the concept of a free agent.

Razi continues his critique by presenting a second proof that exposes a fatal dilemma in the first answer. The argument forces the opponent to choose between two alternatives: either God's will is capable of requiring the world at any time, or it is exclusively determined to one specific time. Both options lead to an unacceptable conclusion, either indeterminism or necessitarianism. This passage sharpens the debate on divine attributes and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two options
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi sets up a dichotomy: either God's will is capable of requiring the world at all times (general), or only at one specific time (specific). These are the two horns of the dilemma.
The structure is a classic argument by cases.
2
Understand the first option: general will
If the will is general, then its relation to different times is equal. Any specific choice would require a preponderator. Without a preponderator, you have indeterminism (رجحان لا لمرجح), which is false. If you invoke a preponderator, it leads to infinite regress.
This branch ends in a problem of either arbitrariness or regress.
3
Understand the second option: specific will
If the will is exclusively determined to one time, then God's essence entails that will, which entails the world at that time. This makes God a necessitating cause (موجب بالذات), not a free agent (مختار). That contradicts the opponent's own claim.
This branch shows the will would be necessitated, not free.
4
Grasp the overall conclusion
Since both options lead to absurdity or contradiction, the first answer is refuted. The proof thus upholds the necessity of a preponderator and divine freedom as understood by Razi.
The argument does not merely reject the answer but shows it self-destructs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان فساد هذا الجواب: أن نقول: إرادة الله تعالى إما أن يقال: إنها صالحة لاقتضاء إحداث العالم في سائر الأوقات، أو غير صالحة، إلا لاقتضاء إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. فإن كان الأول، فحينئذ يكون نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى اقتضاء إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، كنسبتها إلى اقتضاء إحداث العالم في سائر الأوقات. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فحينئذ اقتضاء تلك الإرادة، إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات، يكون أمراً جائزاً. فإن كان قد حصل الرجحان، لا عن مرجح، فقد جوزتم حصول رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، وذلك باطل. وإن أحوجتموه إلى مرجح، عاد البحث في أن ذلك المرجح، هل هو صالح للطرفين، أو غير صالح إلا للطرف الواحد؟ وأما إن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن تلك الإرادة متعينة لاقتضاء إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في سائر الأوقات، فحينئذ لا يكون الله تعالى مختاراً بل كان موجباً بالذات لهذا الأمر المعين فإن ذاته مستلزمة لتلك الإرادة، وتلك الإرادة مستلزمة لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ومستلزم المستلزم: مستلزم. فتكون ذات الله تعالى مستلزمة لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس له إمكان إحداثه في وقت آخر، فلا معنى للموجب إلا ذلك. وهذا يقدح في قولكم: إن إله العالم فاعل مختار.
The second proof in explaining the corruption of this answer: That we say: The will of God Most High, either it is said that it is capable of requiring the bringing about of the world at all times, or not capable except for requiring the bringing about of the world at that specific time. If the first, then the relation of that will to requiring the bringing about of the world at that time is like its relation to requiring the bringing about of the world at all times. And if the matter is so, then the requiring of that will to bring about the world at that time to the exclusion of other times would be contingent. If the preponderance occurred without a preponderator, then you have allowed the preponderance of one of the two sides of a possible over the other without a preponderator, and that is false. And if you require it to a preponderator, then the inquiry returns as to whether that preponderator is capable of both sides or not capable except for one side. And if the truth is the second division, that that will is determinate for requiring the bringing about of the world at that specific time and does not have the capacity to require the bringing about of the world at other times, then God Most High would not be free but would be necessitating by essence for this specific matter, for His essence entails that will, and that will entails the bringing about of the world at that specific time, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer. So the essence of God Most High would entail the bringing about of the world at that specific time, and He would not have the possibility to bring it about at another time, and there is no meaning to 'necessitating' except that. And this impugns your statement that the God of the world is a free agent.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the first answer. The argument is a dichotomy: either God's will is capable of requiring the world's creation at any time, or it is exclusively capable of requiring it at that specific time. If the former, then why would it choose one time over others without a preponderator? That would be indeterminism, which is false. If a preponderator is invoked, it leads to infinite regress. If the latter, then God's will is necessitated, making God a necessitating cause rather than a free agent, which contradicts the claim that God is a free chooser. Thus, the first answer fails.

Key Terms
اختيار
إرادة
موجب بالذات
مرجح
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think both options are acceptable; the proof shows both lead to absurdity.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that either way, the opponent's position collapses: the general-will option leads to indeterminism or regress, and the specific-will option leads to necessitarianism, both of which the opponent rejects.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude if God's will is specifically determined to require the world at one particular time?
A) God is a free agent.
B) God is a necessitating cause.
C) The world is eternal.
D) The argument is inconclusive.

Key Takeaway
God's will cannot be both general and specific; each leads to a denial of divine freedom, so the first answer fails.

?
Razi's dilemma shows that human reasoning about God's will can lead to contradictions. Are you willing to accept that some aspects of divine freedom are beyond your comprehension, or do you try to force God into a logical box? What is the cost of the latter?

Refutation: Specific Will Implies Necessity
Refutes that God's will can be specific without necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that God's will is specific without a preponderator leads to necessitarianism, undermining divine free agency.

Core Idea
A specific will without a differentiator makes God a necessitator, not free.

The author presents a logical dilemma: either God's will is specific without a preponderator (invalid) or with a preponderator that is either indifferent or determined. If determined, then God's essence entails a specific creation, making Him a necessitator. This refutes the opponent's claim of free agency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage to grasp the two options: allowing preponderance without a preponderator (invalid) or requiring one, which leads to further inquiry.
The opponent had claimed God's will can be specific without cause.
2
Identify the two branches
If a preponderator is needed, it is either suitable for both sides or only one. Focus on the latter, which leads to determinism.
3
Trace the entailment chain
God's essence entails a specific will, that will entails creation at a specific time. Therefore, God's essence entails creation, making Him a necessitator.
This uses the principle: the entailer of an entailer is an entailer.
4
Conclude the refutation
Thus, the opponent's claim that God is a free agent is invalid if His will is specific and determined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد جوزتم حصول رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، وذلك باطل. وإن أحوجتموه إلى مرجح، عاد البحث في أن ذلك المرجح، هل هو صالح للطرفين، أو غير صالح إلا للطرف الواحد؟ وأما إن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن تلك الإرادة متعينة لاقتضاء إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس لها صلاحية اقتضاء إحداث العالم في سائر الأوقات، فحينئذ لا يكون الله تعالى مختاراً بل كان موجباً بالذات لهذا الأمر المعين [فإن ذاته مستلزمة لتلك الإرادة، وتلك الإرادة مستلزمة لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين] ومستلزم المستلزم: مستلزم. فتكون ذات الله [تعالى] مستلزمة لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس له إمكان إحداثه في وقت آخر، فلا معنى للموجب إلا ذلك. وهذا يقدح في قولكم: إن إله العالم فاعل مختار.
For you have permitted that a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other occurs without a preponderator, and that is false. And if you make it need a preponderator, the inquiry returns: is that preponderator suitable for both sides, or is it suitable only for the one side? But if the truth is the second division, namely that that will is determined to necessitate the bringing about of the world at that specific time, and it has no capacity to necessitate the bringing about of the world at other times, then in that case God Most High is not a free chooser (mukhtār) but rather a necessitator (mūjib) by essence for this specific matter [for His essence entails that will, and that will entails the bringing about of the world at that specific time] and the entailed of the entailed is entailed. So the essence of God [Most High] becomes entailing the bringing about of the world at that specific time, and He has no possibility of bringing it about at another time, so the meaning of 'necessitator' is nothing but that. And this invalidates your statement: 'The God of the world is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār).'
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that God's will could be specifically directed to create the world at a particular time without a preponderator. The author presents a logical argument: If the opponent allows preponderance without a preponderator, that is invalid. If they require a preponderator, then one must ask whether that preponderator is equally suitable for both sides. If it is suitable only for one side, then God's will becomes determined and necessitated, not free. The author then uses the principle of entailment: if God's essence entails a specific will, and that will entails creation at that time, then God's essence entails creation at that specific time, making God a necessitator rather than a free agent. This, he concludes, invalidates the opponent's claim that God is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār).

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjiḥ)
متعينة (muta‘ayyina)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
مستلزم (mustalzim)
فاعل مختار (fā‘il mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will can be specific (e.g., to create the world at a particular time) without any cause or differentiator, and God remains absolutely free.
Correction: The passage argues that if God's will is specific without a preponderator, it is arbitrary (invalid). If it requires a preponderator that is uniquely determined, then God's essence necessitates that will, making Him a necessitator, not free.

Checkpoint

A) God remains a free agent.
B) God becomes a necessitator by essence.
C) The argument is inconclusive.
D) God has infinite possibilities.

Key Takeaway
A specific will without a differentiator either leads to brute preference (invalid) or to necessitarianism, contradicting free agency.

This argument shows that a determined will cannot be free. Examine your own view: do you often think of God's decisions as 'just what He had to do'? What does that imply about your reliance on His mercy and responsiveness? What change in your belief does this passage demand today?

Third Proof: The Dilemma of the Specific Time
Third argument: examines whether the specific time is present in eternity, leading to infinite regress or eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the opponent's view, which shows that the specific time of creation must either be present in eternity (leading to an eternal world) or be a temporal event leading to infinite regress or eternal creation.

Core Idea
The specific time of creation forces a dilemma: eternal effect or infinite regress.

In this passage, the author presents the third proof that challenges the opponent's claim that the world was created at a specific time in the past. The argument examines the status of that specific time itself: is it eternal or temporal? Each answer leads to a conclusion that either contradicts the opponent's position or implies that God has been creating from eternity, which is exactly what the argument seeks to establish.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage and identify the key question: Is the specific time present in eternity or not?
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Explore the first branch
If the time is present in eternity, then the world must exist in eternity. Notice why this is a problem for the opponent.
The opponent claims the world is temporal, so this branch is unacceptable.
3
Explore the second branch
If the time is not present, it must be created. Consider the two sub-options: either without a further time (leading to eternal effect again) or at another time (leading to infinite regress).
Both sub-options lead to undesirable conclusions for the opponent.
4
Analyze the infinite regress and conclusion
Note that the argument distinguishes between two types of infinite regress: simultaneous (impossible) and sequential (leading to eternal creation). The conclusion is that sequential regress implies God's eternal creating, which is the desired outcome.
This shows that the opponent's position collapses into the view they sought to avoid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في بيان ضعف هذا الكلام: أن نقول: [إن] تلك الإرادة [لما] اقتضت إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. فذلك الوقت المعين هل هو حاضر في الأزل أو غير حاضر في الأزل؟ فإن كان حاضراً في الأزل، لزم حصول الأثر لا محالة في الأزل، وإن كان غير حاضر في الأزل فذلك الوقت المعين أمر حادث، فلا بد وأن يكون حدوثه بإحداث الله تعالى. فإما أن يقال: إنه تعالى أراد إحداث ذلك الوقت، من غير أن يختص إحداثه بوقت معين، أو يقال: إنه أراد إحداثه بشرط أن يختص إحداثه بوقت معين، فإن كان الأول، لزم أن يكون ذلك الوقت حاصلاً [في الأزل] وحينئذ يعود ما ذكرنا من أنه يلزم قدم الأثر، وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى أراد إحداث ذلك الوقت في وقت آخر معين، لزم افتقار ذلك الوقت إلى وقت آخر، ولزم التسلسل. ثم [إن] ذلك التسلسل إن وقع دفعة واحدة فهو محال، لما ثبت أن القول بإثبات أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة: محال. وإن كان بحيث يكون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بآخر، لا إلى أول، كان هذا قولاً بأنه تعالى موجد لهذه الحوادث من الأزل إلى الأبد. وهو المطلوب.
The third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) in showing the weakness of this speech: we say: [Indeed] that will [since] necessitated the bringing about of the world at that specific time. So is that specific time present (ḥāḍir) in eternity (al-azal) or not present in eternity? If it is present in eternity, then the effect (al-athar) necessarily occurs in eternity. If it is not present in eternity, then that specific time is a temporal event (ḥādith), and its occurrence must be by God Most High's bringing about. Then either it is said: He Most High willed to bring about that time, without His bringing about being specific to a particular time, or it is said: He willed to bring it about on condition that its bringing about be specific to a particular time. If the first, then that time must have come to be [in eternity], and then what we mentioned returns, namely that the pre-eternity of the effect is entailed. If the second, namely that He Most High willed to bring about that time at another specific time, then that time would need another time, and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would ensue. Then [indeed] that infinite regress, if it occurs all at once, is impossible (muḥāl), for it has been established that the assertion of infinite causes and effects all at once is impossible. And if it is such that each one of them is preceded by another, without a first, then this is a statement that He Most High is the bringer into existence (mūjid) of these temporal events from eternity to eternity. And that is the desired (al-maṭlūb).
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) against the opponent's view. The argument begins with a conditional: given that God's will necessitated creation at a specific time, we ask whether that specific time is present in eternity (al-azal) or not. If it is present in eternity, then the effect (the world) must exist in eternity, contradicting the opponent's claim that the world is temporal. If the time is not present in eternity, then it is itself a temporal event and must be created by God. Now we consider how God creates that time: either He creates it without specifying a further time, in which case it would have been created in eternity (since God's will is eternal), again leading to an eternal effect. Or He creates it at another specific time, which leads to an infinite regress of times. The argument then considers two forms of infinite regress: if all times occur simultaneously, that is impossible; if each time is preceded by another without a first, then this implies that God is continuously creating temporal events from eternity to eternity, which is exactly the conclusion the argument aims to establish (al-maṭlūb).

Key Terms
الحجة الثالثة (al-ḥujja al-thālitha)
الأزل (al-azal)
حادث (ḥādith)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
المطلوب (al-maṭlūb)
إقتضت (iqtaḍat)
حاضر (ḥāḍir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument claims all infinite regress is impossible.
Correction: The argument specifies that only a simultaneous infinite regress is impossible; a sequential regress is acceptable and leads to the desired conclusion of eternal creation.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, if the specific time is not present in eternity, what are the two possibilities for how God creates that time?
A) Either He creates it without a specific time or at a specific time.
B) Either He creates it from nothing or from pre-existing matter.
C) Either He creates it in eternity or in time.
D) Either He creates it through will or through power.

Key Takeaway
The third argument forces the opponent to accept either that the world is eternal or that God has been creating from eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has been creating you from eternity to eternity, every moment of your existence depends on His constant creative act. Do you live as if you are being created right now, or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency? What one daily habit can you establish to remind yourself that you are continuously dependent on God?
?
The argument shows that God’s will is not bound by time—He creates without beginning. How does that change your perspective on your own past mistakes and future anxieties? Will you trust that God’s eternal will encompasses all moments? What specific worry can you surrender to Him right now, acknowledging that He is the eternal creator of every moment?
?
If the alternative to eternal creation is an infinite regress (which is impossible), then you cannot escape the reality that God’s creative action is without beginning. Does this thought make you feel overwhelmed or liberated? How can you respond to this truth by increasing your gratitude and dependence on God each day?

The Fourth Proof: Time Distinction Implies Pre-Eternity
Fourth argument: time distinction implies pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that argues that the distinction between the time of action and the time of inaction leads to the pre-eternity of times, and why the opponent rejects this implication.

Core Idea
A property distinguishing times forces them to be pre-eternal, but the opponent denies this.

This proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: if God's will chooses one time over another for creation, that time must have a distinguishing property (khāṣṣiyya). But such a property would mean that the times themselves are pre-eternal (qadīma). The opponent cannot accept this conclusion, so this argument exposes a contradiction in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to follow the logical structure of the argument without over-analysing.
The passage is a single continuous argument, not a list.
2
Identify the key logical move
Notice that the proof points out that the will did not necessitate creation before a specific time, so there must be a property that makes that time special. This property is the crucial link.
3
Understand the conclusion and opponent's denial
The argument concludes that this property requires times to be pre-eternal. The opponent rejects this, revealing an inconsistency in their view. Ask yourself why the opponent would resist this conclusion.
The common misunderstanding clarifies that this is a logical implication, not an assertion of reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: هي: أن تلك الإرادة، لما لم تقتض إحداث العالم فيما قبل ذلك، وإنما اقتضت إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت [المعين] فهذا يوجب أن يكون وقت الفعل متميزاً عن وقت الترك، بخاصية لأجلها اقتضت الإرادة إيقاع الفعل فيه لا في غيره، وذلك يقتضي قدم الأوقات. والخصم لا يقول به.
The fourth proof (al-ḥujja al-rābi‘a): it is that that will, since it did not necessitate the bringing about of the world before that time, but rather necessitated the bringing about of the world at that [specific] time, this necessitates that the time of the act is distinguished from the time of non-action by a property (khāṣṣiyya) on account of which the will necessitated the occurrence of the act in it and not in another, and that necessitates the pre-eternity (qidam) of times. And the opponent does not affirm that.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-ḥujja al-rābi‘a). It argues that because God's will did not necessitate creation before that specific time but only at that time, there must be a property (khāṣṣiyya) that distinguishes the time of action from the time of inaction. This property, which accounts for why the will chose that time, must be something inherent in the time itself. But if temporal durations have such distinguishing properties, then these properties must have existed eternally, implying that the times themselves are pre-eternal (qadīma). The opponent does not accept this, so the argument shows a contradiction in their position.

خاصية (khāṣṣiyya)
وقت الفعل (waqt al-fi‘l)
وقت الترك (waqt al-tark)
قدم الأوقات (qidam al-awqāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument asserts that times are actually pre-eternal.
Correction: The argument does not assert that times are pre-eternal; rather, it claims that the opponent's position logically entails the pre-eternity of times, which the opponent denies.

A) The temporality of the world.
B) The pre-eternity of times.
C) The possibility of multiple worlds.
D) The freedom of God's will.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that the opponent's view that God's will chose a specific time for creation logically implies that times are pre-eternal, a position they reject.

?
The opponent in this proof rejects a logical consequence of their own premise. Are there areas in your life where you hold contradictory beliefs because accepting the full truth would be uncomfortable? What would it cost you to be fully consistent?

Fifth Proof: Similarity or Difference of Times
Fifth argument: if times are similar, the will must relate equally; if different, regress follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth proof uses a dichotomy to challenge the claim that the divine will attached to a specific time for creation.

Core Idea
Either time parts are equal or different; both options refute a specific creation time.

The fifth proof presents a clever dilemma. If the chosen time is like all others, the will should relate equally to all times, making a specific attachment impossible. If each time is unique, then time becomes an endless succession of events, implying an infinite regress. This forces the opponent into a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's dichotomy
Identify the two horns of the argument: either times are equal in essence or they are not.
2
Understand the first horn
If times are equal, then by the principle that similar things have similar rulings, the will must relate equally to all times. This makes a specific attachment arbitrary.
3
Understand the second horn
If each time is different, then time is just a sequence of distinct events, each preceded by another, leading to an infinite series.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both outcomes show that the opponent's claim that the will necessarily attached to a particular time is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: أن نقول: إن ذلك الوقت، إما أن يكون مساوياً لسائر الأوقات في تمام الماهية، أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول كانت نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى ذلك الوقت، كنسبتها إلى سائر الأوقات، ضرورة [أن] المتماثلات يجب استواؤها في جميع الأحكام، وذلك يبطل القول بأن تعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت واجب، وأن تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ممتنع: كلام باطل. وإن كان الثاني، وهو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الزمان، فإنه مخالف بالماهية للجزء الآخر، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مرور الوقت عبارة عن تعاقب تلك الحوادث المختلفة، وتوالي تلك الحقائق المتلاصقة، وهذا عين القول بأن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر، لا إلى أول.
The fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisa): we say: that time is either equal to all other times in the entirety of essence (al-māhiyya), or it is not. If the first, then the relation of that will to that time is like its relation to all other times, necessarily [because] similar things must be equal in all rulings (aḥkām), and that invalidates the statement that the attachment (ta‘alluq) of the will to bringing about the world at that time is necessary (wājib), and that its attachment to bringing about the world at other than that time is impossible (mumtani‘): that speech is false. If the second, namely that every part of the parts of time is different in essence (mukhālif bi al-māhiyya) from the other part, then it follows that the passage of time is an expression of the succession (ta‘āqub) of those different temporal events, and the sequence (tawālī) of those contiguous realities, and this is exactly the statement that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event, without a first.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisa). It uses a dichotomy: either the specific time is equal in essence (māhiyya) to all other times, or it is different. If equal, then by the principle that similar things must receive equal judgments (aḥkām), the will's relation to that time should be the same as to all others. Therefore, it cannot be that the will necessarily attaches to that one time and is impossible for others—that is contradictory. If the second horn, that each temporal part differs in essence from every other, then the passage of time becomes a succession of distinct events, each preceding another without a first. This implies an infinite series of temporal events, which is a position the opponent may not accept. Thus both options undermine the opponent's claim that creation occurred at a specific time by a specific will.

Key Terms
الحجة الخامسة
تمام الماهية
متماثلات
استواء في الأحكام
تعلق الإرادة
حادث مسبوق بحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth proof asserts that no time is distinct from another.
Correction: The proof does not deny distinctness; it uses it as a premise in the second horn to show that if each time is distinct, an infinite regress follows.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, if the specific time is equal in essence to other times, what follows about the will's attachment?
A) It must be specific to that time.
B) It must be equal to all other times.
C) It becomes impossible.
D) It necessitates a different essence.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that claiming a specific time for creation leads to contradiction whether or not times are essentially similar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the 'time' of your own existence, do you ever assume that your birth at this particular moment in history is a result of mere chance? How does this argument's first horn—that if all times are equal, God's will would have no reason to single out one—challenge the notion that your life is a random accident? What does it imply about the intentionality of God's choice?
?
The second horn of the argument shows that if every moment is different in essence, then time becomes an infinite chain of distinct events. Reflect on a specific area of your life where you feel stuck in an endless cycle of 'one thing after another'—perhaps in habits, worries, or desires. How does this argument's exposure of the problem of infinite regress push you to seek an anchoring starting point? What would it mean for you to find that 'first moment' of repentance or purpose?
?
The principle that 'similar things require similar judgments' is used here to argue that God's will must treat identical times equally. How does this principle apply to your own judgments of people and situations? Are you consistent in how you treat similar circumstances, or do you allow arbitrary preferences? How does seeing God's perfect consistency challenge you to examine your own justice?
