The Sixth Proof: Pre-Eternal Resolution
Sanusi introduces the sixth proof, asserting that the claim of an eternal will to create implies a pre-eternal resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that God willed creation in eternity implies a pre-eternal resolution to create, and why this is significant for kalām arguments about God's will.

Core Idea
The opponent's statement about God's will secretly assumes a pre-eternal resolution.

After establishing arguments about divine power and will, the author now presents a sixth proof that exposes a hidden implication in the opponent's position. This proof demonstrates that claiming God willed creation in eternity at a specific future time necessarily entails that God had a settled resolution from eternity to bring about existence. Understanding this implication is crucial for evaluating the coherence of the opponent's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the key terms: al-ḥujja al-sādisa, al-azal, mā lā yazāl, ‘azama ‘alā al-ījād.
The phrase 'mā lā yazāl' means 'the future' here.
2
Identify the logical move
The passage says 'our statement implies that...' It is drawing out a consequence. Ask: What is the statement? What does it imply?
3
Connect to previous arguments
Recall that previous proofs argued that God's will must be eternal. This proof shows that the opponent's own claim leads to a pre-eternal resolution, which may conflict with other attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is showing a pre-eternal resolution important? It ties God's will to a definite decision, which then impacts discussions of temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: إنا سنبين بالوجوه الكثيرة: أن قولنا: إنه تعالى أراد في الأزل أن يحدث العالم فيما لا يزال في الوقت المعين، يفيد أنه تعالى كان في الأزل، قد عزم على الإيجاد فيما لا يزال.
The sixth proof (al-ḥujja al-sādisa): we will show by many aspects that our statement: 'He Most High willed in eternity (al-azal) to bring about the world in the future (mā lā yazāl) at the specific time' implies that He Most High in eternity had resolved (‘azama) upon bringing into existence (ījād) in the future.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth proof (al-ḥujja al-sādisa). It is a brief announcement that the argument will show, through multiple aspects, that the opponent's claim—that God willed in eternity to create the world at a specific future time—implies that God had a resolution (‘azm) in eternity to bring about existence in the future. The proof is not completed in the scanned text, but the point is to draw out the implications of such a resolution for God's attributes and the nature of creation. The Arabic phrase 'mā lā yazāl' (what never ceases) refers to the future temporal sequence.

Key Terms
الحجة السادسة
الأزل
ما لا يزال
عزم على الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'mā lā yazāl' means 'that which does not cease to exist'.
Correction: In this context, 'mā lā yazāl' means 'the future' or 'what never ceases' as an ongoing temporal sequence, not a specific eternal entity.

Checkpoint

A) God changed His will.
B) God had a resolution in eternity.
C) The world must be created in eternity.
D) The specific time is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that God willed creation in eternity at a specific future time necessarily implies that God had a pre-eternal resolution to create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's Fourth Argument: Specified Time Implies Pre-Eternal Times
Razi presents the fourth argument against the opponent's first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth argument against the opponent's claim that God's will specified creation's time without a cause, and how it leads to the implication of pre-eternal times.

Core Idea
A specific time for creation implies pre-eternal times.

This lesson presents Razi's fourth argument, which targets the opponent's attempt to explain why God created the world at a particular moment rather than earlier. Razi shows that if the will specifies a time, that time must have a distinguishing property, which would be pre-eternal—a conclusion the opponent rejects. This reveals an inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the key terms: will, time of action, time of non-action, pre-eternity of times.
The Arabic uses concise logical connectors; follow how the argument unfolds.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into three parts: premise (will did not necessitate before, only at that time), implication (time of action is distinct from non-action by a property), and conclusion (that property necessitates pre-eternity of times).
The opponent's denial is highlighted at the end.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Consider why the opponent's position is contradictory: they admit a specific time but deny pre-eternal times. Razi's argument forces them to accept the latter.
This argument shows the strength of Razi's dialectical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : هي : أن تلك الإرادة، لما لم تقتض إحداث العالم فيما قبل ذلك، وإنما اقتضت إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت [المعين] فهذا يوجب أن يكون وقت الفعل متميزاً عن وقت الترك، بخاصية لأجلها اقتضت الإرادة إيقاع الفعل فيه لا في غيره، وذلك يقتضي قدم الأوقات. والخصم لا يقول به.
The fourth argument: it is that that will, since it did not necessitate bringing about the world before that time, but rather necessitated bringing about the world in that specific time, this necessitates that the time of the action be distinguished from the time of non-action, by a property for which the will necessitated performing the action in it and not in another, and that necessitates the pre-eternity of times. But the opponent does not say this.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the opponent's first answer (which claimed that God's will specified a particular time for creation without a cause). The argument states: the will (al-irāda) did not necessitate creation before that specific time, only at that time. This implies that the time of action (waqt al-fi‘l) is distinct from the time of non-action (waqt at-turk) by some property that made the will choose it. That property would have to be pre-eternal, leading to the conclusion that times are pre-eternal (qidam al-awqāt), which the opponent rejects. Thus, the opponent's position is contradictory.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
وقت الفعل (waqt al-fi‘l)
وقت الترك (waqt at-turk)
قدم الأوقات (qidam al-awqāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is about God's knowledge of time rather than the implication of a specified time requiring pre-eternal times.
Correction: The argument focuses on the will's specification of a time and the logical necessity of that time having a distinguishing property that would be pre-eternal, not on God's knowledge.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth argument shows that specifying a time for creation forces the opponent to accept the pre-eternity of times, which they deny, revealing a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the property that distinguishes the time of creation from all others. Do you think such a property exists in God's knowledge or outside of it? If it is outside God, does that not make it eternal alongside Him? How does that sit with your understanding of tawhid?
?
Razi's challenge is not just academic—it confronts your belief about God's relationship to time. If you cannot answer it, does that mean your belief is irrational? Or is there a way forward that preserves both divine sovereignty and logic? What will you do with this tension?

The Fifth Argument: Equality or Difference of Times
Razi presents the fifth argument as a dichotomy between equal and different times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's fifth argument uses a logical dichotomy to refute the claim that a specific time is necessary for creation.

Core Idea
Whether times are equal or different, the opponent's position collapses.

This passage presents Razi's fifth argument, a clever logical dilemma. The opponent asserts that a particular time is uniquely suited for the act of creation. Razi forces a choice: either that time is essentially equal to all other times, or it is different in essence. Both options lead to consequences that contradict the opponent's original claim. Let's follow the reasoning to see how the trap works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing deeply. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the clear 'either-or' structure at the beginning.
The passage uses the phrase 'إما...أو' to set up the dichotomy.
2
Examine the first branch: equality of times
Consider the first possibility: the specific time is equal in essence (māhiyya) to all other times. Since identical things must be treated identically in all rulings, the will must relate equally to all times. This contradicts the opponent's claim that the will necessarily attaches to that specific time.
3
Examine the second branch: difference in essence
Now consider the second possibility: each time has a unique essence. If so, the passage of time becomes a succession of distinct events and realities. This implies that every event is preceded by another event, leading to an infinite regress with no first event. This is impossible according to the opponent's cosmology.
Note the term 'تعاقب' (succession) and 'لا إلى أول' (without a first).
4
Connect the branches to the refutation
Both branches lead to conclusions that the opponent cannot accept. The first makes the necessary attachment of will to that time impossible. The second imposes an infinite chain of events. Thus the original claim is refuted from both sides.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : إن ذلك الوقت، إما أن يكون مساوياً لسائر الأوقات في تمام الماهية، أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول كانت نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى ذلك الوقت، كنسبتها إلى سائر الأوقات، ضرورة أن المتماثلات يجب استواؤها في جميع الأحكام، وذلك يبطل القول بأن تعلق الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت واجب، وأن تعلقها بإحداث العالم في غير ذلك الوقت ممتنع: كلام باطل. وإن كان الثاني، وهو أن كل جزء من أجزاء الزمان، فإنه مخالف بالماهية للجزء الآخر، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مرور الوقت عبارة عن تعاقب تلك الحوادث المختلفة، وتوالي تلك الحقائق المتلاصقة، وهذا عين القول بأن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر، لا إلى أول.
The fifth argument: we say: that time, either it is equal to other times in complete essence, or it is not. If it is the first, then the relation of that will to that time is like its relation to other times, necessarily because identicals must be equal in all rulings, and that invalidates the statement that the attachment of the will to bringing about the world at that time is necessary, and that its attachment to bringing about the world at any other time is impossible: it is false speech. And if it is the second, namely that every part of time is different in essence from another part, then it follows that the passage of time is nothing but the succession of those different events and the sequence of those contiguous realities, and this is exactly the statement that every event is preceded by another event, without a first.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, structured as a dichotomy. Either the specific time is equal in essence (māhiyya) to all other times, or it differs. If equal, then the will must relate equally to all times, so the claim that the will necessarily attaches to that time and cannot attach to others is false. If each time has a unique essence, then the passage of time (murūr al-waqt) is just the succession of different events and realities, which implies that every event is preceded by another ad infinitum (lā ilā awwal), i.e., an infinite regress of events. Both options refute the opponent's position.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya)
متماثلات (mutamāthilāt)
تعاقب (ta‘āqub)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if the opponent admits times are equal.
Correction: The argument covers both possibilities; if the opponent denies equality, the difference option leads to infinite regress, which is also unacceptable.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, if each part of time differs in essence from another, what is the consequence?
A) The will attaches equally to all times
B) The passage of time would be the succession of events without a first
C) The world must be created in all times
D) The specific time must be the best

Key Takeaway
Razi's fifth argument uses a two-pronged logical trap to show that the claim of a uniquely necessary time for creation is untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that a particular time or season of your life has some special significance that makes it more 'likely' for Allah to act in your favor? How does Razi's first horn—that all times are equal before Allah's will—force you to reconsider your attachment to certain moments or milestones? What specific time are you clinging to that you need to release into Allah's absolute sovereignty?
?
If every moment is simply a new event created by Allah, with no inherent essence of its own, then your past, present, and future are all equally dependent on His continuous creation. Does your daily prayer reflect this truth, or do you live as if some moments are 'yours' and others are 'His'? What would change if you truly believed that each second is a fresh act of Allah?
?
Razi's argument demolishes the idea that any time can be 'necessary' for Allah's action. Are there areas in your life where you have insisted that Allah must work on a certain timeline? How does this argument expose the sin of dictating conditions to the Creator? Will you repent from this and submit to His will without imposing your own temporal preferences?

The Sixth Argument: Distinguishing Resolve from Intention
Razi outlines sub-arguments distinguishing resolve from intention to refute the first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth argument that God's pre-eternal resolve to create is distinct from the intention required for actual creation, and why resolve alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Resolve looks forward; intention brings forth.

Razi now introduces a subtle but powerful argument. The opponent claims that God's eternal resolve suffices for creation at the appointed time. Razi argues that resolve and intention are fundamentally different in essence, and that creation requires a new intention at the moment of creation. This passage builds the case step by step, promising to show several key points that together refute the first answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Note that Razi is promising a series of sub-arguments, not giving them in full detail yet. He states the overall plan and conclusion.
The passage is a roadmap for the sixth argument.
2
Identify the five sub-arguments
Razi lists five points he will prove: (1) God's eternal will is a resolve, (2) creation requires intention, (3) resolve and intention have different essences, (4) resolve cannot become intention, (5) a new intention must occur. Find each one in the text.
The Arabic uses 'وسنبين أيضا' to introduce each.
3
Understand the distinction between عزم and قصد
The core of the argument is that عزم (resolve) is a firm decision to act in the future, while قصد (intention) is the direct orientation toward an act. They are not interchangeable.
This distinction is crucial for the refutation.
4
See how this refutes the first answer
The opponent's first answer claimed that God's eternal resolve suffices. Razi argues that resolve alone cannot produce an act; a fresh intention is needed. Thus the first answer fails.
The conclusion: 'فساد قول من يقول إن ذلك العزم يكفي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنا سنبين بالوجوه الكثيرة : أن قولنا : إنه تعالى أراد في الأزل أن يحدث العالم فيما لا يزال في الوقت المعين، يفيد أنه تعالى كان في الأزل، قد عزم على الإيجاد فيما لا يزال. وسنبين أيضا : أن الإيجاد والتكوين لا يمكن إلا بالقصد إلى الإيجاد، ونبين أن ماهية العزم مخالفة لماهية القصد، ونبين أن العزم على الفعل، يمتنع أن ينقلب بعينه قصداً إلى الفعل، وأنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأنه يبطل ذلك وأنه يحدث القصد. وعند هذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : إن ذلك العزم، العزم يكفي في إحداث العالم في الوقت المعين. فهذه جملة الدلائل المذكورة على إبطال الجواب الأول.
The sixth argument: we will show by many aspects that our statement that He, exalted, willed in pre-eternity to bring about the world in the future in the specific time means that He, exalted, was in pre-eternity having resolved (عزم) to bring into existence in the future. And we will also show that bringing into existence and creation are not possible except by intention (قصد) to bring into existence, and we will show that the essence of resolve (عزم) is different from the essence of intention (قصد), and we will show that resolve to do an action cannot transform itself into intention to do the action, and that it is necessary to admit that that fails and that intention occurs. And at this point the corruption of the statement of those who say that that resolve, resolve suffices for bringing about the world in the specific time becomes clear. So this is a summary of the arguments mentioned for invalidating the first answer.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, which is a complex refutation of the opponent's claim that God's eternal resolve (عزم) suffices to create the world at a specific time without a new intention. Razi promises to show: (1) that God's will in eternity is a resolve (عزم) to create in the future, not an intention (قصد) that directly creates; (2) that creation requires an intention (قصد) to create; (3) that the essence (māhiyya) of resolve is different from that of intention; (4) that resolve cannot turn into intention; and (5) that therefore a new intention must occur at the time of creation. This refutes the first answer. The passage concludes by calling all previous arguments a summary of proofs against the first answer.

Key Terms
عزم (عزم)
قصد (qasd)
إيجاد (ījād)
تكوين (takwīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resolve and intention are synonymous or that resolve alone can bring about an action.
Correction: Resolve is a firm decision to act in the future; intention is the direct orientation toward the act at the moment of action. They are distinct essences, and creation requires intention, not just resolve.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, why does God's eternal resolve not suffice to create the world at a specific time?
A) Because resolve is eternal and cannot be temporally specified
B) Because creation requires intention (قصد), and resolve cannot transform into intention
C) Because God must create at all times
D) Because resolve is a kind of intention

Key Takeaway
God's eternal resolve to create does not itself create; a distinct intention is required at the time of creation, so resolve alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I intend to do good' but then delay. Is your 'intention' just a vague resolve that never becomes a specific commitment? What one action will you set a precise time for today so that your resolve transforms into a real intention?
?
Consider a habit you keep meaning to break. Are you hiding behind resolve—telling yourself 'I want to stop'—without forming the intention to act at the next moment of temptation? When the test comes, will you have the distinct intention to resist?
?
Razi proves that even God must have a distinct intention at the moment of action. You are not God—you are prone to neglect. Right now, what act of worship or duty have you left to 'someday'? Write down the exact time and place you will deliberately intend to do it. Not a resolution—a plan.

The First Argument: The Time's Existence in Pre-Eternity
First argument of Razi's refutation of the optimal time answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument against the optimal time answer, which exposes a dilemma about whether the beneficial time existed in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If the beneficial time was pre-eternal, creation should have been pre-eternal.

Razi now tackles the second answer given by opponents: that God chose the specific time because it was most beneficial for humanity. He calls this answer extremely weak and offers two arguments. The first argument focuses on whether that beneficial time existed in pre-eternity or not, leading to a contradiction either way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two branches of the dichotomy: either the beneficial time was pre-eternal or it was not.
Pay attention to the logical connective 'either...or...' (هل...أو).
2
Analyze the first branch
If the time was pre-eternal, then creation should have occurred pre-eternally, which the opponent denies. Understand why this is a problem.
The opponent cannot accept pre-eternal creation, so this branch is impossible.
3
Analyze the second branch
If the time was not pre-eternal, then the opponent must claim that a necessary condition for God's action was absent in pre-eternity. But Razi says this has already been disproven.
This connects to previous arguments about the impossibility of temporal conditions for God's action.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both branches lead to falsehood, so the second answer is refuted. The time cannot be the reason for creation's timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثاني : وهو قولهم : إنما اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، لأجل أن إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت أصلح وأنفع للمكلفين فنقول : هذا الجواب أيضاً في غاية الضعف. والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن الوقت المشتمل على المصلحة الراجحة . هل كان حاصلا في الأزل، أو ما كان حاصلا. فإن كان حاصلا، لزم حصول الفعل في الأزل. وإن قلنا : إنه لو كان حاصلا في الأزل، مع أن ذلك الوقت أحد الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، فحينئذ يكون هذا قولاً بأن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً في الفعل، وموجداً له : ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. لكنا بينا بالدليل أن ذلك باطل. الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : هب أن ذلك الوقت، اختص بهذه الزيادة من المصلحة. إلا أنا نقول : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الزيادة من المصلحة، هل يقتضي وجوب تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت، أو لا يقتضي وجوب هذا التخصيص ؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون إله العالم موجباً بالذات لا فاعلاً.
As for the second answer: it is their statement that the world's occurrence was only specified to that specific time because bringing about the world at that time is more beneficial and more advantageous for the morally responsible beings. We say: this answer is also extremely weak. What indicates that are arguments: The first argument: Whether the time that contains the preponderant benefit was present in pre-eternity or was not present. If it was present, then the occurrence of the action in pre-eternity would be necessary. And if we say that it was present in pre-eternity, while that time is one of the things considered in effectivity, then this would be a statement that everything necessary for Him, exalted, to be an effecter of the action and a producer of it was present in pre-eternity. But we have shown by proof that that is false. The second argument: We say: Suppose that time is specified with this increase of benefit. But we say: Does the specification of that time with that increase of benefit necessitate the specification of bringing about the world at that time, or does it not necessitate this specification? If the first, then at that point the God of the world would be a necessitated being (mūjib bi-dhāt) not a voluntary agent (fā‘il).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponent's second answer, which claimed that the specific time of creation was chosen because it was most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) for humanity. Razi calls this extremely weak and gives two arguments. The first argument (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) poses a dichotomy: either the time with the beneficial quality existed in pre-eternity (azal) or not. If it existed pre-eternally, then creation should have occurred pre-eternally. But the opponent denies this. If they claim the time existed pre-eternally but other necessary conditions were not present, then this implies that all necessary conditions for God to act were not present in pre-eternity, which Razi says has already been disproven. The second argument (al-ḥujja ath-thāniya) states: even if we grant that the time has beneficial properties, does this benefit necessitate that creation occur at that specific time? If yes, then God would be a necessitated agent (mūjib bi-dhāt) rather than a free voluntary agent (fā‘il), which is unacceptable. Thus, the second answer fails.

Key Terms
مصلحة (maṣlaḥa)
أزل (azal)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first branch of the dichotomy in Razi's first argument?
A) The beneficial time was pre-eternal
B) The beneficial time was not pre-eternal
C) The beneficial time was created
D) The beneficial time was multiple

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument shows that if the beneficial time existed pre-eternally, creation would be pre-eternal, contradicting the opponent's view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's actions must align with your understanding of benefit and timing? How does Razi's argument challenge you to trust God's wisdom even when His choices seem arbitrary or delayed in your life? What practical step can you take today to surrender a specific expectation you have of God?
?
If God's will is not bound by external conditions like time or perceived benefit, then your prayers and efforts do not 'cause' God to act. Does this truth liberate you from trying to manipulate God, or does it unsettle your sense of control? How can you deepen your reliance on His perfect will rather than on your own attempts to engineer favorable outcomes?
?
Razi shows that positing a pre-eternal reason for creation's timing leads to absurdity. Reflect on a time when you insisted on knowing 'why' God allowed a trial. How can this lesson teach you to rest in the mystery of His decree, trusting that His wisdom is greater than your need for explanations? What would change in your daily habits if you truly believed that God's actions are not contingent on what seems beneficial to you?

The Second Argument: Benefit Leads to Necessitation
Second argument of Razi's refutation of the optimal time answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that if benefit necessitates the time, then God would be a necessitated agent rather than a free voluntary agent.

Core Idea
If benefit forces the timing, God is not free.

Razi continues his refutation of the optimal time answer with a second argument. Even if we grant that the specific time has an increase in benefit, we must ask: does this benefit necessitate that creation occurs at that exact time? If yes, then God's action becomes necessary, contradicting His status as a voluntary agent (fā‘il).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the second argument. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if...then...' (هل يقتضي...فإن كان الأول).
2
Identify the key question
The argument asks whether the increase in benefit necessitates the specification of creation at that time.
This is a pivotal question because it touches on divine freedom.
3
Understand the two possible answers
If yes (benefit necessitates), then God would be a necessitated agent (mūjib bi-dhāt), not a voluntary agent. If no, then benefit is not the reason.
The opponent cannot accept that God is a necessitated agent, so the answer fails.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi effectively shows that the appeal to benefit either fails to justify the specific time or undermines divine freedom.

?

?

The Fourth Argument: Refutation by Dependency
Sanusi points out a fatal dependency on a refuted principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument refutes the opponent's answer by exposing its reliance on a previously refuted principle about divine benefit.

Core Idea
The opponent's answer collapses because it rests on a false foundation.

This lesson presents the fourth and final argument against the opponent's answer regarding the timing of divine acts. The argument does not engage with details but instead exposes a fatal flaw: the opponent's reasoning depends on the principle that God only acts for the benefit of His creatures. Since this principle was thoroughly refuted earlier, the entire answer is invalid. This shows the importance of foundational principles in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice its structure: it first names the argument, then states the opponent's presupposition, then references a prior refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's presupposition
What does the opponent's answer assume about God's actions? Write it down.
The text says: 'He does not perform actions except in accordance with the interests of the servants.'
3
Understand the refutation
Why does pointing out this presupposition refute the answer? Because if the presupposition is false, the answer built on it is invalid.
The argument does not need to discuss the timing anymore; it attacks the foundation.
4
Connect to prior content
The passage says the refutation of this principle 'has already preceded.' This means the argument is not self-contained; it relies on earlier discussion. For this lesson, we just need to understand the logical move.
Refer to earlier lessons for the full refutation of the principle of divine benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في إبطال هذا الجواب : أن نقول : هذا الكلام بناءً على أنه تعالى لا يفعل الأفعال إلا على وفق مصالح العباد ، وقد سبق الكلام المستقصى في إبطال هذا الأصل
The fourth argument in refuting this answer: We say: This statement is based on the fact that He, exalted, does not perform actions except in accordance with the interests of the servants, and the comprehensive discussion in refuting this principle has already preceded.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a short refutation (the fourth argument against the opponent's response). It points out that the opponent's answer (that the time's benefit is the preponderating factor) depends on the theological principle that God only acts for the benefit of His creatures. The author states that this principle has already been thoroughly refuted earlier in the work. Therefore, the opponent's answer is invalid because it rests on a false premise. This is a meta-argument that does not engage with the specifics but rather attacks the foundation.

Key Terms
مصالح العباد
أصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument itself proves that God does not act for benefit.
Correction: It does not; it merely points out that the opponent's answer relies on that principle, which was already refuted elsewhere. The argument does not make a positive claim about God's actions.

Checkpoint
What principle does the opponent's answer rely on according to this argument?
A) God's omnipotence.
B) God's knowledge.
C) God only acts for the benefit of His servants.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument refutes the opponent's answer by showing it depends on a principle that has already been proven false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent's argument collapsed because it relied on a false foundation. Are there any areas of your faith or practice where you might be leaning on a principle that you haven't verified from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What step will you take this week to examine that foundation?
?
You are accountable for what you believe about Allah's actions. Are you content to simply inherit beliefs, or will you actively seek to know the sound proofs? What is one principle you will investigate more deeply starting today?

The Insignificance of Temporal Differences
A concessive argument showing temporal insignificance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against the claim that God's timing of creation is based on benefits, and why a minuscule temporal change cannot affect the interests of morally responsible beings.

Core Idea
A tiny change in creation's timing changes nothing for God's servants.

This lesson examines a powerful objection: even if God considers benefits, the precise moment of creation cannot matter because a minuscule shift would leave all benefits unchanged. The argument relies on intuitive necessity and the fact that God does not inform us of such subtle differences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This is the fifth argument refuting the opponent's claim that a specific time was chosen for creation due to benefits. The argument is concessive: it grants the opponent's premise and then shows it fails.
Remember that the opponent had tried to justify God's specific timing by reference to benefits.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the concessive structure: 'Suppose that He does consider benefits, yet...'
The phrase 'it is known by necessity' indicates a self-evident truth.
3
Identify the key claim
The argument asserts that advancing or delaying creation by a tiny fraction (a thousandth of a blink) would not affect the interests of the legally responsible beings at all.
This is presented as necessarily true.
4
Grasp the reinforcing point
The argument is strengthened by noting that God does not even inform people of such a tiny difference, so it cannot influence their benefits.
This closes the logical gap further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في إبطاله : أن نقول : هب أنه تعالى يراعي المصالح ، إلا أن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه تعالى لو قدم خلق العالم على الوقت الذي خلقه فيه بجزء من ألف جزء من لمحة واحدة ، أو أخر خلقه عن ذلك الوقت بهذا القدر من الوقت ، فإنه لا يتفاوت شيء من مصالح المكلفين البتة ، لا سيما إذا قلنا : إن الله تعالى لا يطلعهم على هذا القدر من التفاوت ، ولا يوقفهم عليه.
The fifth argument in refuting it: We say: Suppose that He, exalted, does consider the interests, yet it is known by necessity that if He, exalted, had advanced the creation of the world from the time in which He created it by a part of a thousand parts of a single blink, or delayed its creation from that time by this amount of time, then nothing of the interests of the legally responsible beings would differ at all, especially if we say that God, exalted, does not inform them of this amount of difference, nor does He make them aware of it.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is the fifth argument (الحجة الخامسة) refuting the opponent's answer. It presents a concessive argument: even if we grant that God does consider the interests of His servants (which was already refuted), it is still impossible that a minuscule temporal difference (a part of a thousand of a blink) in the creation of the world would affect the interests of the people. This is presented as a matter of immediate knowledge (بالضرورة). The argument is strengthened by noting that God does not even let people know about such a tiny difference, so it would not influence their benefits. Therefore, the opponent's claim that a specific time was chosen for some benefit is undermined because such a tiny shift would not change anything.

Key Terms
مصالح المكلفين (interests of the legally responsible)
بالضرورة (by necessity)
تفاوت (difference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God considers benefits at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God considers benefits; it is concessive, showing that even if He does, the specific timing cannot be based on benefits because a tiny change would not affect them.

Checkpoint
What is the effect on the interests of the servants if God created the world a tiny fraction of a blink earlier or later?
A) They would be completely different.
B) There would be no difference at all.
C) The servants would be punished.
D) The world would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Even if God considers benefits, a minuscule temporal difference in creation would not affect the interests of His servants, so the specific timing cannot be justified by benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience impatience about the timing of events in your life, how does this passage’s assertion that minuscule time changes don’t affect true interests challenge your anxiety? What would it cost you to truly believe that?
?
The passage mentions that God does not inform us of such tiny temporal differences. Are you comfortable with not knowing whether a delay or advance in your life would have been better? What does that comfort (or discomfort) reveal about your trust in God’s silent wisdom?
?
If the interests of the legally responsible beings are unaffected by infinitesimal timing shifts, then what are the things that truly affect their interests? How can you redirect your focus from timing to those deeper concerns, starting today?

The Refutation by Reliance on a Refuted Principle
Refutes a theological answer by revealing its reliance on a refuted principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument refutes a theological answer by exposing its dependence on an already-refuted principle.

Core Idea
An argument stands on its foundation; if the foundation is refuted, the argument collapses.

The author continues his refutation of the answer by presenting a fourth argument. This argument does not introduce new evidence but instead reveals a fatal flaw: the answer is built on a principle that has already been shown to be false. Understanding this move helps you see how theological debates often hinge on foundational premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author states the argument and then immediately refers to an earlier refutation.
The passage is short but contains a complete logical move.
2
Identify the premise of the answer
The answer assumes that God only acts in accordance with people's interests. Identify this as the key premise that the argument targets.
3
Recall the earlier refutation
The author says a full discussion refuting this principle has already preceded. Think about why this principle was rejected earlier.
You may need to review previous lessons to fully appreciate this.
4
Understand the refutational structure
The fourth argument works by showing that the answer depends on a premise that is already invalid. Thus the answer itself is unsound.
This is a common dialectical strategy: refute an opponent's argument by showing it relies on a false premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في إبطال هذا الجواب : أن نقول : هذا الكلام بناءً على أنه تعالى لا يفعل الأفعال [ إلا ] على وفق مصالح العباد ، وقد سبق الكلام المستقصى في إبطال هذا الأصل
The fourth argument in refuting this answer: that we say: this speech is based on the premise that He, exalted, does not perform actions except in accordance with the interests of the servants, and a full discussion has already preceded in refuting this principle.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This is the fourth argument against the answer. The author points out that the entire answer depends on the principle that God only acts for the benefit of His creatures. However, this principle has already been thoroughly refuted earlier in the work. Thus the answer collapses because its foundation is invalid.

Sixth Argument: All Benefits Reduce to Pleasure and Pain
Enumerates the sixth argument reducing benefits to pleasure/pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani reduces all possible benefits to pleasure and pain, and why this undermines the argument that creation time is justified by benefit.

Core Idea
All benefits are just pleasure or pain; God gives these anytime.

In this passage, al-Shahrastani presents his sixth argument against the opponent's claim that the specific time of creation was chosen due to some benefit. He argues that all benefits can be reduced to the experience of pleasure or the removal of pain. Since God can grant these regardless of when the world was created, the time of creation is irrelevant to any real benefit. This argument aims to expose the weakness of the opponent's answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a concise logical argument with premises and conclusion.
The argument is part of a larger discussion; focus on its internal logic.
2
Identify the key reduction
Identify the claim that all benefits are reducible to either pleasure/joy or removal of pain/sorrow. Ask yourself if this reduction is plausible.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow how the reduction leads to the conclusion that the time of creation does not affect benefits, thus the opponent's answer is weak.
The final sentence states the weakness of that answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن كل مصلحة يمكن عودها إلى واحد من المكلفين ، فتلك المصلحة لا معنى لها إلا إيصال نفع في الدنيا أو في الآخرة إليه ، ولا معنى للنفع إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو دفع الألم والغم ، وكل ذلك فإنه تعالى قادر على إيصاله إلى المكلف ، سواء خلق [ العالم ] في ذلك الوقت ، أو خلقه في وقت آخر. فيثبت : أنه لا تختلف أحوال الخلق في المصالح والمفاسد ، بسبب حدوث العالم في الوقت المعين، أو عدم حدوثه في ذلك الوقت . فقد ظهر بهذه البيانات : ضعف هذا الجواب . The sixth argument: every benefit that can return to one of the obligated beings has no meaning except the delivery of a benefit in this world or the Hereafter to him, and benefit has no meaning except pleasure and joy, or the removal of pain and sorrow. And all that, He, exalted, is able to deliver to the obligated being, whether He created the world in that time or created it in another time. Thus it is established that the conditions of creation do not differ in benefits and harms due to the occurrence of the world at the specific time or its non-occurrence at that time. So by these evidences the weakness of this answer has become manifest. [And God knows best.]

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument. The author reduces all possible benefits for creatures to the experience of pleasure or the removal of pain. Since God is omnipotent and can grant these outcomes regardless of when the world was created, the specific time of creation is irrelevant to the interests of the obligated beings. Therefore, the answer that justifies the specific time by some benefit is demonstrably weak.

God's Eternal Knowledge and the Time of Creation
Refutes the answer that God's eternal knowledge determined the specific time of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's eternal knowledge alone determined the specific time of creation leads to a logical dilemma.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge cannot explain creation's time without a contradiction.

The third answer to the problem of creation's timing appeals to God's eternal knowledge: He knew when the world would begin, so that time was inevitable. But this answer faces a devastating objection: what about the time itself? Was it present in eternity or not? Either way, the argument unravels. In this lesson, we walk through that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation without analysing. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the structure: statement of answer, then a single proof with a dilemma.
2
Identify the core claim and refutation
Find where the author states the third answer and then his first refutation. Mark the dilemma: 'was that time present in eternity or not?'
The refutation is a single logical proof (الوجه الأول).
3
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
Consider each option: if the time was present in eternity, the world would be eternal (false). If not, a necessary condition for creation was absent, so God's knowledge did not necessitate creation at that time.
This shows that the third answer fails to explain the timing.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Ask yourself: why does this matter for understanding God's attributes? What does it teach about the relationship between divine knowledge and creation?
The argument preserves the contingency of the world and avoids determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثالث : وهو قولهم : إنه تعالى إنما خص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت ، لأنه تعالى علم في الأزل أن العالم يحدث في ذلك الوقت ، وخلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فنقول : هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف . ويدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن نقول : إن على سياق كلامكم ، يكون حصول ذلك الوقت الذي علم الله تعالى ، أن العالم يحدث فيه أحد الأمور التي لا بد منها في حدوث العالم . فنقول : ذلك الوقت هل كان حاضراً في الأزل أو ما كان حاضراً في الأزل ؟ فإن كان الأول لزم حصول العالم في الأزل ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : إن حضور ذلك الوقت أحد الأمور المعتبرة في حدوث العالم ، وأنه ما كان حاضراً . فهذا يقتضي أن جملة الأمور المعتبرة في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم ، ما كانت حاضرة . فيثبت : أن حاصل هذا الجواب يرجع إلى هذا
As for the third answer: it is their saying: He, exalted, specified the bringing-into-existence of the world at that time only because He knew in eternity that the world would occur at that time, and opposing what God knows is impossible of occurrence. So we say: this answer is also weak. And there are proofs for it: the first proof: that we say: according to the context of your statement, the occurrence of that time which God knew that the world would occur in is one of the necessary things for the occurrence of the world. So we say: was that time present in eternity or was it not present in eternity? If the first, then the occurrence of the world in eternity would be entailed; and if the second, then we say: the presence of that time is one of the things considered in the occurrence of the world, and it was not present. So this requires that the totality of things considered in His being an originator of the world were not present. Thus it is established that the outcome of this answer reduces to this.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage introduces the third answer: that God's eternal knowledge determined the specific time, and since God's knowledge is infallible, it was inevitable. The author declares this answer weak and presents the first proof. He argues that the time itself is one of the conditions for the world's occurrence. He then poses a dilemma: was this time present in eternity? If yes, then the world would have existed eternally, which is false. If no, then one of the necessary conditions for creation was absent in eternity, contradicting the idea that God's knowledge necessitated creation at that time. Hence the answer collapses.

Key Terms
الجواب الثالث
علم في الأزل
خلاف معلوم الله
حاضر
الأمور المعتبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal knowledge alone is a sufficient explanation for why the world began at a specific time.
Correction: The passage shows that this leads to a dilemma about the time's presence, so the explanation is insufficient.

Checkpoint

A) God's knowledge is not eternal.
B) The specific time must be either present in eternity or not; both lead to problems.
C) God does not know future contingents.
D) The world could have been created at any time.

Key Takeaway
Appealing to God's eternal knowledge to explain creation's timing fails because it creates a dilemma about the temporal presence of that time itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Claim that Divine Knowledge Determines Creation's Timing
The author refutes the third answer by presenting three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments used to refute the claim that God's eternal knowledge determines the timing of creation, and why God's will is free and not necessitated by His knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge follows the known; it does not determine His will.

The opponents of the Ash'ari position argued that God created the world at a specific time because He eternally knew it would happen then, and opposing His knowledge is impossible. This passage presents three decisive arguments to refute that claim. As you read, pay attention to how each argument targets a different logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall structure: the author introduces the third answer and then gives three aspects (wujuh) to refute it.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every detail on first read.
2
Identify the three aspects
Look at the three 'al-wajh' sections. Each presents a distinct argument. Label them mentally: (1) the time problem, (2) circularity between will and knowledge, (3) the consequence of a necessary agent.
3
Analyze the first argument
The first argument asks whether the time was present in pre-eternity. If yes, the world would be eternal; if no, the conditions for creation were absent. Notice that this reduces the opponent's answer to a claim the author says he already refuted.
Think about why this argument is effective.
4
Analyze the second argument
The second argument points out that explaining will by knowledge leads to circularity (dawr). Knowledge follows the known; if the known (the specific time) depends on will, then will cannot be caused by knowledge.
This is a logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning.
5
Analyze the third argument
The third argument shows that if knowledge makes the will incapable of choosing other times, then God becomes a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat) rather than a voluntary agent. That would imply the world is eternal, which the opponents reject.
Consider how this argument defends divine free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثالث : وهو قولهم : إنه تعالى إنما خص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت ، لأنه تعالى علم في الأزل أن العالم يحدث في ذلك الوقت ، وخلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فنقول : هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف . ويدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن نقول : إن على سياق كلامكم ، يكون حصول ذلك الوقت الذي علم الله تعالى ، أن العالم يحدث فيه أحد الأمور التي لا بد منها في حدوث العالم . فنقول : ذلك الوقت هل كان حاضراً في الأزل أو ما كان حاضراً في الأزل ؟ فإن كان الأول لزم حصول العالم في الأزل ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : إن حضور ذلك الوقت أحد الأمور المعتبرة في حدوث العالم ، وأنه ما كان حاضراً . فهذا يقتضي أن جملة الأمور المعتبرة في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم ، ما كانت حاضرة . فيثبت : أن حاصل هذا الجواب يرجع إلى هذا الحرف . لكنا بالدليل القاهر بينا صحته . الوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب : أن نقول : تعليل الإرادة بالعلم باطل . وذلك لأن العلم يتبع المعلوم ولا يستتبعه فالعلم يتعلق بالشيء على ما هو عليه ، فإن كان حدوث العالم مختصاً بذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يتعلق علم الله باختصاص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت ، وإذا ظهر أن العالم يتبع المعلوم فنقول : علم الله إنما يتعلق باختصاص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت ، لو كان ذلك الحدوث مختصاً بذلك الوقت، لكنكم زعمتم : أن الحدوث إنما اختص بذلك الوقت ، لأن إرادة الله تعالى اقتضت تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت ، فلو عللنا هذه الإرادة بذلك العلم ، لزم الدور . وأنه باطل . الوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا الجواب : أن نقول : إن مع حصول هذا العلم المخصوص ، إما أن تكون الإرادة صالحة لتخصيص إحداث العالم بسائر الأوقات ، أو لا تصلح . فإن صلحت لم يكن هذا العلم سبباً لذلك الاختصاص ، وإن لم تصلح فحينئذ يكون إله العالم موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار . وهذا عندكم باطل . وبتقدير ثبوته فيلزم قدم العالم .
As for the third answer: which is their saying: He, exalted, specified the creation of the world at that time only because He, exalted, knew in pre-eternity that the world would occur at that time, and contradicting God's knowledge is impossible. So we say: This answer is also weak. And there are aspects that indicate it: The first aspect: We say: According to the flow of your speech, the occurrence of that time which God, exalted, knew that the world would occur in it is one of the necessary matters for the world's occurrence. So we say: Was that time present in pre-eternity or was it not present in pre-eternity? If the first, then the occurrence of the world in pre-eternity would be necessary; if the second, then we say: The presence of that time is one of the matters considered in the world's occurrence, and it was not present. Thus this implies that the totality of matters considered for Him, exalted, to be the originator of the world were not present. So it is established that the gist of this answer reduces to this point. But we have demonstrated its soundness by compelling proof. The second aspect in explaining the weakness of this answer: We say: Explaining the will by knowledge is false. That is because knowledge follows the known and does not make it follow; knowledge pertains to a thing as it is. If the occurrence of the world was specific to that time, then God's knowledge pertains to the specification of the world's occurrence to that time. And when it appears that knowledge follows the known, we say: God's knowledge would pertain to the specification of the world's occurrence to that time only if that occurrence was specific to that time. But you claim that the occurrence was specific to that time because God's will necessitated the specification of the world's creation to that time. So if we explain this will by that knowledge, circularity would follow, and it is false. The third aspect in nullifying this answer: We say: With the presence of this specific knowledge, either the will is suitable for specifying the world's creation to other times, or it is not suitable. If it is suitable, then this knowledge is not the cause for that specification; if it is not suitable, then the God of the world would be a necessary agent by essence, not a voluntary agent. And this is false according to you. And if it were established, then the eternity of the world would follow.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage refutes the third answer of the opponents, who claimed that God created the world at a specific time because He eternally knew it would happen then, and opposing His knowledge is impossible. The author presents three arguments (wujuh) to show its weakness. First, if the time was present in pre-eternity (al-azal), then the world would have to exist eternally; if not, then the necessary conditions for creation were absent, which is what the author claims he has already proven false. Second, explaining divine will (irada) by knowledge (ilm) leads to circularity (dawr), because knowledge depends on the known, and the known (the specific time) depends on the will. Third, if with God's knowledge the will remains able to create at other times, then knowledge is not the cause; if the will is not able, then God is a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat) rather than a voluntary agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar), which the opponents reject and would imply the world's eternity. Overall, the refutation defends the Ash'ari position that God acts by will and choice, not by necessary emanation.

Key Terms
علم
إرادة
دور
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge alone determines when events occur, so His will is automatically aligned with His knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that knowledge follows the known and does not cause it. God's will is free and not necessitated by His knowledge; otherwise circularity or necessary agency would result.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of the third answer, what does the second aspect (al-wajh al-thani) argue?
A) That the time of creation was present in pre-eternity
B) That explaining will by knowledge leads to circularity
C) That God is a necessary agent
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's will is free and not determined by His knowledge; the attempt to explain creation timing by divine knowledge alone leads to logical contradictions and unacceptable theological consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as though your knowledge of a future outcome forces you into a certain decision, forgetting that you still have a choice? How does this lesson challenge that assumption about your own will?
?
When you pray or make plans, do you truly believe that God's knowledge of your actions does not restrict your freedom? What would change in your life if you acted on that belief?
?
If you claim God is all-knowing, do you sometimes fall into thinking that your mistakes were 'destined' because He knew, rather than owning your choice? How can you live today with the full weight of your free will before Him?

The Dilemma Against an Eternal Preventer
A logical dilemma refuting the claim that eternity prevents divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical dilemma refutes the claim that eternity prevents divine action, and why the concept of an eternal preventer collapses.

Core Idea
An eternal preventer would either never allow action or not be eternal.

This passage presents a powerful logical refutation of the idea that God's eternity prevents Him from acting in time. The argument takes the form of a dilemma, forcing the opponent to choose between two impossible consequences. Let's walk through it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional 'either...or' structure.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for the nature of eternity: necessary in itself or possible in itself. Find where these are stated and note the consequence that follows from each.
The first branch leads to impossibility of action; the second leads to non-eternality.
3
Analyze the logical outcome of each branch
For each branch, ask: what must be true if we accept it? The first branch implies action never occurs (contradicts reality). The second branch makes eternity dependent and thus not eternal.
Both outcomes are problematic for the opponent's position.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the supposed eternal preventer is not actually present in eternity. This refutes the claim that eternity prevents divine action.
The outcome is that the opponent's notion of an eternal preventer is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا نقول : هذا الأزل الذي جعلتموه مانعاً من الإيجاد والتكوين ، إما أن يكون واجب الثبوت لذاته ، أو ممكن الثبوت لذاته . فإن كان الأول ، فإنه يمتنع الزوال . فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يوجد الفعل أبداً . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون ممكناً ، فيفتقر إلى المرجح . وكل مفتقر إلى المرجح فإنه ليس أزلياً عند القائلين بهذا الجواب . ينتج : أن الأزل واجب أن لا يكون أزلياً . وعلى هذا التقدير فإن مسمى الذي جعلوه [مانعاً] من الفعل غير حاصل في الأزل . وذلك هو المطلوب
The third: We say: This eternity which you have made a preventer of bringing into existence and composition — either it is necessary in its existence by itself, or possible in its existence by itself. If it is the first, then its removal is impossible. Then it necessarily follows that the act never occurs. And if it is the second, then it is possible, so it depends on a preponderant. And everything that depends on a preponderant is not eternal according to those who hold this answer. It follows that eternity must not be eternal. On this estimation, the thing they have made a preventer of the act is not realized in eternity. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a logical dilemma against the claim that eternity (al-azal) prevents God from creating the world. The author (likely Ibn al-Mulaqqin or similar) structures the argument as follows: The attribute of eternity posited as a preventer must be either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If it is necessary, it cannot cease, so the prevention would be eternal and no act ever occurs — but this contradicts the actual existence of the world. If it is possible, then it requires a preponderant to exist, meaning it is not truly eternal (since what depends on a preponderant is originated). Thus, in either case, the concept of an eternal preventer collapses: either it never begins to prevent (so the act is impossible) or it is not eternal (so it does not prevent from eternity). The conclusion is that the supposed preventer is not actually present in eternity, which is what the author set out to prove.

Key Terms

التكوين (al-takwin)
واجب الثبوت (wajib al-thubut)
ممكن الثبوت (mumkin al-thubut)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
أزلي (azali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is a real attribute that inherently blocks divine action.
Correction: This argument shows that the notion of eternity as a preventer is incoherent: it would either make action impossible or fail to be eternal.

Checkpoint

B) It cannot cease, so action never occurs

D) It is not eternal

Key Takeaway
The claim that eternity prevents divine action collapses under a logical dilemma: an eternal preventer would either never allow action or not be eternal.

?

?

?
The opponents' claim collapsed under scrutiny. Are there beliefs about God you hold that you have never questioned? Pray about one such belief and ask: is it from revelation or from my own limited reasoning? What step will you take today to align your conviction with the truth?

The Dilemma of Eternity: Real Time or Imagination?
A dilemma argument about the nature of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of eternity as a real time is self-contradictory and cannot serve as a preventer of divine action.

Core Idea
Eternity is either a specific time (contradicting eternity) or imaginary (impotent to prevent).

This argument challenges the opponent's notion of eternity head-on. It forces a choice: if eternity is a real, specific time, it has a beginning and is not eternal; if it is not real, it exists only in minds and cannot block anything in reality. Either way, the alleged preventer disappears.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two alternatives presented. The author begins with a direct question.
Notice the two possible answers: 'if it is' and 'if it is not'.
2
Analyze the first alternative
If eternity is a real specific time, it becomes a pointed time with a beginning, contradicting the definition of eternity. Moreover, acts in it are limited, so no preventer exists.
The key is that a specific time has boundaries.
3
Analyze the second alternative
If eternity is not a real time, it has no external existence; it is merely a mental concept. Such fantasies cannot prevent real things.
Mental constructs lack causal power in the external world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن هذا الذي سميتموه بالأزل ، هل هو وقت متعين في نفسه موجود في ذاته ، أم لا ؟ فإن كان وقتاً متعيناً في نفسه موجوداً بذاته ، فهو وقت مشار إليه ، فهو ليس بالأزل . بل كل ما يقع فيه فإنه يكون واقعاً في وقت محدود له أول . فلا يكون المانع من الفعل والتكوين حاصلا . وأما إن كان ذلك الوقت ليس وقتاً موجوداً في نفسه ، متعيناً في ذاته ، فهذا مما لا وجود له في الخارج البتة ، وإنما وجوده في الأوهام والأذهان. ومثل هذه الخيالات والأوهام لا تكون مانعة من الشيء في نفس الأمر
The fourth: This thing which you have named 'eternity' — is it a specific time that exists in itself, or not? If it is a specific time existing in itself, then it is a pointed time, so it is not eternity. Rather, everything that occurs in it occurs in a limited time that has a beginning. So the preventer of the act and composition is not present. But if that time is not a time existing in itself, specific in itself, then it has no existence in the external world at all; its existence is only in imaginations and minds. And such fantasies and imaginations cannot prevent a thing in actual fact.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage offers a second refutation based on the nature of eternity itself. The author splits the opponent's concept of eternity into two possibilities: either it is a real, specific time that exists independently, or it is not. If it is a real specific time, then it is a pointed time with a beginning, which contradicts the definition of eternity (that which has no beginning). Moreover, any act occurring in such a time would be in a limited time, so the alleged preventer (eternity) is not present. If it is not a real time, then it has no external existence; it is merely an idea in minds and imaginations. And such mental constructs cannot serve as real preventers of actual things. Thus, in either case, the supposed eternal preventer is either not eternal or not real, and hence cannot block divine action.

Key Terms

متعين (muta'ayyin)
موجود بذاته (mawjud bi-dhatihi)
مشار إليه (mushar ilayh)
الأوهام (al-awham)
الأذهان (al-adhhan)
الخيالات (al-khayalat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is a real attribute that prevents God from acting.
Correction: Eternity is either a specific time (not eternal) or imaginary (cannot prevent).

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the fourth argument regarding the preventer of action?
A) It is a specific time that exists
B) It is imaginary and cannot prevent real things
C) It exists in the external world
D) It is a necessary attribute of God

Key Takeaway
The alleged eternal preventer is either not eternal or not real, so it cannot block divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the concept of 'waiting for the right time' to avoid an act of obedience? This argument reveals that the 'time' you imagine is either limited or unreal. What 'eternity' are you hiding behind today? Will you act now?

When you say 'I will do it eventually,' what is that 'eventually'? Is it a real time or just an illusion that prevents you from acting? How does this argument expose your delay?
?
Does the idea of 'eternity' ever make you feel secure in postponing repentance? Remember this dilemma: if it's real, it's limited; if imaginary, it has no power. So what is your excuse today?

Refutation of the Sixth Answer on God's Will
Refutes the claim that the timing question is unanswerable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the question of God's specific timing of creation is unanswerable and thus invalid is itself weak, and how a disjunctive argument demonstrates that this claim leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Dismissing the timing question leads to an even greater absurdity.

In this passage, the author tackles a sophisticated objection: the sixth answer attempts to dismiss the question of why God created at this specific time by arguing that the same question would apply to any time, so the question is invalid. The author refutes this by showing that the logic of the objector leads to an unavoidable dilemma that exposes the weakness of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sixth Answer
Read the description of the sixth answer: it claims that the question of why God created at this time would recur for any chosen time, so the question itself drops.
This is a common objection in kalām discussions about divine will.
2
Follow the Disjunctive Proof
The author presents a disjunction: either God's power is specific to a time or not. The first option is impossible because it would require an arbitrary specifier, so the second must hold, implying an eternal world.
This is a classic proof by contradiction.
3
Recognize the Consequence
The opponent objects that eternal creation is also impossible, showing that the sixth answer does not avoid the problem—it merely shifts it.
The passage ends mid-rebuttal, but the argument's structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب السادس: وهو قولهم: إن إرادة الله تعالى لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر، لكان السؤال المذكور عائداً، وهو أن يقال: ولم تعلقت إرادته سبحانه بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت دون هذا الوقت؟ ولما كان هذا السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات، كان السؤال ساقطاً. فنقول: هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: أن نقول: بأن تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم إما أن يكون مختصاً بوقت معين، أو لا يكون مختصاً بوقت معين. والأول باطل، لأن تخصيص ذلك الوقت المعين بذلك الإيجاد من غير مخصص يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح. وهو محال. ولما بطل هذا القسم تعين القسم الثاني، وهو أن يقال: تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم غير مختص بوقت معين، بل هو دائم الحصول في كل الأوقات. وذلك يقتضي كون العالم موجوداً أبداً سرمداً. وعند هذا يظهر أن المحال غير لازم على كل الأقسام، بل هو لازم على أحد القسمين، ولما كان هذا القسم مستلزماً للمحال، ثبت أن مستلزم المحال: محال، ثبت أن هذا القسم محال ولزم منه: أن يكون الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن يكون تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم غير مختص بشيء من الأوقات البتة، بل هو ثابت أزلاً وأبداً. فيثبت: أن هذا الجواب في غاية الضعف. فإن قالوا: وكذلك القول بالتأثير الدائم أيضاً: باطل. لأن إيجاد الفعل في الأزل: محال. فنقول: فهذا
As for the sixth answer: that is their statement: If God's will (irada) were attached to bringing about the world at another time, the aforementioned question would recur, namely: Why did His will attach to bringing about the world at that time rather than this time? And since this question recurs on every supposition, the question drops. We say: This answer is also weak. Proofs indicate it: First: We say: The influence of God's power (qudra) upon the existence of the world is either specific to a particular time or not specific to a particular time. The first is false, because the specification of that particular time for that act of bringing into existence without a specifying principle (mukhassis) would necessitate a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator (murajjih), and that is impossible. And when this division is invalid, the second division is determined, namely: The influence of God's power upon the existence of the world is not specific to a particular time, rather it is continuously obtaining in all times. That necessitates the world existing eternally and perpetually (sarmadan). At this point it becomes clear that the absurd (muhal) is not necessary for all divisions, but it is necessary for one of the two divisions. And since this division entails the absurd, it is established that whatever entails the absurd is absurd, so this division is absurd. And it follows from this that the truth is the second division, namely that the influence of God's power upon the existence of the world is not specific to any time at all, rather it is established eternally and forever. Thus it is established that this answer is extremely weak. Then if they say: Likewise the statement of perpetual influence is also false, because bringing about an act in pre-eternity (azal) is impossible. We say: This...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage refutes the sixth answer to the question of why God created the world at a specific time. The sixth answer claimed that since the same question would arise for any chosen time, the question is invalid. The author first states that this answer is weak and then gives one proof (with more implied). The proof is a disjunctive argument: either God's power is specific to a time or not. If specific, it requires a specifier, leading to an infinite regress of arbitrary choice. So the second option must be true: the power is continuously effective at all times, which implies the world is eternal. The opponent then objects that eternal creation is also impossible, but the author cuts off before completing the response (in the next portion). The argument demonstrates that the sixth answer leads to an absurd consequence, thus invalidating it.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
قدرة الله
تخصيص
مخصص
مرجح
ممكن
محال
أزل
سرمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a question can be asked for any possible time, then the question itself is invalid and can be dismissed.
Correction: This passage shows that dismissing the question leads to a paradox: either the choice of time is arbitrary (impossible) or the world is eternal (which the opponent also rejects). Thus the question cannot be so easily dismissed.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first proof against the sixth answer?
A) God's will attaches to all times equally.
B) God's power must be specific to a time, otherwise the world would be eternal.
C) The choice of a specific time without a specifier is impossible, so the power must be continuously effective, implying an eternal world.
D) The question only recurs if God's will is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Dismissing the question of God's timing does not solve the problem; it leads to equally problematic conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What does this passage teach you about the danger of such dismissals and the need to seek deeper understanding? Will you commit to wrestling with that question rather than pushing it away?
?
The proof shows that if God's power were specific to a time, it would require an arbitrary specifier—something you likely reject. Yet in your own life, do you sometimes treat God's actions as arbitrary, assuming His timing has no wisdom? How can this argument help you trust that His timing is always perfectly chosen, even when you don't see the reason?
?
The opponent claimed the question of timing is unanswerable, but the author shows it leads to a greater absurdity.
