Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A temporal hindrance could explain why God created later, without affecting His eternal power.
Correction: The first proof shows that any hindrance that exists in pre-eternity must be eternal and thus irremovable, making creation impossible forever. The second proof (incomplete) suggests that if the hindrance is temporal, its cessation requires a cause, leading to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if a hindrance is pre-eternal, what follows?
A) The hindrance must cease at some point.
B) The hindrance cannot cease, so the impossibility never ends.
C) The hindrance is caused by God's will.
D) The hindrance is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
A pre-eternal hindrance would be eternal and unremovable, making the impossibility of creation permanent; the second proof further challenges the coherence of a temporal hindrance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a pre-eternal hindrance would make God's act eternally impossible. What 'hindrance' are you using to delay your obedience to Allah? Is it truly a barrier, or is it an excuse you treat as permanent? Will you remove it today by an act of sincere repentance?
?
The opponent refined his objection, but it fell apart under scrutiny. In your life, you may have refined excuses for not submitting fully—like 'I am not ready yet' or 'I have time later.' Examine one such excuse. How will you abandon it after seeing that it contradicts what you know about Allah's power and your mortality?
?
If the hindrance were temporal, it would need a cause for its removal. Who else but Allah can remove the obstacles between you and Him? Are you relying on your own efforts to remove them, or are you begging Him directly? Make a specific du'a now to ask Him to remove the one hindrance you have identified.

Proof that Power is Not Capability in Pre-Eternity
Proof that power as capability is impossible and response to a constraint objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power cannot be understood as a 'capability' to act or refrain in pre-eternity, and how the author refutes an objection.

Core Idea
A capability that can never be used is no capability at all.

This lesson examines a key argument in classical Islamic theology about the nature of divine power. The author argues that if God's power were merely a 'capability' to act, it would have to exist either eternally or temporally—both lead to contradictions. An objector raises a hypothetical scenario, but the author shows it fails on its own terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: conditional, then objection, then answer.
2
Understand the proof-by-contradiction
The author assumes power as capability exists, then derives two possibilities (eternal or originated), shows both are impossible, so the assumption is false. Trace this reasoning.
3
Identify the objection
The objector says: why not say God had power in pre-eternity but pre-eternity itself acted as a constraint? Find this in the text.
The objector is trying to salvage the concept of capability.
4
Follow the two-fold response
The author gives two answers: (1) the objection also applies to other theories, (2) capability requires the act to be possible in itself. Understand each part.
The second answer is the decisive one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيثبت : أنه لو حصلت القدرة بمعنى المكنة من الفعل والترك لكانت إما أن تكون أزلية ، وإما أن تكون حادثة ، وثبت فساد كل واحد من هذين القسمين ، فكان ثبوت الملزوم : محالاً فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى في الأزل كان موصوفاً بالقدرة على الإيجاد ، إلا أن قيام الأزل يجري مجرى المانع من الإيجاد ، ولا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون القادر قادراً على الفعل إلا أنه يتعذر عليه الفعل لمانع . ثم نقول : هذا أيضاً لازم على القائلين بالتأثير الذاتي ، لأنه يلزم كونه مؤثراً في وجود العالم في الأزل ، أن العالم كان موجوداً في الأزل ، وذلك يقتضي تحصيل الحاصل ، وهو محال والجواب عن الأول : أن نقول : المكنة من الفعل والترك يمتنع حصولها ، إلا حيث كان حصول الفعل ممكناً في نفسه ، فلما كان حصول الفعل في الأزل ممتنعاً ، امتنع كون القادر قادراً عليه .
So it is established: that if power (al-qudra) in the sense of capability (al-makna) of action and inaction existed, it would either be eternal or originated; and the corruption of each of these two divisions is established, so the establishment of the necessary condition (al-malzum) is impossible. Then if it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, in pre-eternity was described with power to bring into existence, except that the subsistence of pre-eternity acts as a constrainter (al-mani') from bringing into existence, and it is not impossible in reason that the capable one be capable of the act but the act is prevented due to a constrainter? Then we say: This also applies necessarily to the proponents of intrinsic causal effect (al-ta'thir al-dhati), because it necessitates His being effective in the existence of the world in pre-eternity, that the world existed in pre-eternity, and that entails conferring existence on what exists, which is impossible. And the answer to the first: that we say: The capability of action and inaction is impossible to obtain except where the occurrence of the act is possible in itself; so when the occurrence of the act in pre-eternity is impossible, the capable one's being capable of it is impossible.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a proof-by-contradiction followed by an objection-and-answer. The author begins by establishing that if God's power were understood as 'capability' (al-makna) to act or refrain, then such capability would have to be either eternal or originated—both of which are shown to be impossible. Therefore, the very notion of such power is impossible. An objector then raises a challenge: why not say that God in pre-eternity did possess power to create, but the state of being pre-eternal itself (qiyam al-azal) acted as a constrainter preventing actual creation, much like a capable agent may be prevented by an external obstacle? The author responds in two parts. First, he notes that this very objection applies also to those who hold the theory of intrinsic causal effect (al-ta'thir al-dhati), because it would entail that God effected the world in pre-eternity, leading to the absurdity of 'conferring existence on what already exists'. Second, he gives the direct answer: capability of action and inaction can only exist where the action itself is possible. Since the occurrence of an act in pre-eternity is impossible (because pre-eternity is before all temporal existence), the capability cannot be attributed to God in that state.

Key Terms

المكنة
الأزل
المانع
التأثير الذاتي
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power is a 'capability' that could be thwarted by external obstacles, such as the state of pre-eternity.
Correction: The author shows that a capability that can never be actualized is contradictory; if the act is impossible in itself, capability cannot exist.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if power as capability existed, what would be the two possibilities for its temporal status?
A) Eternal or originated
B) Necessary or contingent
C) Prior to creation or after
D) With action or without

Key Takeaway
God's power cannot be merely a capability to act, because in pre-eternity such capability would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's power, do you ever imagine it as a 'potential' that might not be used? How does that belief affect the urgency of your prayers and your trust that He will act? This lesson says such a view is impossible. Will you let that truth change how you rely on Him today?
?
The objection mentioned a 'constrainer' in pre-eternity. In your own life, what 'constraints' do you imagine prevent God from acting—such as your past, your circumstances, or your unworthiness? This passage shows that God's power is never held back by any external factor. Are you willing to stop making excuses and live as if God's power is fully active in your situation right now?
?
The proof shows that God's capability is impossible because it would require the act to be possible in pre-eternity. But are you living as though God's commands are merely possibilities that you might fulfill one day? What would change in your life if you truly believed that God's power makes obedience possible here and now, without any 'constrainer'?

The First Argument: An Eternal Constraint Cannot Cease
First argument: eternal constraint leads to eternal prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal constraint on God's action would be permanent and thus impossible.

Core Idea
If the constraint is eternal, it can never cease, so creation would never happen.

The first argument tackles the assumption that pre-eternity itself is a constraint. If capability existed in pre-eternity, the prevention must be due to an existing constraint. That constraint would be eternal, and by the opponent's own logic, the eternal cannot cease. Therefore, prevention would never end—contradicting the observed existence of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how the author sets up the objection and then the first argument.
Pay attention to the logical flow: from the opponent's claim to the refutation.
2
Identify the core premises
Find the key premises: capability existed in pre-eternity, prevention must be due to a constraint, that constraint is eternal, and the eternal cannot cease.
3
Understand the conclusion
If the constraint cannot cease, prevention never ends, so creation would not occur—but it did, so the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: why can't the eternal cease? What does that say about pre-eternity?
The opponent is forced into a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القادرية كانت حاصلة في الأزل ، إلا أنه تعذر الفعل لمانع ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل .. ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه لما كانت المكنة حاصلة في الأزل ، فتعذر الفعل يمتنع يكون لعدم المقتضى ، بل لا بد وأن يكون لوجود المانع وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المانع من الإيجاد : أزلياً. وعندكم : أن الأزلي يمتنع زواله ، فيلزم امتناع زوال ذلك المانع . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما زال الامتناع الثاني : إنه لو حصل مانع يمنع من حصول ذلك الأثر . فإما أن يكون ذلك المانع . بحيث يزول أو لا يزول. فإن زال ، كان زواله مختصاً بوقت معين . لأن كل ما كان موجوداً ، ثم صار معدوماً . فإن لابد وأن يكون مختصاً بوقت معين. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالمكنة التامة من الإيجاد والإحداث ، ما كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت. إذ لو حصلت مكنة الإيجاد قبل ذلك الوقت ، لوقع ذلك الإيجاد في الأزل . إلا أن هذا الكلام باطل . لأن الوقت المتقدم على ذلك الوقت بمدة متناهية ، يكون خارجاً عن الأزل ، وواقعاً في الأبد. فيلزم أن يقال : إن بعد انقضاء الأزل بقي الله عاجزاً عن الإيجاد والإحداث . وذلك يبطل قولكم : إن الفعل امتنع لقيام المانع وهو حصول الأزل وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن ذلك المانع لا يزول البتة فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقال : إن الله تعالى لا يقدر على الإيجاد والإحداث البتة . وذلك يبطل كونه تعالى خالقاً للعالم وهو محال والوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا السؤال : أن نقول : مسمى الأزل ، وهو الذي حكمتم بكونه مانعاً للقادر من الفعل والتأثير . إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته أو ممكن الوجود لذاته. فإن كان الأول ، امتنع زواله فوجب أن لا تحصل المكنة من الإيجاد البنة وإن كان الثاني كان ممكن الارتفاع نظراً إليه ، من حيث إنه هو ، إذ لو كان ممتنع الارتفاع من حيث هو هو ، لصار واجباً . وقد فرضنا ممكناً لذاته ، وإذا كان ممكن الارتفاع من حيث هو هو ، فبتقدير ارتفاعه ، كان القادر متمكناً من الإيجاد والإحداث دائماً وقد فرضنا : أن ذلك محال : فثبت : أن القول بالأزل مانع من حصول هذه المكنة قول يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة . فوجب كونه باطلا . وأيضاً : فحصول
It remains to be said: Why is it not permissible to say that capability (al-qadariyya) existed in pre-eternity, but the act was prevented due to a constrainter? So we say: This is false... and there are three arguments (wujuh) indicating it: First: that when capability existed in pre-eternity, the prevention of the act cannot be due to the absence of the requisite, but rather must be due to the existence of a constrainter. On this estimation, the constrainter from bringing into existence would be eternal. And according to you, the eternal is impossible to cease, so it necessitates the impossibility of the cessation of that constrainter. And if it were so, prevention would never cease. Second: that if a constrainter prevents the occurrence of that effect, then that constrainter is either such that it ceases or does not cease. If it ceases, its cessation is specific to a particular time. Because everything that exists and then becomes non-existent must be specific to a particular time. On this estimation, the complete capability of bringing into existence and originating was not present before that time. For if the capability of bringing into existence had existed before that time, that bringing into existence would have occurred in pre-eternity. However, this statement is false, because the time preceding that time by a finite duration is outside of pre-eternity and occurs in perpetuity. So it follows that after the end of pre-eternity, God would remain incapable of bringing into existence and originating. And that invalidates your saying that the act was prevented due to the subsistence of the constrainter, which is the existence of pre-eternity. As for the second division: that this constrainter never ceases at all, we say: if it were so, then it would be necessary to say that God, exalted, is not able to bring into existence and originate at all. And that invalidates His being Creator of the world, and it is impossible. The third argument in refuting this question: that we say: The referent of 'pre-eternity' (al-azal), which you have judged to be a constrainter for the capable from acting and effecting, is either necessary of existence in itself or possible of existence in itself. If it is the first, its cessation is impossible, so capability of bringing into existence would never be obtained. If it is the second, it is possible of being removed in itself, because if it were impossible of being removed in itself, it would become necessary. But we have assumed it is possible in itself. And if it is possible of being removed in itself, then upon its removal, the capable would be able to bring into existence and originate perpetually, and we have assumed that to be impossible. So it is established that the statement that pre-eternity is a constrainter from obtaining this capability is a statement that leads to false divisions. Therefore it must be false. And also: that obtaining...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second objection, structured as three independent arguments (wujuh). The objection claims that God in pre-eternity possessed capability (al-qadariyya) but was prevented from acting by a constraint (al-azal itself). The author states this is false and provides three arguments. First argument: If capability existed in pre-eternity, the prevention must be due to an existing constraint, which would be eternal and thus eternal like the constraint; but an eternal constraint cannot cease, so prevention would never end—contradicting the observed temporal creation. Second argument: The constraint either ceases or does not cease. If it ceases, its cessation occurs at a specific time; before that time, complete capability was absent, otherwise creation would have occurred in pre-eternity. Moreover, the time before that finite time is still outside pre-eternity, implying God remained incapable for a period—contradicting the claim that the constraint was pre-eternity. If it never ceases, God would never be able to create, ruining His being Creator—absurd. Third argument: Pre-eternity itself is either necessary in itself (so cannot cease, thus capability never obtained) or possible in itself. If possible, its removal is possible, leading to God having capability perpetually—but we already established that is impossible. Hence the notion of pre-eternity as a constraint leads to contradictions and is false. The passage ends abruptly with 'wa-ayḍan: fa-ḥuṣūl' suggesting additional points.

Key Terms
القادرية (al-qadariyya)
المانع (al-mani')
الأزل (al-azal)
الوجوه (al-wujuh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pre-eternity could be a temporary constraint that later lifts.
Correction: The argument shows that if the constraint exists in pre-eternity, it is eternal per the opponent's own definition, and thus cannot cease.

Checkpoint
In the first argument, what does the author assume about the nature of the constraint if it exists in pre-eternity?
A) It is temporal and can cease
B) It is eternal and cannot cease
C) It is created by God
D) It is a mere possibility

Key Takeaway
The first argument reduces the opponent's position to absurdity: an eternal constraint would prevent creation forever, contradicting the fact that creation exists.

The Second Argument: The Constraint Either Ceases or Does Not
Second argument: dilemma of the constraint's cessation or permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument's dilemma: if the constraint ceases, God was temporarily incapable; if it never ceases, God is never capable, both impossible.

Core Idea
A constraint that ceases implies God was temporarily incapable; one that never ceases implies God is never capable.

The second argument presents a dilemma: either the constraint on divine action ceases at some point, or it never ceases. If it ceases, its cessation occurs at a specific time, meaning before that time God lacked complete capability—making Him temporarily incapable, which is absurd. If it never ceases, God can never create at all, which denies His being Creator. Both options are impossible, so the notion of pre-eternity as a constraint is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation, following the two branches of the dilemma.
2
Analyze the first branch: constraint ceases
If the constraint ceases at some time, then before that time complete capability was absent. This implies God was incapable for a period, which is absurd.
Also, the time before that finite period is outside pre-eternity, so God would remain incapable after pre-eternity ended.
3
Analyze the second branch: constraint never ceases
If the constraint never ceases, God is never able to create, which denies His power and role as Creator.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both branches lead to impossibilities, so the initial claim that pre-eternity is a constraint must be false.

بقي أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القادرية كانت حاصلة في الأزل ، إلا أنه تعذر الفعل لمانع ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل .. ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه لما كانت المكنة حاصلة في الأزل ، فتعذر الفعل يمتنع يكون لعدم المقتضى ، بل لا بد وأن يكون لوجود المانع وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المانع من الإيجاد : أزلياً. وعندكم : أن الأزلي يمتنع زواله ، فيلزم امتناع زوال ذلك المانع . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما زال الامتناع الثاني : إنه لو حصل مانع يمنع من حصول ذلك الأثر . فإما أن يكون ذلك المانع . بحيث يزول أو لا يزول. فإن زال ، كان زواله مختصاً بوقت معين . لأن كل ما كان موجوداً ، ثم صار معدوماً . فإن لابد وأن يكون مختصاً بوقت معين. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالمكنة التامة من الإيجاد والإحداث ، ما كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت. إذ لو حصلت مكنة الإيجاد قبل ذلك الوقت ، لوقع ذلك الإيجاد في الأزل . إلا أن هذا الكلام باطل . لأن الوقت المتقدم على ذلك الوقت بمدة متناهية ، يكون خارجاً عن الأزل ، وواقعاً في الأبد. فيلزم أن يقال : إن بعد انقضاء الأزل بقي الله عاجزاً عن الإيجاد والإحداث . وذلك يبطل قولكم : إن الفعل امتنع لقيام المانع وهو حصول الأزل وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن ذلك المانع لا يزول البتة فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقال : إن الله تعالى لا يقدر على الإيجاد والإحداث البتة . وذلك يبطل كونه تعالى خالقاً للعالم وهو محال والوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا السؤال : أن نقول : مسمى الأزل ، وهو الذي حكمتم بكونه مانعاً للقادر من الفعل والتأثير . إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته أو ممكن الوجود لذاته. فإن كان الأول ، امتنع زواله فوجب أن لا تحصل المكنة من الإيجاد البنة وإن كان الثاني كان ممكن الارتفاع نظراً إليه ، من حيث إنه هو ، إذ لو كان ممتنع الارتفاع من حيث هو هو ، لصار واجباً . وقد فرضنا ممكناً لذاته ، وإذا كان ممكن الارتفاع من حيث هو هو ، فبتقدير ارتفاعه ، كان القادر متمكناً من الإيجاد والإحداث دائماً وقد فرضنا : أن ذلك محال : فثبت : أن القول بالأزل مانع من حصول هذه المكنة قول يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة . فوجب كونه باطلا . وأيضاً : فحصول
It remains to be said: Why is it not permissible to say that capability (al-qadariyya) existed in pre-eternity, but the act was prevented due to a constrainter? So we say: This is false... and there are three arguments (wujuh) indicating it: First: that when capability existed in pre-eternity, the prevention of the act cannot be due to the absence of the requisite, but rather must be due to the existence of a constrainter. On this estimation, the constrainter from bringing into existence would be eternal. And according to you, the eternal is impossible to cease, so it necessitates the impossibility of the cessation of that constrainter. And if it were so, prevention would never cease. Second: that if a constrainter prevents the occurrence of that effect, then that constrainter is either such that it ceases or does not cease. If it ceases, its cessation is specific to a particular time. Because everything that exists and then becomes non-existent must be specific to a particular time. On this estimation, the complete capability of bringing into existence and originating was not present before that time. For if the capability of bringing into existence had existed before that time, that bringing into existence would have occurred in pre-eternity. However, this statement is false, because the time preceding that time by a finite duration is outside of pre-eternity and occurs in perpetuity. So it follows that after the end of pre-eternity, God would remain incapable of bringing into existence and originating. And that invalidates your saying that the act was prevented due to the subsistence of the constrainter, which is the existence of pre-eternity. As for the second division: that this constrainter never ceases at all, we say: if it were so, then it would be necessary to say that God, exalted, is not able to bring into existence and originate at all. And that invalidates His being Creator of the world, and it is impossible. The third argument in refuting this question: that we say: The referent of 'pre-eternity' (al-azal), which you have judged to be a constrainter for the capable from acting and effecting, is either necessary of existence in itself or possible of existence in itself. If it is the first, its cessation is impossible, so capability of bringing into existence would never be obtained. If it is the second, it is possible of being removed in itself, because if it were impossible of being removed in itself, it would become necessary. But we have assumed it is possible in itself. And if it is possible of being removed in itself, then upon its removal, the capable would be able to bring into existence and originate perpetually, and we have assumed that to be impossible. So it is established that the statement that pre-eternity is a constrainter from obtaining this capability is a statement that leads to false divisions. Therefore it must be false. And also: that obtaining...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second objection, structured as three independent arguments (wujuh). The objection claims that God in pre-eternity possessed capability (al-qadariyya) but was prevented from acting by a constraint (al-azal itself). The author states this is false and provides three arguments. First argument: If capability existed in pre-eternity, the prevention must be due to an existing constraint, which would be eternal and thus eternal like the constraint; but an eternal constraint cannot cease, so prevention would never end—contradicting the observed temporal creation. Second argument: The constraint either ceases or does not cease. If it ceases, its cessation occurs at a specific time; before that time, complete capability was absent, otherwise creation would have occurred in pre-eternity. Moreover, the time before that finite time is still outside pre-eternity, implying God remained incapable for a period—contradicting the claim that the constraint was pre-eternity. If it never ceases, God would never be able to create, ruining His being Creator—absurd. Third argument: Pre-eternity itself is either necessary in itself (so cannot cease, thus capability never obtained) or possible in itself. If possible, its removal is possible, leading to God having capability perpetually—but we already established that is impossible. Hence the notion of pre-eternity as a constraint leads to contradictions and is false. The passage ends abruptly with 'wa-ayḍan: fa-ḥuṣūl' suggesting additional points.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani')
الأزل (al-azal)
الوقت (al-waqt)
المكنة (al-makna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second argument, what is the consequence if the constraint never ceases?
A) God will eventually create
B) God is never able to create
C) The constraint becomes temporal
D) Pre-eternity ends

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that whether the constraint ceases or not, both outcomes are impossible, thus refuting the claim of pre-eternity as a constraint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is truly unlimited and not constrained by pre-eternity, what does that mean for your own understanding of His ability to intervene in your life at any moment? Are you living as if His power is limited by time or circumstances?
?
The argument shows that thinking of God as constrained by pre-eternity leads to absurdities. Have you ever, even subconsciously, placed limits on God's power based on your own expectations of when He should act? Confess that to yourself and resolve to trust His timing without conditions.
?
This lesson refutes the idea that God could be temporarily incapable. Do you truly believe that God is always fully capable, even in moments when you feel He is silent? Will you pray today with the confidence that His power is not hindered by any constraint, including your doubts?

The Third Argument: Pre-Eternity's Modality
Third argument: dichotomy of necessity and possibility in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument, which examines whether pre-eternity itself is necessary or possible in existence, showing both lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity cannot be a constraint because whether it is necessary or possible, the result is absurd.

The third argument takes a different angle: it analyzes the metaphysical status of pre-eternity (al-azal) itself. If pre-eternity is necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), it cannot cease, so capability never arises. If it is possible in itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi), it can cease, and if it does, God would have perpetual capability—a claim already dismissed. Both options lead to false conclusions, so the original claim that pre-eternity is a constraint is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the classification of pre-eternity as necessary or possible.
2
Analyze the first possibility: necessary in itself
If pre-eternity is necessary in itself, it cannot cease, so capability never arises—contradicting God's power.
3
Analyze the second possibility: possible in itself
If possible, it can cease. If it ceases, God would be perpetually capable, which was already deemed impossible.
Also, if it were impossible to cease, it would be necessary, contradicting possibility.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both possibilities yield false consequences, so the notion of pre-eternity as a constraint is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Impossibility of a Finite Time Before Eternity
Refutation of the claim that divine power is temporally specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a specific time to divine power logically leads to the absurdity of God becoming powerless after eternity, thus refuting the claim that an obstacle prevented creation.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be limited to a specific time without implying incapability.

This passage presents a logical refutation of the claim that God's power to create was absent in eternity because of an obstacle. The author shows that if divine power were confined to a particular time, then before that time it would either have to create in eternity or leave God powerless afterward—both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the assumption and its consequence
The author assumes the opponent's view that divine power is specific to a particular time. Then he shows that if power existed before that time, creation would have occurred in eternity.
The consequence is presented as hypothetical.
3
Understand the refutation and conclusion
The author rejects the consequence because the time before the specified time is finite and falls within perpetuity, not eternity. This leads to the conclusion that after eternity, God would be incapable, which invalidates the obstacle theory.
The argument demonstrates a logical contradiction in the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن يكون مختصاً بوقت معين. وعلى هذا التقدير، فالمكنة التامة من الإيجاد والإحداث، ما كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت. إذ لو حصلت مكنة الإيجاد قبل ذلك الوقت، لوقع ذلك الإيجاد في الأزل. إلا أن هذا الكلام باطل. لأن الوقت المتقدم على ذلك الوقت بمدة متناهية، يكون خارجاً عن الأزل، وواقعاً في الأبد. فيلزم أن يقال: إن بعد انقضاء الأزل بقي الله عاجزاً عن الإيجاد والإحداث. وذلك يبطل قولكم: إن الفعل امتنع لقيام المانع وهو حصول الأزل.
And that it is specific to a particular time. On this assumption, the complete capability of bringing into existence and originating was not present before that time. For if the capability of bringing into existence had been present before that time, that bringing into existence would have occurred in eternity. However, this statement is false. Because the time preceding that time by a finite duration is outside of eternity and falls within perpetuity. Consequently, it must be said that after the termination of eternity God remained incapable of bringing into existence and originating. And that invalidates your statement that the act was prevented due to the existence of the obstacle, which is the occurrence of eternity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the claim that God's power to create was absent in eternity due to an obstacle (mani'). The author begins by assuming the opponent's position: that divine power is specific to a particular time. On this assumption, the complete capability (al-makina al-tamma) of bringing into existence (al-ijad) and originating (al-ihdath) was not present before that time. The author then draws a consequence: if the capability had been present before that time, then the act of bringing into existence would have occurred in eternity (al-azal). But this is false. The reason is that the time preceding that specified time by a finite duration is outside of eternity and falls within perpetuity (al-abad). Thus, it follows that after the end of eternity, God remained incapable of bringing into existence. This invalidates the opponent's claim that the act was prevented only because of the obstacle, i.e., the state of being eternal. The argument shows that the opponent's position leads to a contradiction: either creation occurs in eternity (which is presumably not the opponent's view) or God becomes powerless after eternity, which is impossible.

Key Terms
مكنة (al-makina): capability
إيجاد (al-ijad): bringing into existence
إحداث (al-ihdath): originating
أزل (al-azal): eternity without beginning
أبد (al-abad): eternity without end
مانع (al-mani'): obstacle
عاجز (ajiz): incapable

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power was absent in eternity due to an obstacle (eternity itself).
Correction: If divine power were absent before a specific time due to an obstacle, then after eternity God would be powerless, which is absurd. The obstacle theory fails.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does the assumption that divine power is specific to a particular time lead to a contradiction?
A) It implies God is incapable after eternity.
B) It proves that God's power is eternal.
C) It shows that creation occurs in eternity.
D) It invalidates the concept of an obstacle.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that attributing a specific time to divine power leads to the absurdity of God being powerless, thus refuting the obstacle theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believe that God's power is not bounded by time, why do you still worry about the future as if His ability to help you is not always complete?
?
In what areas of your life have you acted as if God's power is 'on hold' until a certain time, rather than being fully present now? What would change if you lived with the conviction that His complete capability is always available?

Divine Attributes
beginner

The Refutation of a Permanent Obstacle
The author refutes the claim that the obstacle never ceases, leading to God's perpetual inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming a permanent obstacle to divine action leads to an impossible conclusion about God's power and role as Creator.

Core Idea
A permanent obstacle makes God permanently unable to create, which contradicts His being the Creator.

In this short but decisive passage, the author addresses the second possibility raised by the opponent: that the obstacle preventing God's action never ceases. He shows that if this were true, God would never be able to create at all, which would directly negate His attribute as Creator. This argument reinforces that obstacles to divine action must be temporary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with its English translation. Notice the structure of the argument: the opponent's claim, then the conditional reasoning, then the conclusion.
The passage is only four sentences long but packs a logical punch.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: (1) The opponent says the obstacle never ceases. (2) If that were the case, then God would never be able to create. (3) But that would mean God is not the Creator, which is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opponent's claim leads to a contradiction.
3
Reflect on the theological consequence
Consider why the conclusion 'God is not the Creator' is impossible in Islamic theology. This shows that the opponent's claim must be false.
The attribute of being Creator is essential to God; negating it is rationally and theologically impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: إن ذلك المانع لا يزول البتة فنقول: لو كان الأمر كذلك، لوجب أن يقال: إن الله تعالى لا يقدر على الإيجاد والإحداث البتة. وذلك يبطل كونه تعالى خالقاً للعالم وهو محال.
As for the second division: it is that it is said that that obstacle never ceases at all. We say: if the matter were such, then it would necessarily be said that God, exalted, is not able at all to bring into existence and originate. And that invalidates His being the Creator of the world, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of the refutation. The opponent might claim that the obstacle (al-mani') never ceases at all (la yazulu al-battata). The author argues that if this were the case, then it would necessarily follow that God is perpetually incapable of bringing into existence (al-ijad) and originating (al-ihdath). This would invalidate the fact that God is the Creator of the world (khaliq al-alam), which is impossible (muhal). The argument is straightforward: a permanent obstacle implies permanent inability, which contradicts the established belief that God is the Creator.

Key Terms
قسم (al-qism): division
مانع (al-mani'): obstacle
يزول (yazulu): ceases
البتة (al-battata): at all
قادر (qadir): able
خالق (khaliq): Creator
محال (muhal): impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An obstacle to God's action can be permanent without affecting His creative power.
Correction: The passage shows that a permanent obstacle implies God is permanently unable to create, which contradicts His being the Creator. Therefore, no obstacle to divine action can be permanent.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the obstacle never ceases?
A) God's power becomes eternal.
B) God ceases to be the Creator.
C) The obstacle is removed after eternity.
D) Creation occurs in eternity.

Key Takeaway
If an obstacle to God's action never ceases, then God would be permanently unable to act, contradicting His essential attribute as the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a recurring sin or struggle, do you ever treat it as a 'permanent obstacle' that can never change? How does this lesson's argument about God's power challenge your assumption and call you to trust in His ability to transform you?
?
If no obstacle can permanently prevent God from acting, what does that mean for the obstacles you see in your prayers, your repentance, or your relationship with Him? Will you today ask Him to remove what you think is permanent?
?
This lesson shows that claiming a permanent obstacle leads to denying God's attribute as Creator. Is there any area of your life where you are acting as if God's power is permanently blocked? How can you change your perspective and reliance on Him starting now?

The Nature of Eternity: Necessary or Contingent
The author analyzes the essence of eternity as necessary or contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how analyzing the essence of eternity (al-azal) as either necessary or contingent refutes the claim that eternity is an obstacle to divine action.

Core Idea
Eternity cannot be an obstacle because its essence must be either necessary or contingent—both lead to falsehood.

This lesson continues the refutation of the objection that eternity prevents divine action. The author now examines the very thing called 'eternity' and asks: what kind of existence does it have? By classifying it as necessary or contingent, he shows that either way the claim collapses. The passage also considers the cause of this supposed obstacle, leading to further contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Examine the dichotomy
Read the passage and identify that the author divides the referent of eternity into two categories: necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) or contingent of existence by itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi).
These two categories are the fundamental division of existence in kalam.
2
Trace the consequences of each option
For the necessary option, its ceasing is impossible, so capability of creation is never obtained. For the contingent option, it can be removed, leading to perpetual capability, which was assumed impossible. Both outcomes are false.
The conclusion: the claim that eternity is an obstacle leads to falsehood.
3
Consider the cause of the obstacle
The author adds a sub-argument: the obstacle itself is contingent, so it requires an efficient cause. That cause is either a necessitating cause or a free agent, both leading to further contradictions.
This reinforces the refutation by showing the obstacle's own existence is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا السؤال: أن نقول: مسمى الأزل، وهو الذي حكمتم بكونه مانعاً للقادر من الفعل والتأثير. إما أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته أو ممكن الوجود لذاته. فإن كان الأول، امتنع زواله فوجب أن لا تحصل المكنة من الإيجاد البتة. وإن كان الثاني كان ممكن الارتفاع نظراً إليه، من حيث إنه هو، لو كان ممتنع الارتفاع من حيث هو هو، لصار واجباً. وقد فرضنا ممكناً لذاته، وإذا كان ممكن الارتفاع من حيث هو هو، فبتقدير ارتفاعه، كان القادر متمكناً من الإيجاد والإحداث دائماً وقد فرضنا: أن ذلك محال: فثبت: أن القول بالأزل مانع من حصول هذه المكنة قول يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة. فوجب كونه باطلا. وأيضاً: فحصول ذلك المانع لما كان ممكناً لذاته، وجب أن يكون لوجود مؤثر. وذلك المؤثر إما موجب بالذات، أو فاعل بالاختيار. فإن كان الأول لزم من دوام ذلك الموجب دوام هذا المانع، ويلزم من دوام هذا المانع، دوام الامتناع. وإن كان الثاني فنقول كل ما كان فعلاً لفاعل مختار، لم يكن أزلياً. فيلزم أن يقال: إن مسمى الأزل لا يكون أزلياً، وذلك محال.
The third aspect in refuting this question: that we say: the referent of 'eternity' (al-azal), which you have judged to be an obstacle preventing the powerful from act and effect, is either necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) or contingent of existence by itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi). If it is the first, its ceasing is impossible, so capability of bringing into existence must not be obtained at all. If it is the second, it is possible to be removed with respect to itself, because if it were impossible to be removed in itself, it would become necessary. And we have assumed it contingent by itself. And if it is possible to be removed in itself, then assuming its removal, the powerful would be capable of bringing into existence and originating perpetually. And we have assumed that that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that eternity is an obstacle preventing the obtaining of this capability is a statement that leads to false conclusions. So it must be false. Also, the occurrence of that obstacle, since it is contingent by itself, must have an existence from an efficient cause (mu'aththir). That efficient cause is either necessitating by essence (mujib bi al-dhat) or free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). If it is the first, from the perpetuity of that necessitating cause follows the perpetuity of this obstacle, and from the perpetuity of this obstacle follows the perpetuity of prevention. If it is the second, we say: everything that is an act of a free agent is not eternal. So it necessarily follows that the referent of 'eternity' is not eternal, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the third aspect of the refutation, which analyzes the very essence of 'eternity' (al-azal) that is claimed to be the obstacle. The author argues that the referent of 'eternity' (musamma al-azal) must be either necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) or contingent of existence by itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi). These are the two categories of existence in kalam. If it is necessary, then its ceasing is impossible, so the capability (al-makina) of bringing into existence can never be obtained, which is absurd. If it is contingent, then it is possible for it to be removed (irtifa') in itself. Its removal would imply that the powerful (al-qadir) would then be capable of creating perpetually (da'iman), which was assumed impossible. Thus, the claim that eternity is the obstacle leads to false conclusions. The author then provides an additional sub-argument: since the obstacle (al-mani') is contingent in itself, it must have an efficient cause (mu'aththir). That cause is either a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat) or a free agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). If it is necessitating, its perpetuity necessitates the perpetuity of the obstacle, hence perpetual prevention. If it is free, then its act cannot be eternal, so the referent of 'eternity' would not be eternal, a contradiction. Consequently, the notion that eternity is an obstacle is false.

Key Terms
وجه (al-wajh): aspect
مسمى الأزل (musamma al-azal): referent of eternity
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud): necessary of existence
ممكن الوجود (mumkin al-wujud): contingent of existence
زوال (zawal): ceasing
ارتفاع (irtifa'): removal
مؤثر (mu'aththir): efficient cause
موجب بالذات (mujib bi al-dhat): necessitating by essence
فاعل بالاختيار (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar): free agent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'eternity' (al-azal) is sometimes misunderstood as a state that can be an obstacle.
Correction: The passage analyzes it as a referent that must be either necessary or contingent, showing both possibilities lead to false conclusions if claimed as an obstacle.

Checkpoint
What is the first option considered for the referent of 'eternity' in this argument?
A) It is contingent.
B) It is necessary.
C) It is eternal.
D) It is temporary.

Key Takeaway
Eternity cannot be an obstacle because claiming it leads to false conclusions whether its referent is necessary or contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that nothing—not even time or eternity—can limit God's power, why do you still act as if some things are impossible for Him in your life? What specific situation are you treating as an 'obstacle' that His power cannot remove?
?
The proof shows that claiming an obstacle leads to absurdity. Do you ever make excuses for God's actions, implying He is constrained by something? How might that be a subtle form of limiting His sovereignty, and what would it mean to stop doing that?
?
This argument forces you to see eternity not as a barrier but as compatible with God's free act of creation. How does that change the way you think about your own place in time? Are you willing to live with the urgency of now because God is eternally free to act?

The Fourth Aspect: Conceding Absence of Power in Eternity
The author argues that the opponent's distinction does not avoid the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conceding the absence of capability in eternity, regardless of the reason, still supports the desired conclusion.

Core Idea
Admitting the absence of power in eternity is enough, regardless of the reason.

In this fourth argument, the author refines the point: even if the opponent says the lack of power is due to an obstacle rather than absence of requisite, the core admission remains the same. Let's see how this subtle distinction doesn't change the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'al-muqtada' (requisite) and 'al-mani'' (obstacle).
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice that the opponent says the absence of capability is due to an obstacle, not the absence of a requisite. This is their attempted distinction.
3
Understand the author's response
The author argues that in both cases (absence of requisite or presence of obstacle), it is admitted that the capability is not present. This admission supports the author's desired conclusion.
The distinction is irrelevant to the core admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع: وهو أن حاصل ما ذكرتم، يرجع إلى أن المكنة والقدرة على الإيجاد، ما كانت حاصلة في الأزل. إلا أنكم تقولون: إن عدم المكنة ليس لعدم المقتضى، لكن لقيام المانع. إلا أن على كلا التقديرين، ففيه تسلیم أن هذه المكنة غير حاصل وذلك مساعدة على المطلوب.
The fourth aspect: that the summary of what you have mentioned reduces to the fact that the capability and the power to bring into existence were not present in eternity. However, you say that the absence of capability is not due to the absence of the requisite (al-muqtada), but due to the presence of the obstacle (al-mani'). But in both cases, there is in it an admission that this capability is not obtained, and that is assistance to the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
In this fourth aspect, the author points out that the opponent's argument ultimately concedes that the capability (al-makina) and power (al-qudra) to bring into existence were not present in eternity (al-azal). The opponent tries to differentiate by saying that the absence of capability is not due to the absence of the requisite (muqtada), but due to the presence of the obstacle (mani'). However, the author argues that in both cases—whether due to absence of requisite or presence of obstacle—there is an admission (taslim) that the capability is not present. And this admission is in fact assistance (musa'ada) to the very point the author is trying to prove (al-matlub), which is that the opponent's position does not truly negate the required conclusion. Thus, the distinction is irrelevant.

Correction: Both concede that capability was not present in eternity, which is the key admission; the reason does not matter.

Checkpoint

A) That the obstacle is eternal.
B) That the capability is not present in eternity.

D) That God is not powerful.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's admission that capability was not present in eternity, for any reason, supports the desired conclusion.

Refutation of the Second Objection: The Necessitating Cause vs. Free Agent
The author refutes the second objection by distinguishing necessary causation from free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that an eternal cause must necessitate an eternal effect does not apply to a free agent who acts by intention and choice.

Core Idea
Free agency in eternity is not comparable to necessary causation.

The author now addresses a second objection that tries to equate God's eternal will with a necessitating cause. He shows that while an eternal necessitating cause would indeed produce an eternal effect, a free agent acting by choice in eternity is a different rational category. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection claims an eternal cause must produce an eternal effect, so the world should be eternal.
The objection is called 'al-mu'arada bil-mujib'.
2
Understand the distinction
The author separates two cases: the effect of a necessitating cause is rational, but a free agent acting in eternity is not rational. Identify these two phrases in the Arabic.
The key terms are 'علة موجبة' and 'مختار'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction invalidates the objection. Why is the free agent case 'not rational'?
Free agency involves intention and choice, which introduce a contingent relation to the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني وهو المعارضة بالموجب، فساقط. وذلك لأن المعلول بدوام العلة الموجبة: أمر معقول غير منكر. أما كون المختار موجداً بالقصد والاختيار، لما كان موجوداً في الأزل، فهو غير معقول فظهر الفرق بين البابين والله أعلم
As for the second question, which is the objection by [the argument of] the necessitating cause, it is invalid. That is because the effect by the perpetuation of the necessitating cause is a rational matter, not denied. As for that the free agent is a bringer into existence by intention and choice while being existent in eternity, that is not rational. So the difference between the two cases is clear, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage responds to a second objection (al-su'al al-thani) that appeals to the notion of a necessitating cause (al-mujib) to argue that an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect. The author declares this objection invalid (sāqit). The reason is that it is rationally acceptable that an effect (ma'lul) follows from the perpetuation (dawam) of a necessitating cause (illa mujiba) – that is, if the cause is eternal and necessitates perpetually, the effect is also eternal. However, the case of a free agent (al-mukhtar) bringing something into existence by intention (qasd) and choice (ikhtiyar) while being existent in eternity (al-azal) is not rational (ghayr ma'qul). In other words, the notion of a free agent acting in eternity in a way that produces a temporal effect is conceptually different from a necessary cause producing an eternal effect. Therefore, the objection fails, as the two cases are distinct. The author concludes that the difference between the two categories (al-babayn) is clear.

Key Terms

معارضة
موجب
معلول
علة موجبة
مختار
قصد
اختيار
أزل
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal cause must produce an eternal effect, so God cannot create temporally.
Correction: While a necessitating cause does produce an eternal effect, a free agent acting by intention and choice is rationally different; thus the objection fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the second objection invalid?
A) Because a necessitating cause cannot produce an effect.
B) Because the effect of a necessitating cause is rational, but a free agent acting in eternity is not rational.
C) Because God is not a free agent.
D) Because the objection admits that capability is absent.

Key Takeaway
The key is to recognize that the necessity of an eternal cause does not apply to a free agent, because intention and choice introduce a different rational dynamic.

Fourth Proof: Equal Ability to Act or Refrain is Impossible
The author states the claim of the fourth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim of the fourth proof—that divine power cannot be equal ability to act or refrain—and why this claim is a necessary step in demonstrating God's perfection.

Core Idea
God's power cannot be equal capacity for opposites; that would imply potential imperfection.

This lesson introduces the fourth proof in a series of arguments about divine power. The author states upfront what he aims to prove: that power defined as equal ability to either act or refrain is impossible for God. Although the full argument is not included here due to a page break, the claim itself challenges a common view and sets the stage for a deeper theological reasoning.

1

2

4

Two Differences Between Voluntary and Necessary Agents
Author distinguishes two differences between voluntary and necessary agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key differences between a voluntary agent and a necessary cause in the created world, and which difference applies to God.

Core Idea
Voluntary agents act through changing motives and awareness; natural causes are uniform and unaware.

This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah distinguishes two differences between voluntary agents (like humans) and necessary natural causes. The first concerns the variability of motives; the second concerns knowledge and awareness. The author then applies this to God, showing that only the second difference is relevant because change cannot be attributed to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice how the author uses concrete examples (heating, cooling, motion).
2
Identify the first difference
The author explains that natural causes produce uniform effects, while voluntary agents act based on changing motives (dawāʿī). Find the example of motion right/left.
The first difference is about constancy vs. changeability.
3
Identify the second difference
The second difference is that natural causes lack awareness, while voluntary agents act through knowledge and desire.
This is explicitly about شُعور (awareness) and عِلْم (knowledge).
4
Apply to God
The author argues that since change in God's essence and attributes is impossible, only the second difference (knowledge and will) applies to Him.
This is a crucial theological move: God is voluntary but unchanging.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding divine action: God acts with knowledge and will, but not with changing motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمؤثر في التسخين، يبقى موصوفاً أبداً، بالوجه الذي لأجله كان موجباً للتسخين، ولا يتغير عنه، وكذلك القول فيما يؤثر في التبريد، بخلاف الأحوال الاختيارية، فإن الموجب للحركة يمنة، هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي إلى تلك الحركة المخصوصة والموجب للحركة يسرة، هو مجموع القدرة مع حصول الداعي إلى الحركة يسرة، وهذه الدواعي سريعة التبدل والتغير، فالقادر إذا حصل في قلبه داعية الحركة يمنة، صارت القدرة تلك الداعية، موجباً للحركة يمنة. ثم إن تلك الداعية تتغير سريعاً، وتحصل الداعية إلى الحركة يسرة. فيصير الآن هذا المجموع موجباً للحركة يسرة. فالحاصل: أن في الأمور الطبيعية، ما لأجله صار موجباً للتسخين، يبقى ولا يتغير. وأما في الأمور الاختيارية، فتلك الاختيارات سريعة التبدل والتغير. فهذا هو أحد الفرقين بين المؤثر المختار، وبين المؤثر الموجب، في الشاهد.
وأما الفرق الثاني فهو أن المؤثرات الطبيعية، لا شعور لها بما يصدر عنها، وأما المؤثرات التي تؤثر بالاختيار، فهي إنما تؤثر بواسطة العلم والشعور، فإنه ما لم يحصل تصور أن ذلك الفعل يفيد نفعاً وخيراً لم تحصل الرغبة فيه. ولهذا السبب، سمي هذا الفعل فعلا اختيارياً، وسمي الفاعل فاعلاً مختاراً.
فهذان الوجهان يوجبان الفرق بين الموجب بالذات وبين الفاعل المختار. وأما في حق الله تعالى، فالتغير عليه في ذاته وفي صفاته: محال. فلم يبق الفرق المعتبر، إلا الوجه الأخير والله أعلم.
The agent of influence in heating remains constantly qualified by the aspect for which it was a necessitator of heating, and does not change from it. Likewise for what influences cooling. This is unlike voluntary states, for the necessitator of motion to the right is the combination of power with the motive to that specific motion, and the necessitator of motion to the left is the combination of power with the occurrence of the motive to motion to the left. These motives are quick to change and alter. So when a capable agent (al-qadir) has in his heart a motive for motion to the right, that power together with that motive becomes a necessitator of motion to the right. Then that motive changes quickly, and the motive for motion to the left arises. Now this combination becomes a necessitator of motion to the left. The conclusion: in natural matters, what made something a necessitator of heating remains and does not change. As for voluntary matters, those choices are quick to change and alter. This is one of the two differences between a voluntary agent (al-mu'aththir al-mukhtar) and a necessitating agent (al-mu'aththir al-mujib) in the witnessed realm. As for the second difference: natural causers have no awareness of what issues from them, while causers that act by choice only act through knowledge and awareness, for until there occurs a conception that that action brings benefit and good, desire for it does not occur. For this reason, this action is called a voluntary act, and the agent is called a voluntary agent. These two aspects necessitate the difference between the necessitator by essence (al-mujib bi-dhatihi) and the voluntary agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar). As for the case of God Almighty, change in His essence and His attributes is impossible. So the only valid difference remains the last aspect. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents two differences between a necessary cause (natural agent) and a voluntary agent, as observed in the created world. The author first describes the first difference: natural causes produce their effects uniformly without variation, whereas voluntary agents act based on changing motives (dawa'i). The second difference is that natural causes lack awareness of their effects, while voluntary agents act through knowledge and desire. The author then applies this to God: since change is impossible in God, only the second difference (knowledge and will) is relevant. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine action as voluntary yet not subject to change.

Key Terms
المؤثر المختار
المؤثر الموجب
الموجب بالذات
الفاعل المختار
الداعي
القدرة
العلم
الشعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first difference (changing motives) applies to God as well.
Correction: Change in essence and attributes is impossible for God, so only the second difference (knowledge and will) distinguishes Him.

Checkpoint
What is the second difference between a voluntary agent and a natural cause?
A) Natural causes have awareness, while voluntary agents do not.
B) Voluntary agents act through knowledge and awareness, while natural causes lack awareness.
C) Natural causes are changeable, while voluntary agents are permanent.
D) Voluntary agents act without any knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary agents differ from natural causes in having changing motives and awareness; for God, only the awareness/will aspect is relevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that human motives change quickly. When was the last time you changed your intention for an action from something good to something vain? What does that tell you about the state of your heart, and what will you do to guard it?
?
The author says that God does not change in His essence or attributes. Does your prayer reflect a belief in an unchanging God, or do you sometimes act as if His response depends on your varying moods? Commit to one specific change in your supplication today.
?
Natural causes have no awareness of what they do; you do. Have you ever acted without really thinking about the benefit or harm of the action? Describe one area of your life where you are acting like a natural cause, and resolve to bring knowledge and intention back into that action starting now.

The Fourth Proof: Divine Attributes Necessitate Events
Author argues that divine attributes necessitate events, negating equal ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that God's power cannot include equal ability to act or refrain, based on the unchanging nature of His attributes.

Core Idea
Unchanging divine attributes necessitate events, so equal ability is impossible.

This lesson examines a key proof from kalam theology that challenges the common notion that power requires equal ability to act or refrain. The author argues that God's eternal attributes—will, power, and knowledge—collectively necessitate the occurrence of specific events, leaving no room for equal possibility. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Islamic theological position on divine determinism and the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Recall the theological premise
Remember that theologians hold that change cannot occur in God's attributes and that non-existence is impossible for the Eternal. This is the foundation of the argument.
If this premise is unfamiliar, review the attributes of God in kalam.
2
Analyze the hypothetical scenario
Consider what follows if God's will, power, and knowledge all concur on a particular event at a particular time. The text asks whether it could then fail to occur. Walk through the two possibilities and why the first is rejected.
Pay attention to the three attributes mentioned: will, power, knowledge.
3
Understand why non-occurrence is impossible
If the event did not occur, it would imply a change in God's attributes—a new will would arise, knowledge would become ignorance—all impossible. Therefore, the event must necessarily occur.
This necessity is not compulsion but logical consequence of eternal attributes.
4
Draw the conclusion about power
Since the event's occurrence is necessary, God cannot have equal ability to act or refrain. His power results in necessary effects, not contingent possibilities.
This refutes the view that a capable agent must be able to do or not do an action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: في بيان أن القدرة بمعنى حصول المكنة من الفعل والترك بالسوية: محال.
أن نقول: أليس من مذهب المتكلمين: أن التغير يمتنع في صفات الله تعالى، وأن العدم على القديم: محال. فإذا كانت إرادة الله تعالى، متعلقة من الأزل إلى الأبد، بترجيح وجود الحادث المعين على عدمه، وكانت قدرته من الأزل إلى الأبد متعلقة بإيجاد ذلك الحادث المعين في ذلك الوقت المعين، وكان علمه متعلقاً بوقوع ذلك الحادث المعين، في الوقت المعين. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إن مع حصول هذه الصفات الثلاثة واجتماعها على اقتضاء وقوع ذلك الحادث، إما أن يمكن أن لا يقع ذلك الفعل، وإما لا يمكن. والأول باطل، لأن ذلك يقدح في كون هذه الصفات مؤثرة في وقوع ذلك الفعل، ولأنه يلزم وقوع التغير في صفات الله تعالى، فإنه تعالى إذا أراد شيئاً آخر، غير ما أراده في الأزل، لزم زوال تلك الإرادة الأزلية وحدثت إرادة أخرى. وأيضاً: لزم أن ينقلب ذلك العلم جهلاً، وكل ذلك: محال. فيثبت: أن تغير تلك الصفات عقلاً، وأن عدم وقوع ذلك الحادث في الوقت المعين الذي تطابقت الصفات الثلاثة على اقتضاء وقوعه فيه، ممتنع عقلاً. وهذا يقدح في قولهم: إن شرط القادر: أن يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك، بل على هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يقال: إنه تعالى يؤثر في الأفعال على سبيل الوجوب، لا على سبيل الإمكان، المتردد بين الوجود والعدم.
The Fourth Proof: Demonstrating that power (al-qudra) in the sense of having the ability to both act and refrain equally is impossible. We say: Is it not from the position of the theologians (al-mutakallimun) that change is impossible in the attributes of God Most High, and that non-existence is impossible for the Eternal? So if the will of God Most High is related, from pre-eternity to eternity, to preferring the existence of a particular originated thing over its non-existence, and His power is related from pre-eternity to eternity to bringing that particular originated thing into existence at that specific time, and His knowledge is related to the occurrence of that particular originated thing at that specific time — if this is established, we say: given the occurrence of these three attributes and their consensus on necessitating the occurrence of that event, either it is possible that that act does not occur, or it is not possible. The first is false, because that would impugn these attributes being effective in the occurrence of that act, and because it would necessitate the occurrence of change in the attributes of God Most High. For if He wills something other than what He willed in pre-eternity, that pre-eternal will would have to cease and another will arise. Also, that knowledge would have to turn into ignorance. All of that is impossible. Therefore it is established that change in those attributes is rationally impossible, and that the non-occurrence of that event at the specific time that the three attributes have concurred on necessitating its occurrence is rationally impossible. This impugns their statement that the condition of the capable agent (al-qadir) is that he be able to both act and refrain. Rather, on this basis, one must say that He Most High influences actions in the manner of necessity (wujub), not in the manner of possibility (imkan) which is undecided between existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This proof argues that God's power cannot mean equal ability to act or refrain because God's attributes are eternal and unchanging. If His will, power, and knowledge all necessitate a specific event, then its non-occurrence would require change in those attributes, which is impossible. Therefore, God's action is necessary (bi-l-wujub) not contingent on equal possibility. The proof targets a common theological claim that power requires ability to do or not do, showing that divine attributes entail determinism.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
التغير
الوجوب
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's power necessitates events, then God is not truly powerful and free.
Correction: The author does not deny God's power or will; rather, he argues that the eternal nature of these attributes means they necessarily produce effects, which is consistent with God's perfection—His will is eternal and unchangingly effective.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that equal ability to act or refrain is impossible for God?
A) Because God is all-powerful.
B) Because God's attributes are unchanging and if they necessitate an event, its non-occurrence would require change.
C) Because God does not have will.
D) Because humans have free will.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal and unchanging attributes necessitate events, so His power does not include equal ability to act or refrain; rather, it acts by logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely believe that every detail of your life—every decision, every mistake—was eternally known, willed, and empowered by God to occur with absolute necessity? If so, how can you still hold yourself accountable for your actions without despairing or becoming passive?
?
When you think of a past sin or failure, do you truly accept that God's knowledge and will perfectly encompassed that moment, and yet you are responsible? What does that mean for your repentance and your trust in God's mercy?
?
If God's actions are necessary and not based on equal ability, how does that reshape your understanding of worship? Are you willing to submit to a God whose will is inescapably effective, even in your own choices?

The Fifth Proof: God's Knowledge Determines Events
Author presents the fifth proof: God's knowledge entails determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof from divine knowledge to determinism, and how it concludes that all events are either necessary or impossible based on God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's perfect knowledge makes the known event unavoidable.

This passage presents one of the most direct arguments for divine determinism (qadar) used by the Ash'ari school. The author takes the fact of God's omniscience and draws a logical conclusion: if God knows something will happen, it must happen; if He knows it won't, it cannot. This is not a proof about God's attribute, but about the implication of that attribute for the world. Let's see how he builds it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Notice the first premise: God knows all things. The second premise: God has specific knowledge of events at specific times.
The author states these without proof as self-evident.
3
Understand the contradiction argument
The author argues that if what God knows to occur fails to occur, that would be a contradiction. Contradictions are rationally impossible.
This is the key logical move.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, what God knows will occur becomes necessary (wajib); what He knows will not occur becomes impossible (mumtani'). Events are not contingent.
This leads to determinism.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider how this proof supports the Ash'ari view of predestination and why it might be controversial for free will.
This proof is often used in debates on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في هذا الباب: أن نقول: لا شك أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات. فوجب أن يعلم أن الشيء الفلاني، يقع في الوقت الفلاني وأن الشيء الفلاني لا يقع في الوقت الفلاني. وخلاف المعلوم: محال الوقوع. لأن عدم وقوع الشيء، مع العلم بوقوعه ضدان، والجمع بين الضدين: محال عقلاً. وإذا كان كذلك، فما علم الله وقوعه، كان واجب الوقوع، وما علم الله عدم وقوعه، كان ممتنع الوقوع.
The Fifth Proof in this chapter: We say: There is no doubt that He Most High knows all known things. It follows that He knows that such a thing will occur at such a time and that such a thing will not occur at such a time. Contradiction of the known is impossible of occurrence. For the non-occurrence of a thing along with knowledge of its occurrence is a contradiction, and combining two contradictories is rationally impossible. If that is so, then what God knows will occur is necessary of occurrence (wajib al-wuqu'), and what God knows will not occur is impossible of occurrence (mumtani' al-wuqu').
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This proof uses divine knowledge to establish determinism. Since God's knowledge is infallible and encompasses all events, any event known to occur must occur; otherwise, knowledge would be false, which is impossible. Therefore, events are either necessary (if God knows they will happen) or impossible (if God knows they won't). This supports the idea that created acts are determined by divine knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
خلاف المعلوم
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge only predicts what will happen, it does not cause it.
Correction: The author argues that God's knowledge makes the event necessary—since contradiction is impossible, the event must occur as known. It is not mere foreknowledge; it is a logical entailment.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of God's knowledge that a thing will occur?
A) The thing becomes possible.
B) The thing becomes impossible.
C) The thing becomes necessary of occurrence (wajib al-wuqu').
D) The thing becomes contingent.

Key Takeaway
God's omniscience logically implies that all events are either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your life—including your sins and mistakes—is either necessary or impossible because of God's knowledge, what does that mean for the regrets you hold onto? Are they truly regrettable, or simply part of a necessary tapestry?
?
When you struggle to obey God, do you ever think, 'It's not written for me'? How does this proof challenge or affirm that thought? Will you use it as an excuse or as a reminder to strive harder?
?
This proof says your choices are not truly contingent; they align with God's knowledge. How does that affect the way you ask Allah for guidance or repentance? Do you still feel the urgency to change?
