Concurrence of Divine Attributes Necessitates Occurrence
Argument from concurrence of divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concurrence of God's will, power, and knowledge necessitates the occurrence of a particular event, and why this does not negate divine power.

Core Idea
When all divine attributes agree, the event must happen.

This argument from the mutakallimūn is a cornerstone of Islamic causal theology. It shows that when God's will, power, and knowledge unite for a single event, that event cannot fail to occur—otherwise, God's attributes would change, which is impossible. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Focus on its flow from premise to conclusion.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'
2
Identify the premises
Find the three premises: (1) change in God's attributes is impossible, (2) God's will, power, and knowledge are eternally directed toward the event, (3) they concur on its occurrence at a specific time.
3
Trace the two possibilities
The argument offers two options: either the event can fail to occur, or it cannot. See how the first is rejected.
Why is the first rejected? Because it implies change in God's attributes.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the event must occur necessarily. This challenges the idea that a capable agent can always do both the act and its opposite.
The argument does not deny God's power; it shows its efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن نقول : أليس من مذهب المتكلمين : أن التغير يمتنع في صفات الله تعالى ، وأن العدم على القديم : محال . فإذا كانت إرادة الله تعالى ، متعلقة من الأزل إلى الأبد ، بترجيح وجود الحادث المعين على عدمه ، وكانت قدرته من الأزل إلى الأبد متعلقة بإيجاد ذلك الحادث المعين في ذلك الوقت المعين ، وكان علمه متعلقاً بوقوع ذلك الحادث المعين، في الوقت المعين . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن مع حصول هذه الصفات الثلاثة واجتماعها على اقتضاء وقوع ذلك الحادث، إما أن يمكن أن لا يقع ذلك الفعل، وإما لا يمكن والأول باطل ، لأن ذلك يقدح في كون هذه الصفات مؤثرة في وقوع ذلك الفعل ، ولأنه يلزم وقوع التغير في صفات الله تعالى فإنه تعالى إذا أراد شيئاً آخر ، غير ما أراده في الأزل ، لزم زوال تلك الإرادة الأزلية وحدثت إرادة أخرى . وأيضاً : لزم أن ينقلب ذلك العلم جهلاً ، وكل ذلك : محال . فيثبت : أن تغير تلك الصفات عقلا ، وإن عدم وقوع ذلك الحادث في الوقت المعين الذي تطابقت الصفات الثلاثة على اقتضاء وقوعه فيه، ممتنع عقلا . وهذا يقدح في قولهم : إن شرط القادر : أن يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك ، بل على هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إنه تعالى يؤثر في الأفعال على سبيل الوجوب ، لا على سبيل الإمكان ، المتردد بين الوجود والعدم
We say: Is it not from the doctrine of the theologians (mutakallimūn) that change is impossible in the attributes of God Most High, and that non-existence with respect to the eternal is impossible? So if the will of God Most High from eternity to eternity is connected with giving preponderance to the existence of a particular contingent event over its non-existence, and His power from eternity to eternity is connected with bringing that particular event into existence at that particular time, and His knowledge is connected with the occurrence of that particular event at that particular time — if this is established, we say: When these three attributes are present and concur on requiring the occurrence of that event, either it is possible that the act does not occur, or it is not possible. The first is false, because it detracts from these attributes being effective in the occurrence of that act, and because it would entail change in the attributes of God Most High: for if He wills something other than what He willed from eternity, then that eternal will would cease and another will would arise. Also, that knowledge would turn into ignorance, and all that is impossible. So it is established that change in those attributes is impossible, and that the non-occurrence of that event at the particular time which the three attributes concurred on requiring its occurrence is impossible. And this undermines their statement that the condition of the powerful agent (al-qādir) is to be capable of both action and omission. Rather, according to this estimation, one must say that He causes effects in acts by way of necessity (wujūb), not by way of possibility (imkān) fluctuating between existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument that if God’s will, power, and knowledge all concur on a particular event, then that event must necessarily occur; otherwise, it would imply change in God’s attributes, which is impossible. The argument begins by stating a premise accepted by theologians: change in God’s attributes is impossible, and non-existence cannot be attributed to the eternal. Then it imagines that God’s will eternally favors the existence of a specific contingent event, His power is eternally connected to its creation at a specific time, and His knowledge eternally knows it will occur at that time. Given the concurrence of these three attributes, the argument considers two possibilities: either the event can fail to occur, or it cannot. The first is rejected because it would negate the efficacy of these attributes (they would not actually necessitate the event) and would entail change in God (a new will, a different knowledge, etc.). Since change is impossible, the non-occurrence of that event is impossible. This conclusion challenges the view that a capable agent must be able to do both the act and its opposite; rather, God acts necessarily, not on the basis of possibility.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
قدرة (qudra)
علم (ʿilm)
وجوب (wujūb)
إمكان (imkān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies God's ability to do otherwise or that it implies God is not truly powerful.
Correction: The argument does not deny God's power; it shows that when all attributes are perfectly aligned, the outcome is determined by those attributes. God's power remains infinite and effective.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of this argument?
A) God can change His will.
B) The concurrence of divine attributes makes the occurrence of an event necessary.
C) God acts by pure possibility.
D) The three attributes are not always present.

Key Takeaway
When God's will, power, and knowledge concur on an event, that event must necessarily occur, because any alternative would imply a change in God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that God's will, power, and knowledge concur on every event in your life—including the ones you dread—does that fill you with peace or unease? What does your emotional response reveal about your trust in His attributes?
?
If the non-occurrence of any divinely willed event is impossible, then why do you still struggle to accept certain outcomes as decreed? What would it look like for you to truly surrender to that reality today?
?
The passage demands that God's act is necessary, not merely possible. How does that challenge your assumption that you could have changed something by your own effort? Will you thank Him even for the painful events knowing they were necessitated by His perfect attributes?

Fifth Argument: Divine Knowledge Necessitates Occurrence
Argument for necessity from omniscience

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from God's omniscience leads to the conclusion that all events are necessitated.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of events makes them necessarily occur.

This lesson presents a key argument from the Ashʿari tradition for causal necessity based on God's omniscience. The author argues that since God knows all events infallibly, what He knows to occur must occur, implying necessity. By engaging directly with the source passage, you will see the logical steps that lead to this conclusion and its implications for divine power and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style: the argument is presented in a few dense sentences.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into its premises and conclusion. Premise: God knows all. Premise: contradiction is impossible. Conclusion: events known to occur must occur. Implication: no equal capacity.
This is a classic syllogistic argument.
3
Understand the contradiction
The argument hinges on the impossibility of combining knowledge of an event's occurrence with its non-occurrence. Recognize this as a rational impossibility, not a physical one.
4
Consider the implication for free will
Ask yourself: if every event is necessitated by God's knowledge, what room is left for human choice? The author explicitly says equal capacity is impossible. Reflect on this.
This connects to broader debates about free will and determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في هذا الباب : أن نقول : لا شك أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات . فوجب أن يعلم أن الشيء الفلاني ، يقع في الوقت الفلاني وأن الشيء الفلاني لا يقع في الوقت الفلاني وخلاف المعلوم : محال الوقوع . لأن عدم وقوع الشيء ، مع العلم بوقوعه ضدان ، والجمع بين الضدين : محال عقلاً . وإذا كان كذلك ، فما علم الله وقوعه ، كان واجب الوقوع ، وما علم عدمه ، كان ممتنع الوقوع ، وهذا يقتضي أن يقال : إنه تعالى يؤثر في بعض الماهيات على سبيل الوجوب ، ويمتنع كونه مؤثراً في بعض الماهيات على سبيل الوجوب . فأما المكنة من الفعل والترك على سبيل السوية فهي ممتنعة الحصول .
The fifth proof in this chapter: We say: There is no doubt that He, Most High, is knower of all known things. So it is necessary that He knows that a certain thing occurs at a certain time and that a certain thing does not occur at a certain time. And what contradicts the known is impossible of occurrence. For non-occurrence of a thing, together with knowledge of its occurrence, are two opposites, and combining two opposites is rationally impossible. If this is so, then what God knows to occur is necessarily occurring, and what He knows not to occur is impossible of occurrence. This necessitates saying that He, Most High, causes some essences (māhiyyāt) by way of necessity, and it is impossible that He causes some essences by way of necessity. As for the capacity for action and omission equally, it is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for divine necessitation, based on God's omniscience. The argument begins with the premise that God knows all things, including which events will occur at specific times and which will not. To say that an event occurs but God knew it would not (or vice versa) would involve a contradiction between knowledge and reality, which is impossible. Therefore, whatever God knows to occur must necessarily occur, and whatever He knows not to occur must be impossible. This leads to the conclusion that God causes effects by necessity, not by equal capability for both action and omission. The capacity for both (al-makna min al-fiʿl wa al-tark ʿalā sabīl al-sawiyya) is thus impossible.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm)
معلوم (maʿlūm)
وجوب (wujūb)
امتناع (imtināʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument means God is forced to act in a certain way, limiting His power.
Correction: The argument does not limit God's power; it emphasizes the logical consistency between His knowledge and reality. God's knowledge is eternal and infallible, so what He knows must occur—not because He is compelled, but because His knowledge is perfect.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument conclude that God acts by necessity?
A) Because God has no power to do otherwise.
B) Because His knowledge makes the occurrence of events certain.
C) Because theologians defined power incorrectly.
D) Because the event itself is necessary.

Key Takeaway
God's omniscience entails that all events are necessitated, leaving no room for equal capacity for action and omission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge makes every event in your life necessary, does that make you feel trapped or liberated? Honestly examine why you feel that way, and what it reveals about your trust in God's plan.
?
Think of a moment when you desperately wished something had gone differently. Knowing that God knew it would happen exactly that way, can you say 'alhamdulillah' without reservation? If not, what is holding you back?
?
The argument says there is no equal capacity for action and omission. How does this challenge your sense of personal responsibility? Are you willing to take full responsibility for your choices while affirming that they were known and necessitated by God?

The Contradiction of the Object of Power
Razi presents the sixth argument leading to contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core contradiction in the sixth argument: the determination of the object of power must be both prior and posterior to the attachment of power, making the opponents' definition of a capable agent impossible.

Core Idea
The object of power must be both before and after the agent's power—a contradiction.

In this lesson, we will examine Razi's sixth argument against the Ash'arite definition of a capable agent. Razi demonstrates that their definition leads to an impossible temporal contradiction. We will walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its translation. Notice how Razi states the contradiction at the outset.
The argument is a proof by contradiction (taql al-munaqada).
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) The object of power must be distinguished prior to the attachment of power. (2) The object of power is an effect of power, so its determination must be posterior. Find these in the passage.
These lead to the conclusion that the same thing must be both prior and posterior.
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why this is a contradiction. The opponents' definition of a capable agent cannot hold because it requires the object of power to have contradictory temporal properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: أن نقول: القول يكون المؤثر قادراً بالتفسير الذي يذكرونه، يفضي إلى التناقض، فكان القول به باطلا. وإنما قلنا: إنه يفضي إلى التناقض. وذلك لأن كون القادر قادراً على المقدور، موقوف على التمييز ذلك المقدور عن غيره، لأنه لولا سبق هذا الامتياز وإلا لامتنع القصد على إيجاد بعض المقدورات دون بعض. فهذا يقتضي أن يكون تعين ماهية المقدور، وامتيازها عن غيرها: سابقاً على تعلق تلك القدرة بها. وأيضاً: أن المقدور وإنما يكون مقدوراً، لو كان وقوعه وحصوله بالقدرة. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تعينه وتميزه عن غيره، متأخراً عن تعلق تلك القدرة به. وإذا كان كذلك، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون تعين المقدور وتميزه عن غيره، سابقاً على تعلق تلك القدرة به، ومتأخراً عن تعلق تلك القدرة: محالاً. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: ماهية المقدور متقدمة على تعلق القدرة به، ووجوده متأخر عن تعلق القدرة به كما هو مذهب القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء. وأيضاً: فهذا الذي أوردتموه في القادر، قائم بعينه في الموجب. والجواب عن الأول: إنه إذا كانت الماهية مفتقرة إلى الوجود والعدم وحال الوجود كانت ممتنعة الفناء والزوال في نفسها، لم تكن الماهية مقدورة البتة، بل كان المقدور، إما الوجود وإما جعل الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، وحينئذ يعود ما ذكرناه، من أن هذا المعنى، لكونه متعلق القدرة يجب تقدمه على تعليق القدرة به. ومن حيث إنه أثر لتلك القدرة، واقع بها يجب تأخره عن القدرة وحينئذ يلزم المحال المذكور. والجواب عن السؤال الثاني: إن الفرق بين الموجب والقادر: أن القادر هو الذي يصح منه إيجاد هذا، بدلاً عن ذاك، وبالضد. فلولا امتياز بعض هذه الماهيات عن بعض، قبل دخولها في الوجود، وإلا لما صح أن يقال: إنه يقدر على إيجاد هذا بدلاً عن ذاك فهذا يقتضي أن يكون تعين تلك الماهيات، متقدماً على تعلق القدرة بها. ولما كان المقدور أثراً لتلك القدرة، لزم كونها متأخرة عن تلك القدرة. أما الموجب، فإنه لا تأثير له، إلا وقع الأثر به، ولا يقتضي أن يكون تعيين تلك الماهيات، سابقاً على كونه مقتضياً لها. فظهر الفرق.
The sixth argument: That we say: The statement that the effective agent is capable according to the interpretation they mention leads to contradiction, so the statement is invalid. And we only say that it leads to contradiction because the fact that the capable is capable of the object of power depends on distinguishing that object of power from others, because without this distinction preceding, intending to bring about some objects of power without others would be impossible. This requires that the determination of the quiddity of the object of power and its distinction from others be prior to the attachment of that power to it. And also: The object of power is only an object of power if its occurrence and existence are by power. This requires that its determination and distinction from others be posterior to the attachment of that power to it. And if this is so, then this requires that the determination of the object of power and its distinction from others be prior to the attachment of that power to it, and posterior to the attachment of that power: impossible. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the quiddity of the object of power is prior to the attachment of power to it, and its existence is posterior to the attachment of power to it, as is the doctrine of those who say that non-existence is something? And also: This very objection you have raised against the capable applies equally to the necessitating. The answer to the first: If the quiddity is in need of existence and non-existence, and in the state of existence it is impossible of annihilation and removal in itself, then the quiddity is not an object of power at all. Rather, the object of power is either existence or the making of the quiddity qualified by existence. Then what we mentioned returns, that this meaning, because it is the object of power, must be prior to the attachment of power to it, and because it is an effect of that power, occurring by it, must be posterior to the power. Then the aforementioned impossibility follows. The answer to the second question: The difference between the necessitating and the capable is that the capable is the one from whom it is possible to bring about this instead of that, and conversely. But for the distinction of some of these quiddities from others before their entrance into existence, it would not be correct to say that he is able to bring about this instead of that. This requires that the determination of those quiddities be prior to the attachment of power to them. And since the object of power is an effect of that power, it necessarily follows that it is posterior to that power. As for the necessitating, it has no effect except that the effect occurs by it, and it does not require that the determination of those quiddities be prior to its being a requisite for them. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument (al-hujja al-sadisah) against the claim that the Agent (al-Qadir) acts necessarily or that His power is limited. The argument is a proof by contradiction (taql al-munaqada). The author begins by stating that saying the effective agent is 'capable' under the opponents' interpretation leads to contradiction, so their claim is false. The contradiction arises because for something to be an object of power (maqdur), it must be distinguished from other possibilities. This distinction (imtiyaz) must be prior to the attachment of power (ta'allaq al-qudra) to it, otherwise the agent could not intend it specifically. Yet at the same time, the object of power only becomes actual by the power, so its determination (ta'ayyun) must be posterior to that attachment. Thus the same thing would have to be both prior and posterior—a contradiction. The author then addresses two objections. The first objection suggests that the quiddity (mahiya) could be prior and its existence posterior, as per the view that non-existence is a thing. The answer is that if the quiddity itself is not an object of power, only its existence or the qualification of the quiddity by existence, then the same dilemma recurs. The second objection states that the same reasoning applies to the necessitating agent (mujib). The answer distinguishes the two: the capable agent could have done something else instead, so the quiddities must be already distinct before the act, while the necessitating agent has no such alternative—the effect follows necessarily without requiring prior distinction. Thus the difference is clear.

Key Terms
قادر (qadir)
مقدور (maqdur)
تعين (ta'ayyun)
امتياز (imtiyaz)
تعلق القدرة (ta'allaq al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is circular because it assumes the prior distinction of quiddities, but Razi's point is that this assumption is necessary for intentional agency, which the opponents accept.
Correction: The argument does not assume the conclusion; it shows that the opponents' own commitment to intentional agency leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what contradiction arises from saying the agent is 'capable' in the opponents' sense?
A) The agent would have to be both finite and infinite.
B) The determination of the object of power would have to be both prior and posterior to the attachment of power.
C) Power would have to be both eternal and created.
D) The object of power would have to be both existent and non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument reveals that the opponents' definition of a capable agent forces the object of power to have contradictory temporal properties, rendering the definition invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the opponents' definition leads to a logical impossibility. Are there any beliefs you hold about God's attributes that you have never tested for internal consistency? Are you willing to abandon a comfortable belief if it leads to contradiction?
?
This argument forces you to consider that for power to be directed, the object must be distinguished prior. Do you think of God's power as operating through such logical steps, or do you assume it bypasses all distinctions? How does that affect your trust in divine wisdom?
?
If the opponents' definition fails due to a contradiction, what does that say about the importance of precise theological language in your own faith? Are you content with vague or emotionally satisfying descriptions of God, or do you pursue rigorous understanding even when it is uncomfortable?

Objections and Answers to the Sixth Argument
Razi responds to two objections against the sixth argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the sixth argument by responding to two key objections: one about the quiddity being prior to power, and one about the argument applying equally to necessitating agents.

Core Idea
The quiddity is not the object of power, and necessitating agents differ from capable agents in requiring prior distinction.

In this lesson, we will continue with the sixth argument. Razi addresses two objections that challenge his reasoning. Pay close attention to how he distinguishes between the quiddity and existence, and between capable and necessitating agents.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and its answer
The first objection suggests that the quiddity could be prior to power and its existence posterior. Notice how Razi responds by clarifying that the quiddity itself is not the object of power.
This draws on the doctrine that non-being (ma'dum) is a thing.
2
Read the second objection and its answer
The second objection claims the same reasoning applies to necessitating agents (mujib). Razi's answer explains the difference: a capable agent could have done otherwise, requiring prior distinction, whereas a necessitating agent cannot.
3
Summarize the key distinctions
Reflect on two distinctions: (1) between quiddity and existence, and (2) between capable and necessitating agents. These are crucial for understanding the argument.

Source Text

The Seventh Argument: Leaving Cannot Be an Object of Power
Razi argues that leaving cannot be an object of power because it is non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that 'leaving' (tark) cannot be an object of power because it is non-existence and remaining, and how this transforms a capable agent into a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Leaving is non-existence; power is for producing existence, so leaving cannot be an object of power.

This passage presents one of Razi's arguments against the view that an agent must have power over both the act and its abandonment. Razi argues that 'leaving' is merely remaining in the original state of non-existence, which by definition cannot be produced by power. This challenges our understanding of free will and leads to the conclusion that a truly capable agent would be a necessitating cause. Read carefully to follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. The main claim is that 'leaving' cannot be an object of power. Look for the reason given: because it is non-existence and remaining.
Note the term 'al-'adam al-asli' (original non-existence).
2
Understand the definition of 'leaving'
The passage defines leaving as remaining upon the original non-existence. This is key: leaving is not a positive act but the absence of action.
3
Follow the logical implication
If leaving is non-existence, it cannot be produced by power (qudra), which brings about existence. Also, remaining cannot be produced by power. Therefore, the agent has no power over leaving, only over the act.
This leads to the agent being a 'mujib' (necessitating cause).
4
Consider the objection and reflect on incompleteness
The passage ends with an objection from someone who says 'We do not say that leaving...' but the answer is missing. Think about what the answer might be based on the argument so far.
This passage is incomplete; the full argument would include a response.

Source Text

الحجة السابعة: إذا قلنا القادر لا بد وأن يقدر على الفعل والترك، لزم كون الترك مقدوراً. وهذا محال لأن الترك عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي، وكونه عدماً، ينافي كونه مقدوراً. وكونه باقياً، ينافي أيضاً كونه مقدوراً. فالعدم الأصلي الباقي، يمتنع كونه مقدوراً. وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أن القادر لا قدرة له البتة على الترك، وليس له صلاحية الإيجاد والتأثير. وعلى هذا التقدير، فإنه ينقلب القادر موجباً. فإن قال قائل: نحن لا نقول: الترك
The seventh argument: If we say that the capable must be able to perform and to leave, then leaving becomes an object of power. And this is impossible because leaving is an expression of remaining upon the original non-existence, and its being non-existence contradicts its being an object of power, and its being remaining also contradicts its being an object of power. So the remaining original non-existence is impossible to be an object of power. And if this is established, it becomes clear that the capable has no power at all over leaving, and he has no capability of bringing into existence and effecting. And upon this estimation, the capable turns into a necessitating cause. Then if someone says: We do not say that leaving...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument (al-hujja al-sabi'ah) against the view that the agent (al-Qadir) must have power over both the act (fi'l) and its abandonment (tark). The argument asserts that if the agent must be capable of both act and leave, then 'leaving' would have to be an object of power (maqdur). But this is impossible because leaving is merely remaining in the original state of non-existence (al-'adam al-asli). Non-existence cannot be an object of power, since power (qudra) brings about existence. Also, remaining (baqa') also cannot be an object of power, as power is for bringing about rather than preserving. Therefore, the agent has no power over leaving, and consequently he cannot be said to have the ability to leave—he can only act. This effectively turns the 'capable' agent into a necessitating cause (mujib), because he must produce his act. The passage ends with an objection from an opponent who says 'We do not say that leaving...' but the answer is cut off in this window.

Key Terms
ترك (tark)
العدم الأصلي (al-'adam al-asli)
مقدور (maqdur)
قادر (qadir)
موجب (mujib)
فعل (fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'leaving' is a positive act of refraining, requiring power to sustain the non-action.
Correction: The author insists that 'leaving' is merely the absence of action, not a positive state that power could bring about. It is non-existence, and power is for existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'leaving' (tark) cannot be an object of power?
A) Because leaving is a positive action that requires power.
B) Because leaving is non-existence and remaining, both of which cannot be produced by power.
C) Because the agent is too weak to refrain.
D) Because leaving is not a possible thing to intend.

Key Takeaway
Leaving cannot be an object of power because it is non-existence, so a truly powerful agent cannot have the ability to refrain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you believe you have the power to simply 'leave' doing something good? According to Razi's argument, leaving is non-existence—how does this challenge your assumption that you have genuine control over your omissions?
?
If God cannot have power over leaving because it is non-existence, what does that mean for your understanding of God's will? Does it make God less free, or does it reveal a deeper truth about the nature of power?
?
In what areas of your life do you rely on the idea that you could have done otherwise? How would your sense of responsibility change if you accepted that power is only over bringing about existence, not over refraining?

Seventh Proof: Impossibility of Power over Refraining (Tark)
Author presents a proof with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari position that God has power over both act and refraining (tark) leads to absurd consequences according to the author.

Core Idea
Refraining (tark) cannot be an object of power because it is non-existence.

This lesson examines the seventh proof presented by the author, which challenges the notion that a powerful agent must have power over both action and refraining. The argument demonstrates that if refraining were an object of power, it would reduce the agent to a necessitating cause. The passage also considers and refutes an objection that redefines refraining as the act of an opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author defines tark as remaining upon original non-existence and argues it cannot be an object of power.
Notice that the author distinguishes between non-existence and existence.
2
Understand the consequence
If tark cannot be an object of power, then the powerful agent lacks power over refraining, reducing him to a necessitating cause (mu'jib).
This is a key theological implication.
3
Follow the objection
An opponent redefines tark as the act of the opposite, making it an existing act that could be an object of power.
The objection attempts to salvage the position that tark is a voluntary act.
4
Trace the refutation
The author shows that this redefinition leads to either the impossibility of leaving all opposites together or the pre-eternity of bodies or their opposites, both absurd.
Pay attention to the two cases presented.
5
Conclude the proof
The objection is refuted, and the original proof stands: tark cannot be an object of power, so the powerful agent must be a necessitating cause, which contradicts the Ash'ari view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: إذا قلنا القادر لا بد وأن يقدر على الفعل والترك، لزم كون الترك مقدوراً. وهذا محال لأن الترك عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي، وكونه عدماً ينافي كونه مقدوراً، وكونه باقياً ينافي أيضاً كونه مقدوراً. فالعدم الأصلي الباقي يمتنع كونه مقدوراً. وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أن القادر لا قدرة له البتة على الترك، وليس له صلاحية الإيجاد والتأثير. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه ينقلب القادر موجباً. فإن قال قائل: نحن لا نقول: الترك عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي، بل هو عبارة عن فعل ضده. وعلى هذا التقدير فالترك يصح أن يكون مقدوراً. والجواب: إن هذا باطل. لأنا قلنا: الله تعالى قادر على إيجاد الجسم وعلى تركه، ثم فسرنا الترك بفعل الضد، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون للجسم ضد. فنقول: إنه تعالى إما أن يكون قادراً على تركهما معاً أو لا يمكنه ذلك. فإن أمكنه تركها معاً لم يكن ترك جميع الأضداد أمراً وجودياً مضاداً لها، وإلا لزم أن يكون عدم جميع الأضداد الوجودية ضداً، فيكون نفي الشيء عين ثبوته، وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن القادر لا يمكنه ترك الضدين معاً، فنقول: لما كان الباري تعالى قادراً على فعل الأجسام في الأزل، لزم أن يقال: إنه يجب أن يكون إما فاعلاً للأجسام في الأزل وإما فاعلاً لضد الأجسام، وعلى هذا التقدير لزم إما قدم الأجسام وإما قدم ضد الأجسام. وكل ذلك عندكم باطل محال.
The seventh proof: If we say that the powerful must be able to do both the act and the opposite (tark), then the opposite (tark) would be an object of power. And this is impossible, because the opposite (tark) is the remaining upon the original non-existence (al-'adam al-asli). Its being non-existence contradicts its being an object of power, and its being remaining also contradicts its being an object of power. So the original remaining non-existence cannot be an object of power. When this is established, it becomes clear that the powerful has no power at all over the opposite, and he has no capacity for bringing into existence and effecting. On this assumption, the powerful turns into a necessitating cause. If someone says: We do not say that the opposite is the remaining upon original non-existence, rather it is the act of its opposite. On this assumption, the opposite can be validly an object of power. The answer: This is false. Because we said: God (exalted) is powerful over bringing the body into existence and over leaving it, then we interpreted the leaving as the act of the opposite. So this necessitates that the body has an opposite. Then we say: He (exalted) is either powerful over leaving them both together, or He cannot do that. If He can leave them both together, then leaving all the opposites would not be a positively existent thing opposite to them, otherwise the non-existence of all the existent opposites would be an opposite, so the negation of a thing would be its affirmation, which is impossible. As for the second division, that the powerful cannot leave the two opposites together, we say: Since the Creator (exalted) is powerful over the act of bodies in pre-eternity, it follows that it must be said: He must be either a doer of bodies in pre-eternity or a doer of the opposite of bodies. On this assumption, it follows either the pre-eternity of bodies or the pre-eternity of the opposite of bodies. All that is impossible and absurd according to you.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the seventh) with an objection and answer. The author argues that if a powerful agent must be able to both act and refrain (tark), then the refraining would have to be an object of power. But refraining is remaining upon original non-existence. Non-existence cannot be an object of power, and remaining (stasis) also cannot be an object of power. Hence the powerful cannot have power over refraining, and therefore he cannot be an effective agent, reducing him to a necessitating cause. The objector then redefines refraining as the act of the opposite, making it an existing act. The author refutes this: if refraining is the act of the opposite, then the body must have an opposite. Then the argument proceeds to examine whether God can leave both opposites together or not. If He can, then leaving all opposites would not be an existent thing opposite to them; if it were, non-existence would become an opposite, which is absurd. If He cannot leave both together, then in pre-eternity He must be either creating bodies or creating their opposite, leading to the absurd consequence of pre-eternal bodies or their opposites. Thus the objection is refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that refraining (tark) can be an object of power because it is a voluntary choice, even if it is non-existence.
Correction: The author argues that non-existence itself cannot be an object of power, and remaining is a state of non-action, also not an object of power.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the powerful agent cannot have power over refraining (tark)?
A) Because refraining is an act of the opposite.
B) Because refraining is non-existence and remaining, both of which cannot be objects of power.
C) Because refraining requires a prior power.
D) Because God is not obligated to act.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that attributing power over refraining entails absurd consequences, thereby supporting the author's position that the powerful agent is a necessitating cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's power is such that He cannot 'refrain' (in the sense of non-existence), what does that tell you about His constant action? Does this make you feel secure in His continuous creation or challenged in your own inaction?

Eighth Proof: Power Cannot Be Created or Eternal
Author argues divine power cannot be created or eternal, with an incomplete objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that divine power cannot be created or eternal, and see the beginning of an objection.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be created or eternal without contradiction.

In this proof, the author examines the nature of divine power. He first divides it into two possibilities—created or eternal—then shows each leads to an impossibility. An objection is raised, but its full answer is cut off here.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and translation
Read the Arabic text and the English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a division followed by two refutations, then an objection.
2
Analyze the refutation of created power
The author rejects created power because it would require a prior power, leading to infinite regress. Understand why infinite regress is problematic.
3
Analyze the refutation of eternal power
Eternal power is rejected because its attachment to a specific temporal event creates problems after the event occurs. Either the attachment remains (impossible) or ceases (impossible).
Note the two impossibilities.
4
Examine the objection
The objection claims God remains powerful after the event. The answer begins but is incomplete. Reflect on how you might complete it.
The text cuts off after 'من وجهين:'.

The second is also false, because it was attached from pre-eternity to pre-eternity to bringing into existence that particular temporal event. And if it does not remain, then the elimination of the eternal (zawal al-qadim) would follow, which is also impossible, impossible. Commentary
This passage is a proof (the eighth) with an objection. The author divides power into created or eternal. Created power is impossible because it would require another power, leading to infinite regress. Eternal power is also impossible because from eternity it was attached to a specific temporal event. After the event occurs, if the attachment remains, it would mean power over what already exists, which is impossible. If the attachment ceases, then the eternal would cease, which is impossible. The objection claims that God remains powerful after the event. The explanation of this objection is begun but cut off at the end of the page.

Refuting the Claim That Abandonment Is an Act
Refutes the claim that 'ترك' is an act and shows its impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'ترك' (abandonment) is an act leads to absurdities about divine power and pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Calling abandonment an act forces impossible choices about God's power.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle theological claim: that 'ترك' (abandonment) is a real act and thus an object of divine power. He shows that defining abandonment as an act leads to contradictions about God's ability to abandon opposites. The argument proceeds by dividing possibilities and exposing absurdities in each branch.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Note the dense, technical style of the argument.
The passage assumes familiarity with terms like 'ضد' (opposite) and 'مقدور' (object of power).
2
Identify the opponent's definitions
The opponent first defines 'ترك' as remaining on original non-existence, then redefines it as performing the opposite. Notice how the author responds to this redefinition.
3
Follow the division of possibilities
The author divides the scenario: either God can abandon both the body and its opposite together, or He cannot. Trace each case carefully.
This is a classic 'taqsim' (division) argument.
4
See the absurdities in each branch
If God can abandon both together, then non-existence would be an opposite, which is absurd. If He cannot, then since God is powerful over bodies in pre-eternity, either bodies or their opposite must be pre-eternal, which is also absurd.
Both branches lead to conclusions the opponent must reject.
5
Conclude the refutation
The argument forces the opponent's position into impossibility. Understand that the passage is a complete refutation by reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي. بل هو عبارة عن فعل ضده. وعلى هذا التقدير، فالترك يصح أن يكون مقدوراً. والجواب: إن هذا باطل. لأنا قلنا: الله تعالى قادر على إيجاد الجسم وعلى تركه ثم فسرنا الترك بفعل الضد، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون للجسم ضد. فنقول: إنه تعالى، إما أن يكون قادراً على تركهما معاً، أو لا يمكنه ذلك. فإن أمكنه تركها معاً، لم يكن ترك جميع الأضداد أمراً وجودياً مضاداً لها، وإلا لزم أن يكون عدم جميع الأضداد الوجودية ضداً، فيكون نفي الشيء عين ثبوته. وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني، وهو أن القادر لا يمكنه ترك الضدين معاً فنقول: لما كان الباري تعالى قادراً على فعل الأجسام في الأزل، لزم أن يقال: إنه يجب أن يكون إما فاعلا للأجسام في الأزل، وإما فاعلاً لضد الأجسام، وعلى هذا التقدير، لزم إما قدم الأجسام، وإما قدم ضد الأجسام. وكل ذلك عندكم باطل محال.
It is an expression of remaining upon the original non-existence. Rather it is an expression of performing its opposite. And on this estimation, abandonment is valid to be an object of power. The answer: This is false. For we said: God (exalted) is powerful over bringing the body into existence and over abandoning it, then we interpreted abandonment as performing the opposite, so this implies that the body has an opposite. Then we say: He (exalted) is either powerful over abandoning both together, or He cannot do that. If He can abandon both together, then abandoning all opposites would not be an existent thing opposite to them, otherwise it would necessitate that the non-existence of all existent opposites is an opposite, so the negation of a thing would be identical to its affirmation, which is absurd. As for the second division, that the powerful [i.e. agent] cannot abandon the two opposites together, we say: Since the Creator (exalted) is powerful over performing the bodies in pre-eternity, it must be said: He must be either a doer of bodies in pre-eternity, or a doer of the opposite of bodies, and on this estimation it necessitates either the pre-eternity of bodies or the pre-eternity of the opposite of bodies. All of that, according to you, is false and absurd.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that 'ترك' (abandonment/leaving) is an act and therefore an object of divine power. The opponent defines 'ترك' as remaining upon original non-existence but then redefines it as performing the opposite. The author first states that if 'ترك' is defined as performing an opposite act, then it would be an act and thus possible as an object of power. But the author rejects this as false, because God is powerful over both bringing a body into existence and 'abandoning' it, and 'abandonment' was interpreted as performing the opposite, which implies the body has an opposite. Then the author argues by division: either God can abandon both the body and its opposite together, or He cannot. If He can, then abandoning all opposites would not itself be an existent opposite, otherwise non-existence would be an opposite, which is absurd. If He cannot abandon both together, then since God is powerful over bodies in pre-eternity, He must either be a doer of bodies or of their opposite, implying either bodies or their opposite are eternal, both of which are absurd. Thus the original claim fails.

The Eighth Proof: Divine Power Cannot Be Eternal
Presents the eighth proof that divine power cannot be eternal or originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof that divine power cannot be eternal, including the dilemma, objection, and refutation.

Core Idea
An eternal power related to a temporal act leads to absurdity.

Building on discussions of divine attributes, this lesson examines a sophisticated proof that the power (قادرية) behind creation cannot be eternal. The argument proceeds by division, showing both eternal and originated options lead to contradictions. It then addresses an important objection about God's remaining power after creation, refining the core issue.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the opening of the passage where the author presents two options for divine power: originated or eternal. See why the first option (originated) is rejected due to infinite regress.
This sets up the main argument.
2
Examine the eternal option
The author shows that an eternal power must have an eternal relation to a specific temporal thing. When that thing comes to exist, either the relation remains (impossible) or ceases (implies change in the eternal).
Focus on the reasoning about the relation (تعلق).
3
Consider the objection
An objection claims God remains powerful after creation, giving two examples: creating other worlds and re-creating this world after annihilation.
The objection tries to show power is unchanged.
4
Analyze the answer
The author refines the objection's locus: God does not remain powerful over the exact same world without intermediary. The two examples are addressed; re-creation is rejected on two grounds.
The answer clarifies the real issue is the specific relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: إن تلك القادرية إما أن تكون حادثة أو أزلية. والأول باطل، لأنها لو كانت حادثة لافتقر حدوثها إلى سبق قادرية أخرى، ولزم التسلسل. والثاني أيضاً باطل. لأنها ما كانت متعلقة من الأزل إلى الأبد بإيجاد ذلك الحادث المعين. فإذا وجد ذلك الحادث، فإن بقي ذلك التعلق، فهو محال. لأنه يقتضي القدرة على إيجاد ما هو موجود، وهو وإن لم يبق، فحينئذ يلزم زوال القديم، وهو أيضاً محال. فإن قيل: إنه تعالى بعد إيجاد ذلك الحادث، بقي قادراً كما كان. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إنه يمكنه إيجاد عوالم أخرى، بعد إيجاد ذلك العالم فيثبت: أن القدرة باقية كما كانت. والثاني: أنه تعالى بقي أيضاً قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم، من بعض الوجوه. وذلك لأنه قادر على أن يعدم هذا العالم. وإذا أعدمه، كان قادراً على أن يوجده مرة أخرى على سبيل الإعادة. فيثبت: أنه تعالى بعد إيجاد هذا العالم، بقي قادرا على ما كان. والجواب أن نقول: إن محل الإلزام شيء، مغاير لما ظننتموه وهو أنه تعالى كان من الأزل إلى الأبد: قادرا على إيجاد هذا العالم ثم بعد أن أوجد هذا العالم لم يبق قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم بعينه، وإلا لزم أن يكون قادراً على إيجاد الموجود وهو محال. وإذا لم يبقى قادراً على إيجاد هذا أنه كان من الأزل إلى وقت إيجاد هذا العالم، كان قادراً على إيجاد العالم، مع هذا العالم. فحينئذ يلزم عدم القديم، وزوال الأزلي. فأما أنه تعالى بقي قادراً على إيجاد عوالم أخرى غير هذا العالم، فلا تعلق له بمحل الإلزام. أما قوله: ثانياً بأنه تعالى بقي قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم، بشرط أن يعدمه أولاً، ثم يعيده ثانياً، فنقول: هذا أيضاً بعيد من وجهين: الأول: إن ما فني وعدم ولم يبق له ذات ولا خصوصية، فإن عوده بعينه مما لا يقبله العقل. الثاني: هب أنه ذلك، إلا أنه تعالى كان في الأزل قادراً على إيجاد يصح هذا العالم من دون هذه الواسطة، ثم إنه بعد أن أوجد هذا العالم، ما بقيت
The eighth proof: That ability (al-qudriyya) is either originated or eternal. The first is false, because if it were originated, its origination would need a preceding ability, leading to infinite regress. The second is also false, because it was related from pre-eternity to eternity to the bringing into existence of that specific temporal thing. So when that temporal thing comes to exist, if that relation remains, it is absurd, because it would imply power to bring into existence what already exists; and if it does not remain, then the disappearance of the eternal follows, which is also absurd. If it is said: After bringing that temporal thing into existence, He (exalted) remained powerful as before. Its explanation is from two aspects: First, He can bring other worlds into existence after bringing that world into existence, proving that power remained as before. Second, He (exalted) also remained powerful over bringing this world into existence in some respects, because He is powerful to annihilate this world, and if He annihilates it, He is powerful to bring it into existence again by way of restoration. So it is proven: that after bringing this world into existence, He remained powerful over what He was. The answer: We say: The locus of the objection (mahall al-ilzām) is something different from what you thought: namely, that He (exalted) was from pre-eternity to eternity powerful over bringing this world into existence, then after He brought this world into existence, He did not remain powerful over bringing this very world into existence; otherwise it would necessitate that He is powerful over bringing into existence what exists, which is absurd. And if He did not remain powerful over bringing this... He was from pre-eternity to the time of bringing this world into existence, He was powerful over bringing the world together with this world. So then the disappearance of the eternal and the cessation of the pre-eternal follow. As for His remaining powerful over bringing other worlds besides this world, it has no connection to the locus of the objection. As for your second statement: that He (exalted) remained powerful over bringing this world into existence, on condition that He annihilates it first then restores it again, we say: This is also far-fetched for two aspects: First: What has perished and ceased to exist and has no essence or particularity remaining, its return exactly as it was is something the intellect does not accept. Second: Even if that were so, yet He (exalted) in pre-eternity was powerful over bringing... this world without this intermediary, then after He brought this world into existence, it did not remain...
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof concerning the divine attribute of power (قادرية). The proof argues by division: either this power is originated (حادثة) or eternal (أزلية). The first option is impossible because an originated power would require a preceding power, leading to infinite regress. The second option is also impossible: if the power is eternal, it was eternally related to the bringing into existence of a specific temporal entity. When that entity comes to exist, either the relation remains (implying power over the existent, which is absurd) or it ceases (implying change in the eternal, also absurd). An objection is raised that after creation, God remains powerful, citing two examples: creating other worlds and re-creating this one after annihilation. The answer refines the objection: the real point is that God does not remain powerful over the exact same world after its creation without intermediary, as that would be power over the existent; and if the power relation changes, the eternal is compromised. The ability to create other worlds is irrelevant. As for the suggestion of re-creation after annihilation, it fails for two reasons: first, the intellect does not accept the return of an annihilated particular; second, even if it were possible, God in pre-eternity had the power to create without that condition, and after creation that unconditional power is lost, implying change.

Key Terms
قادرية
حادثة
أزلية
تعلق
قدرة
إيجاد
موجود
قديم
إعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power remains completely unchanged after creation, including power over the same exact world.
Correction: The power relation to the specific world changes; otherwise, power over the existent would be implied.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof conclude about an eternal divine power?
A) It is possible with proper conditions.
B) It is false because it forces a relation to a temporal event that is impossible.
C) It is true only if the power is unlimited.
D) It is true but only for non-existent things.

Key Takeaway
An eternal power must have a relation to a specific temporal entity; when that entity exists, the relation must change, implying impossibility for an eternal power to be the source of a first temporal act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that God's power over bringing this specific world into existence is unique and cannot remain unchanged after creation. How do you feel knowing that the very act of creation reveals a specific aspect of God's power that is now 'completed'? Does this deepen your sense of the seriousness of this world as a unique act of God? Pause and thank God for bringing this specific world into existence.
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of power over something already existing. Have you ever found yourself trying to 'control' something in your life after it has already happened, as if you could change it? Reflect on how this proof reminds you that power over existence is not the same as power over the existent. Let go of that false sense of control and submit to God's decree.
?
The objection about God remaining powerful to recreate after annihilation is refuted because the intellect cannot accept the return of something that has perished completely. Does this challenge your assumptions about resurrection? Have you truly trusted in God's absolute power despite this rational difficulty? Commit to deepening your understanding of divine power beyond human analogies.

The Objection and the Core Answer: The Impossibility of Creating What Exists
The author presents the objection and gives the first part of the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's power remains after creation and the initial answer that God cannot create the exact same world because creating what exists is impossible.

Core Idea
God cannot create what already exists; otherwise, creation would be redundant.

This lesson introduces the objection from those who argue that God's power must remain unchanged after creation, and the author's initial refutation focusing on the logical impossibility of creating the same world twice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objector presents two points: first, God can create other worlds; second, He can recreate this world after annihilation.
Notice that the objector aims to prove that God's power remains as it was.
2
Identify the two points of the objection
The objector gives two reasons: ability to create other worlds and ability to recreate this world after annihilation. Both are meant to show the power is unchanged.
3
Read the author's answer: the locus of the objection is different
The author states that the real issue is about the ability to create the exact same world after it already exists, which would be creating what exists, an impossibility.
The author argues that God's power over this specific world ceases after creation due to logical impossibility.
4
Reflect on why creating what exists is impossible
Consider that 'bringing into existence what already exists' is a contradiction because existence is already actual. This is not a limitation on God's power but a logical absurdity.
This is a key concept in kalam: the object of power must be possible, not actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه يمكنه إيجاد عوالم أخرى ، بعد إيجاد ذلك العالم فيثبت : أن القدرة باقية كما كانت . والثاني : أنه تعالى بقي أيضاً قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم ، من بعض الوجوه . وذلك لأنه قادر على أن يعدم هذا العالم . وإذا أعدمه ، كان قادراً على أن يوجده مرة أخرى على سبيل الإعادة . فيثبت : أنه تعالى بعد إيجاد هذا العالم ، بقي قادرا على ما كان والجواب : إن محل الإلزام شيء ، مغاير لما ظننتموه وهو أنه تعالى كان من الأزل إلى الأبد : قادرا على إيجاد هذا العالم ثم بعد أن أوجد هذا العالم لم يبق قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم بعينه ، وإلا لزم أن يكون قادراً على إيجاد الموجود وهو محال . [ وإذا لم يبق قادراً على إيجاد ] هذا [؟] أنه كان من الأزل إلى وقت إيجاد هذا العالم ، كان قادراً على إيجاد العالم ، مع هذا العالم . فحينئذ يلزم عدم القديم، وزوال الأزلي فأما أنه تعالى بقي قادراً على إيجاد عوالم أخرى غير هذا العالم ، فلا تعلق له بمحل الإلزام أما قوله : ثانياً بأنه تعالى بقي قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم ، بشرط أن يعدمه أولاً ، ثم يعيده ثانياً ، فنقول : هذا أيضاً بعيد من وجهين : الأول : إن ما فني وعدم ولم يبق له ذات ولا خصوصية ، فإن عوده بعينه مما لا يقبله العقل الثاني : هب أنه ذلك ، إلا أنه تعالى كان في الأزل قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم من دون هذه الواسطة ، ثم إنه بعد أن أوجد هذا العالم ، ما بقيت تلك القادرية على الوجه المذكور ، وإنما حدثت القادرية على الإعادة ثانياً ، بتوسط الإعدام أولاً ، ثم بعد ذلك تحصل القدرة على الإعادة ثانياً . فالقدرة الثابتة في الأزل ، مغايرة لهذه القادرية الحادثة بهذه الواسطة وحاصل الكلام فيه : يرجع إلى الاعتراف بزوال القادرية ، ثم إدعاء نوع آخر من القادرية مغايرة للقادرية التي كانت موجودة قبل ذلك ، وهذا لا يدفع الإلزام المذكور .
The first: That He is able to bring into existence other worlds, after bringing that world into existence, so it establishes that power remains as it was. And the second: That He, exalted, also remained able to bring this world into existence in some respects. That is because He is able to annihilate this world. And if He annihilates it, He is able to bring it into existence again by way of re-creation. So it establishes that He, exalted, after bringing this world into existence, remained able to do what He was [able to do]. The answer: That the locus of the objection is something different from what you thought. And it is that He, exalted, was from pre-eternity to eternity able to bring this world into existence. Then after He brought this world into existence, He did not remain able to bring this very world into existence; otherwise, it would necessitate that He be able to bring into existence what already exists, which is impossible. [And if He did not remain able to bring into existence] this [?] that He was from pre-eternity to the time of bringing this world into existence, He was able to bring the world into existence together with this world. Then at that point the non-existence of the eternal and the removal of the pre-eternal would be entailed. As for that He, exalted, remained able to bring into existence other worlds besides this world, that is not related to the locus of the objection. As for His statement, 'secondly: that He, exalted, remained able to bring this world into existence on condition that He annihilate it first, then return it secondly,' we say: This too is remote from two aspects: The first: That what has perished and become non-existent and no essence or particularity remains for it, its return as the very same thing is not acceptable to reason. The second: Suppose that were so, but He, exalted, was in pre-eternity able to bring this world into existence without this intermediary. Then after He brought this world into existence, that ability did not remain in the mentioned manner; rather, the ability for re-creation secondarily occurred, mediated by annihilation first, then after that the ability for re-creation secondarily is obtained. So the ability established in pre-eternity is different from this ability that occurred through this intermediary. And the summary of the discourse about it returns to admitting the removal of the ability, then claiming another type of ability different from the ability that existed before that, and this does not repel the aforementioned objection.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning God's power after creating the world. The objector presents two points: first, that God can create other worlds, so His power remains; second, that He can recreate this world after annihilating it. The answer begins by stating that the real issue (محل الإلزام) is different: God was eternally able to create this world, but after creating it, He cannot create the exact same world because that would mean bringing into existence what already exists, which is absurd. The argument then proceeds to a second point: the objector claims God can recreate after annihilation. The author refutes this from two angles: first, once a thing perishes without any remaining essence, its exact return is intellectually unacceptable; second, even if granted, the ability to recreate is a new ability arising after annihilation, different from the original eternal ability, which admits that the original ability ceased, thus not repelling the objection. The summary emphasizes that the objector's reply essentially admits the loss of the original ability and claims a new one, which does not counter the initial objection.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإلزام
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God cannot recreate the exact same world, then His power is limited.
Correction: God's power is infinite, but the object of power must be possible. Creating what already exists is logically impossible, so it is not a valid object of power.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't God create the exact same world after He has created it?
A) Because His power is exhausted.
B) Because creating what already exists is impossible.
C) Because He prefers other worlds.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's inability to create the same world after it exists is due to logical impossibility, not lack of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe God is all-powerful, do you ever find yourself expecting Him to do the logically impossible? How does this lesson correct that expectation, and what does that mean for how you trust Him?
?
This lesson shows that God's inability to create the same world again is not a weakness but a logical necessity. Is there an area in your life where you view God's 'inability' to answer a prayer as a failure, when it might be a logical impossibility? How can you realign your understanding?
?
The objector's mistake was misidentifying the issue. In your own life, when you question God's power or justice, could you be misidentifying the real issue? What would it take to shift from objection to submission?

Refutation of the Second Objection: Can God Recreate After Annihilation?
The author refutes the second objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's two-fold refutation of the claim that God can recreate the world after annihilating it.

Core Idea
A thing that has perished without any remaining essence cannot return exactly as it was; and even if it could, the ability to recreate is a new ability, not the original.

This lesson examines the second objection that God can recreate the world after annihilating it, and the author's detailed response showing why this does not demonstrate the continuation of the original power.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the passage where the objector claims that God can recreate this world after annihilating it, thus His power remains.
2
Understand the first refutation: perished things without essence cannot return
The author argues that once a thing perishes and no essence remains, its exact return is intellectually unacceptable.
This is a strong metaphysical point about identity and continuity.
3
Understand the second refutation: the new ability is different from the original
Even if recreation were possible, the ability to recreate is a new ability arising after annihilation, mediated by that act. This is different from the original eternal ability.
The objector's claim actually admits the original ability ceased.
4
Summarize the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the objection fails because it relies on claiming a new type of ability, not the continuation of the original.
This effectively repels the objection.

The first: That He is able to bring into existence other worlds, after bringing that world into existence, so it establishes that power remains as it was. And the second: That He, exalted, also remained able to bring this world into existence in some respects. That is because He is able to annihilate this world. Commentary

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the exact return of a perished thing not acceptable to reason?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because nothing perishes completely.
C) Because there is no remaining essence to be recreated.

Key Takeaway
The ability to recreate after annihilation is a new ability, not the continuation of the original, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, do you assume it works like human power—that it must continue unchanged? What in your life are you tempted to compare to God in a way that limits His absolute freedom? Can you name one assumption you hold about God that this lesson challenges, and how will you correct it today?
?
The lesson argues that God's ability to recreate is a new ability, not the old one. Does your worship rely on a sense that God 'owes' you consistency or predictability? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly believed He can act in entirely new ways, not bound by your expectations?
?
If the exact return of a perished thing is impossible, what does that teach you about the uniqueness of every moment and every thing God creates? Are you living as if you can get a 'second chance' without transformation, or are you seizing the one chance you have right now to turn to Him?

Refutation of Renewed Power for Recreation
Refutation of the claim that a new power for recreation avoids the problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming a new, temporally arising power for recreation does not avoid the theological objection against change in divine attributes.

Core Idea
A new power for creation is just an admission that the old power vanished—it doesn't solve the problem.

The author now addresses a specific counterargument: that God acquires a new power after annihilation to bring about recreation. At first glance, this seems to avoid the need for change in the eternal attributes. But as the passage demonstrates, this move merely shifts the problem without resolving it. Understanding this refutation deepens our grasp of divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without translation. Notice the logical flow.
2
Follow the argument
The passage lays out the opponent's claim then shows its flaw. Identify each move: the claim, the admission, the conclusion.
The key phrase is 'يرجع إلى الاعتراف بزوال القادرية'.
3
Reflect on the objection
Consider why admitting removal of original ability is problematic. How does this reaffirm divine immutability?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك القادرية على الوجه المذكور ، وإنما حدثت القادرية على الإعادة ثانياً ، بتوسط الإعدام أولاً ، ثم بعد ذلك تحصل القدرة على الإعادة ثانياً . فالقدرة الثابتة في الأزل ، مغايرة لهذه القادرية الحادثة بهذه الواسطة . وحاصل الكلام فيه : يرجع إلى الاعتراف بزوال القادرية ، ثم إدعاء نوع آخر من القادرية مغايرة للقادرية التي كانت موجودة قبل ذلك ، وهذا لا يدفع الإلزام المذكور .
That power in the manner mentioned. And power for re-creation only occurs secondly, through the intermediacy of annihilation firstly, then after that the ability for re-creation is obtained secondly. So the power established in eternity is different from this newly occurring power through this intermediary. And the summary of the discourse regarding it returns to admitting the removal of the ability, then claiming another type of ability different from the ability that existed before that, and this does not repel the aforementioned objection.

Commentary
This passage refutes a claim that the ability for recreation is a new, distinct power that arises after annihilation. The author argues that this admission does not avoid the original objection because it concedes the removal of the original ability and then posits a different ability, which merely shifts the problem without solving it. The argument is that the claim reduces to an acknowledgment that the original power ceases and is replaced by another, but the required objection—that such a change implies a temporal origination in God's attributes—remains unaddressed.

The Total Cause and the Impossibility of Circularity
The ninth proof against dependence on originated conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the total efficient cause cannot depend on an originated condition without leading to circularity.

Core Idea
The total cause either eternally produces its effect or requires a condition—but a condition leads to circularity.

This lesson presents the ninth proof from the author, which examines the concept of the total efficient cause (المؤثر التام). The argument systematically considers all possibilities for what constitutes the total cause and shows that any dependence on an originated condition results in a logical dead end. We will walk through the three possibilities and see why circularity is inevitable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key term
Learn the definition of 'total efficient cause' (المؤثر التام) as the cause that combines all aspects of effectivity. Without it, the act does not occur.
This sets the foundation for the argument.
2
Examine the three-way classification
The total cause is divided into three possibilities: only the divine Essence, the Essence with other eternal things, or the Essence with originated things.
This division is exhaustive.
3
Evaluate the first two possibilities
If the total cause is the Essence alone or with other eternal things, it is eternal. An eternal cause would produce an eternal effect, contradicting God's ability to act or refrain.
4
See the circularity in the third
If the total cause includes originated conditions, the cause's effectiveness depends on the effect's origination, creating a vicious circle (دور). This is impossible.
This refutes any objection that posits an originated condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لا شك أنه ما لم يوجد المؤثر التام المستجمع جميع جهات المؤثرية، فإنه لا يقع الفعل. فذلك المؤثر [ التام ] : إما أن يكون [ هو ] ذات واجب الوجود فقط، أو ذاته مع أمور أخرى قديمة، أو ذاته مع أمور أخرى حادثة. فإن كان الحق هو الأول أو الثاني، لزم من قدم ذلك المؤثر قدم [ ذلك ] الأثر، وذلك يقدح في كونه متمكناً من الفعل والترك معاً، والثالث باطل. لأن حدوث الحادث موقوف على حصول المؤثر التام في المؤثرية، فلو كان حصول المؤثر التام في المؤثرية موقوفاً على حدوث الحادث لزم الدور، وهو محال. واعلم أن هذا التقسيم منحصر مضبوط، سواء قيل: إنه يعتبر في تلك المؤثرية حضور وقت، أو حصول مصلحة، أو زوال مانع. فإن الكل داخل فيما ذكرناه. والأجوبة التسعة المذكورة في الحجة الأولى يرجع حاصلها إلى توقيف مؤثرية الله تعالى في وجود العالم على حصول شرط حادث. وقد دللنا على أن ذلك يوجب الدور، فكانت تلك الأجوبة ساقطة بالكلية [ والله أعلم ].
The ninth proof: There is no doubt that as long as the total efficient cause, combining all aspects of effectivity, has not come to be, the act does not occur. So that total efficient cause: either it is the Essence of the Necessary Existence alone, or His Essence together with other eternal things, or His Essence together with other originated things. If it is the first or second, then from the eternity of that cause follows the eternity of that effect, and that detracts from His being capable of both doing and leaving. The third is invalid because the origination of the originated thing depends on the occurrence of the total efficient cause in its effectivity. So if the occurrence of the total efficient cause in its effectivity depended on the origination of the originated thing, circularity would follow, which is impossible. And know that this division is exhaustive and precise, whether it is said that in that effectivity a time of occurrence, or attainment of benefit, or removal of an obstacle is considered. For all is included in what we have mentioned. And the nine answers mentioned in the first proof: their summary returns to making the effectivity of God Almighty in the existence of the world dependent on the occurrence of an originated condition. And we have demonstrated that that entails circularity, so those answers are completely invalid. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof, which argues that the total efficient cause (المؤثر التام) cannot depend on an originated condition without circularity. The author divides the total efficient cause into three possibilities: the divine Essence alone, the Essence with other eternal things, or the Essence with originated things. If it is the first or second, the effect would be eternal, contradicting God's ability to act or refrain. The third leads to circularity because the effect's origination would depend on the cause being fully effective, and if that effectiveness depends on the effect's origination, a vicious circle results. The author then notes that this division covers all proposals, such as requiring a specific time, benefit, or removal of an obstacle. Finally, he asserts that the nine earlier answers to the first proof all reduce to positing an originated condition, which has been shown to entail circularity, rendering them invalid.

Key Terms

جهات المؤثرية
الدور
الحادث
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's effectivity can depend on an originated condition without circularity.
Correction: The argument shows that any originated condition creates a circular dependency because the effect's origination would be required for the cause to be fully effective, and the cause's full effectiveness is required for the effect's origination.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what logical problem results if the total efficient cause requires an originated condition to be fully effective?
A) Contradiction between divine attributes.
B) Eternity of the effect.
C) Circularity (دور).
D) Invalidity of the first two possibilities.

Key Takeaway
The total efficient cause cannot depend on an originated condition, as that would create a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's total cause does not depend on any condition, then your existence and every action of yours are directly caused by Him without any intermediary condition. Does the way you live reflect this dependency, or do you act as if your will is self-sufficient?
?
The proof demolishes circular reasoning in divine causation. Yet, in your spiritual life, do you ever fall into a subtle circularity—seeking external conditions for obedience when obedience itself is the condition? How can you break that cycle by relying solely on God's unconditional grace?
