Two Proofs That Divine Power Has No Direct Effect
Two proofs that divine power cannot directly produce the object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs that establish divine power (qudra) cannot directly cause the existence of its object (maqdur).

Core Idea
Power relates equally to opposites; creation is the real cause.

This tenth proof from al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah tackles a subtle point: divine power itself does not directly bring about the object of power. The author gives two arguments—one logical, one empirical—to separate the concept of ability from the act of creation. Understanding this distinction clarifies the nature of divine causation and avoids attributing direct causal efficacy to mere capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage uses a compressed argumentative style.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage presents two proofs (الوجه الأول and الثاني). Find each one and notice how they are introduced and concluded.
The first proof is based on the contradictory implications of equal relation; the second on the distinction between capability and actual creation.
3
Analyze the first proof
Focus on the logical argument: why mere ability cannot be effective because it would require combining equal relation and preponderance, which is a contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the second proof
Focus on the empirical argument: why we cannot say something exists just because the agent is capable of it, since the agent is capable of many things that do not exist.
The conclusion distinguishes between capability and the act of bringing into existence.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Notice how the passage concludes that power has no effect on the object of power; rather, the agent's creative act is the true cause.
This prepares the ground for affirming that Allah creates directly by His will, not by a passive capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن القدرة يمتنع [ أن يكون ] لها أثر في حصول المقدور . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع القول بثبوت القدرة . أما بيان المقام الأول فمن وجهين : الأول : إن القادر لما كان متمكناً من الفعل والترك ، امتنع أن يكون مجرد القادرية مؤثراً في وقوع الفعل، لأن كونها قدرة يقتضي أن تكون نسبتها إلى الطرفين على السوية. وكونها مؤثرة في وقوع الفعل بعينه يقتضي أن يكون نسبتها [ إلى طرف ] الوجود راجحة على نسبتها إلى طرف العدم . والجمع بين هذين القولين جمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال . فيثبت أن مجرد القادرية لا تأثير له في وقوع المقدور البتة . الثاني : إنا إذا قلنا : لم وجد هذا الشيء بعد أن كان معدوماً؟ فإن العقل لا يجوز أن نقول : إنما وجد هذا لأن القادر كان قادراً عليه . فإنه يقال : وهو قادر على سائر المقدورات مع أنها لم توجد . فعلمنا أن هذا الذي وجد إنما وجد لا لكون القادر قادراً عليه، بل لأجل أن القادر أوجده وصنعه وخلقه وأخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود. وصدق هذا النص والإثبات يدل على أن المؤثر في وجود الأثر إنما هو كون المؤثر مؤثراً فيه وموجداً له، وأن هذا المفهوم مغاير للمفهوم من كونه قادراً على الفعل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القدرة يمتنع أن يكون لها أثر في وجود المقدورة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، امتنع أن يكون للقدرة أثر في الشيء . وذلك لأنه ثبت بما ذكرنا : أن
The tenth proof: It is impossible for power to have an effect in the occurrence of the object of power. And if the matter is so, it is impossible to affirm the existence of power. As for the explanation of the first point, it is from two aspects: First: Since the capable agent is able to both do and leave, it is impossible for mere ability to be effective in the occurrence of the act, because its being power requires that its relation to the two sides be equal. And its being effective in the occurrence of the specific act requires that its relation to the side of existence be preponderant over its relation to the side of non-existence. Combining these two statements is combining two contradictories, which is impossible. So it is established that mere ability has no effect whatsoever in the occurrence of the object of power. Second: When we say: Why did this thing exist after it was non-existent? The intellect does not allow us to say: This existed only because the capable agent was capable of it. For it is said: And he is capable of all other objects of power, yet they do not exist. So we know that this which exists exists not because the capable agent is capable of it, but because the capable agent brought it into existence, made it, created it, and brought it from non-existence to existence. And the truth of this text and affirmation indicates that the effect in the existence of the effect is only that the cause is effective in it and brings it into existence, and that this concept is different from the concept of being capable of the act. So it is established by what we have mentioned that it is impossible for power to have an effect in the existence of the object of power. And once this is established, it is impossible for power to have an effect in the thing. And that is because it is established by what we have mentioned that
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, which argues that divine power cannot have a direct effect in bringing about the object of power. The argument has two parts. The first part relies on the nature of power: if the agent is capable of both doing and leaving, then power by itself relates equally to both sides. If it were effective in one specific act, it would require a preponderant relation to that side, contradicting its equal relation—a direct contradiction. Therefore, mere ability has no effect. The second part is an empirical argument: if we ask why something exists, we cannot answer 'because the agent was capable of it', since the agent is capable of many things that do not exist. The real reason is that the agent specifically brought it into existence. Thus, the effective cause is the act of bringing into existence, not the mere capacity. The conclusion is that power has no causal role in the existence of its object; rather, the agent's act of creation is the true cause.

Key Terms

المقدور
الفعل والترك
القادر
المؤثر
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine power directly creates the object, but the author distinguishes between power (which enables) and the act of creation (which actually brings about existence).
Correction: The passage shows that power itself does not bring the object into existence; rather, the agent's specific act of creating does. Power is merely the capacity to do either act, not the efficient cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof in the tenth argument claim that mere ability cannot be effective in the occurrence of the act?
A) Because ability is eternal and unchanging.
B) Because it would require two different abilities for the same act.
C) Because it would combine two contradictories: equal relation and preponderant relation.
D) Because the agent is not truly capable.

Key Takeaway
Divine power, as mere capacity, cannot directly cause existence; the agent's creative act is the true cause of the object of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson argues that power is neutral and does not cause existence. What part of your life are you most tempted to attribute to your own power? Will you sincerely repent of that and ascribe all existence to Allah alone?

Effectiveness vs. Power: The True Cause of Acts
The author distinguishes effectiveness from power and discusses the attribute of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why effectiveness and causative agency are distinct from power and how this distinction resolves the nature of divine action.

Core Idea
Effectiveness, not power, is the true cause of existence.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between power (qudra) and effectiveness (fa'iliyya). The author argues that power has no direct causal role; rather, effectiveness is the real influencer. We will also examine the nature of the attribute of action (fi'l/takwin) and why it cannot be neutral without implying necessitarianism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style of the author.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage begins by asserting that effectiveness and causative agency are something other than power. Find where the author argues that power has no effect on the existence of the act, and why two independent causes are impossible.
This is the core claim of the lesson.
3
Analyze the dilemma on action
The last part presents a dilemma about the attribute of action. Consider both possibilities: if it is neutral like power, or if it is determined to existence. What are the implications?
This shows the author's method of argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاعلية والموجدية أمر مغاير للقدرة ، وثبت : أن المؤثر في وجود الأثر هو الفاعلية والموجدية . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون للقادرية أثر فيه ، وإلا لزم أن يجتمع على الأثر الواحد سببان مستقلان ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : فقد بينا أن القادرية ليس لها صلاحية التأثير البتة. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القادرية لا أثر لها البتة في وجود الأثر وحينئذ لا تكون القدرة صالحة للتأثير البتة . وأما الصفة المسماة بالفعل والتكوين ، فإنها إن كانت صالحة لطرفي الفعل والترك ، كانت المباحث المذكورة في القدرة عائدة فيه ، وإن كانت متعينة لطرف الوجود وغير صالحة البتة لطرف العدم، كان ذلك موجباً بالذات ، لا قادراً متمكناً من الفعل والترك .
Effectiveness and causative agency are something other than power, and it is established that the influencer in the existence of the effect is effectiveness and causative agency. If that is so, it is impossible that power (al-qudriyya) has any effect in it, otherwise two independent causes would have to combine upon one effect, which is absurd. Also we have shown that power (al-qudriyya) has no capacity for influence at all. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that power has no effect at all in the existence of the effect, and then power is not suitable for influence at all. As for the attribute called action and creation (al-fi'l wa al-takwin), if it is suitable for both sides of action and omission, then the aforementioned discussions concerning power recur in it; but if it is determined to the side of existence and not at all suitable for the side of non-existence, then it would be a necessitator by essence, not a capable agent (qadir) having ability over action and omission.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between effectiveness (al-fa'iliyya) and causative agency (al-mujidiyya) on one hand, and power (al-qudra) on the other. The author begins by stating that effectiveness and causative agency are something other than power, and it has been established that the real influencer in the existence of the effect is effectiveness and causative agency, not power. Therefore, it is impossible for power to have any effect in bringing about the act, because that would mean two independent causes—effectiveness and power—combine on one effect, which is logically impossible. The author then reiterates that they have already shown that power has no capacity for influence at all. Consequently, power is not suitable for influence in any way. Next, the author turns to the attribute called 'action and creation' (al-fi'l wa al-takwin). The discussion presents a dilemma: if this attribute is like power in being suitable for both action and omission, then the same problems and arguments that were raised against power would apply here as well. But if it is determined specifically to the side of existence and not at all suitable for the side of non-existence, then it would not be a free and capable agent; rather, it would be a necessitator by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat)—that is, a necessary cause that cannot but produce the effect. Thus, the author clarifies that true causal agency is not power but effectiveness, and the attribute of action, if not neutral, leads to necessitarian causality.

Key Terms
الفاعلية
الموجدية
القدرة
الفعل
التكوين
موجب بالذات
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power (qudra) is the direct cause of acts, but the author argues that effectiveness (fa'iliyya) is the true cause, and power has no influence.
Correction: The passage clearly states that effectiveness and causative agency are the influencers, and power has no effect on the existence of the act. Two independent causes cannot combine on one effect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if the attribute of action (fi'l/takwin) were suitable for both action and omission?
A) It would be a necessary cause
B) It would lead to two independent causes
C) The same problems as with power would recur
D) It would have no effect

Key Takeaway
Effectiveness and causative agency are the true causes of existence, while power has no direct causal influence, and the attribute of action must be understood as either neutral (leading to the same issues as power) or determined (implying necessitarianism).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something, do you subconsciously attribute it to your own power and ability, or do you recognize that the true cause is Allah's effectiveness? How can you train your heart to see His hand in every outcome?
?
The passage argues that if the attribute of action were determined only to existence, it would be a necessitator, not a free agent. In your own life, do you sometimes feel like a victim of circumstances, as if your actions are forced? How can you embrace the freedom Allah has given you and take responsibility for your choices?

Philosophers' Concession: Capability vs. Alternation
Philosophers concede definition, deny alternation, announce proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' concession on the definition of qadir and their claim that capability does not require actual alternation in will.

Core Idea
Being able to do something doesn't mean you have to do it.

This passage opens the concluding section of the article, where the philosophers respond to earlier arguments. They accept the definition of a capable agent (qadir) as one who can act or refrain if they will. However, they reject the implication that this definition requires God to actually will action at one time and omission at another. The full argument, including two promised proofs, is cut short by a page break, but the key distinction between capability and actual alternation is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the philosophers begin by conceding the definition of qadir, then immediately deny the implication of alternation.
The passage ends abruptly; we only have the opening of their argument.
2
Identify the concession and the denial
Find where the philosophers say 'we concede' (سلمنا) and what they deny. The definition is about capability (if he wills...); the denial is that this entails alternation in God's actual will.
The phrase 'لا يقتضي' (does not entail) is the key logical operator.
3
Understand the core distinction
Reflect on the difference between capability (being able to do X if you will) and actual occurrence (actually doing X at some time). The philosophers argue that capability does not force actual alternation.
This distinction is central to the debate about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتختتم هذه المقالة بخاتمة وهي أن الفلاسفة قالوا : سلمنا : أن القادر هو الذي إن شاء أن يفعل فعل ، وإن شاء أن يترك ترك . إلا أن هذا لا يقتضي صدق قولنا : إنه تعالى إن شاء الفعل تارة ، والترك أخرى . ويدل عليه وجهان :
And this article is concluded with a conclusion, which is that the philosophers said: We concede that the capable agent (al-qadir) is the one who, if He wills to do, He does, and if He wills to refrain, He refrains. But this does not entail the truth of our statement that He, exalted, wills action sometimes and omission another time. And two proofs indicate this:
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the concluding section (khatima) of the article. The author presents the philosophers' response: they accept the definition of the 'capable agent' (al-qadir) as one who can act if He wills and refrain if He wills. However, they argue that this definition does not imply that God actually exercises both capabilities at different times—i.e., that He alternates between willing action and willing omission. In other words, the definition is about capability, not about actual variety in volition. The philosophers then promise to provide two proofs (wajhan) for this claim, but the text breaks off at this point due to the end of the page. This sets up a continuation that would likely elaborate on these proofs.

The Agent by Essence Necessitates Eternal Effect
Concludes that the agent by essence entails eternal effect, citing mutakallimun agreement and a reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun held that if God acts by essence, His effect must be eternal, and why a delayed effect without an intervening cause is impossible.

Core Idea
An agent by essence cannot produce a delayed effect without an intervening cause.

Having presented eleven proofs against the philosophers' notion of a capable agent, the author now draws the conclusion. If God's effect in creation arises from His very essence or necessary concomitants, then the effect must be co-eternal with Him – a point the mutakallimun themselves admitted. This passage solidifies the link between essence-based action and necessary eternal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the condensed argument style; the author builds on previous proofs.
The phrase 'eleven proofs' refers to earlier arguments not included here.
2
Identify the central claim
The passage asserts that if God's effect is from His essence or necessary concomitants, then the effect must be eternal. Underline this key sentence.
3
Examine the two supporting arguments
First, the mutakallimun themselves admitted that a necessitator by essence yields an eternal effect. Second, if the cause existed without effect for infinite time and then the effect occurred without a new cause, it would be pure chance – an absurdity.
These arguments show that necessity by essence cannot coexist with temporal delay.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument forces the mutakallimun to affirm that God's will is not a necessitation by essence but involves choice and temporal specification.
This sets up the next discussion on divine will and the world's temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا أحد عشر وجهاً، في تقرير أن القول بالقادر على التفسير الذي ذكروه محال. وإذا ثبت هذا، ظهر أن تأثيره تعالى في تكوين العالم، وفي تكوين سائر آثاره إنما كان لعين ذاته، أو للأمور اللازمة لذاته. وقد اتفق الكل على أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك، فإنه يلزم من قدمه الآثار والأفعال والنتائج. ولهذا السبب، فإن المتكلمين بعد أن أقاموا الدلالة على حدوث العالم، قالوا: لو كان المؤثر في حدوث العالم موجباً بالذات، لزم من قدمه، قدم العالم. وذلك يدل على أنهم كانوا معترفين بأن كل ما كان تأثيره لذاته، لزم من دوامه، دوام الأثر. وأيضاً: فإذا كان المؤثر الموجب بالذات حاصلاً أنه كان منفكاً عن الأثر، مدة غير متناهية، ثم حصل الأثر بعد ذلك، من غير أن اختص ذلك الوقت، بإرادة معينة، أو بنوع آخر من الأمور المقتضية للرجحان، فحينئذ يكون ذلك الحدوث في ذلك الوقت: حدوثاً، لا لمؤثر أصلاً، بل محض الاتفاق. وذلك: باطل: فيثبت: أنه يلزم من دوام الموجب بالذات، دوام الأثر والنتيجة. والله أعلم
So these are eleven proofs for establishing that the statement about the capable agent according to their explanation is impossible. And when this is established, it becomes clear that His effect — exalted is He — in the formation of the world and in the formation of all His other effects is only from His very essence or from matters necessary to His essence. And everyone has agreed that whenever the matter is so, then the effects, actions, and results follow necessarily from His eternity. And for this reason, the mutakallimun, after establishing the proof for the temporal origination of the world, said: If the effecting cause of the temporal origination of the world were a necessitator by essence, then the eternity of the world would follow from His eternity. And that indicates that they acknowledged that everything whose effect is from its essence, then from its duration follows the duration of the effect. And also: If the effecting necessitator by essence existed while being separated from the effect for an infinite period, then the effect occurred after that without that particular time being distinguished by a specific will or by another type of matter requiring preponderance, then that occurrence at that time would be an occurrence without any cause at all, but pure chance. And that is false. So it is established that from the duration of the necessitator by essence, the duration of the effect and result follows necessarily. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage concludes a series of eleven proofs rejecting the philosophers' notion of a 'capable agent' as they define it. The author argues that if God's effect in creating the world and all His effects is from His very essence or from matters necessarily concomitant with His essence, then everyone agrees that this necessitates that the effects, actions, and results are co-eternal with Him. The mutakallimun themselves, after proving the world's temporal origination, stated that if the cause of origination were a necessitator by essence, the world would be eternal from His eternity. This shows they admitted that whatever acts by essence has its effect co-eternal with it. The author then adds a second argument: if the necessitator by essence were separated from its effect for an infinite time, and then the effect occurred without any specific will or preponderating factor at that moment, the occurrence would be uncaused and purely contingent, which is absurd. Hence, duration of the necessitator by essence entails duration of the effect and result.

القادر
المؤثر
دوام الأثر
حدوث العالم
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mutakallimun deny any form of necessitation, but they admit that if the agent acts by essence, the effect follows necessarily.
Correction: The mutakallimun accept that an essence-based cause yields an eternal effect; they only deny that God acts by essence without will.

Checkpoint

A) The effect is temporally originated after the agent.
B) The effect is eternal if the agent is eternal and acts by essence.
C) The effect occurs only if the agent chooses to create it.
D) The effect can occur without any cause.

Key Takeaway
The duration of the necessitator by essence entails the duration of the effect and result necessarily.

God's Power and the Avoidance of Infinite Regress
Second aspect: God's power does not imply actual willing, avoiding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power over an action does not require Him to actually will it, and how affirming a created will leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's power is independent of His actually willing an action.

This passage presents the second argument against the claim that God's power necessarily implies that He must will every action He is capable of. The author shows that describing God as powerful does not depend on whether He actually performs or leaves an action. He then carefully distinguishes the conditional statement 'if He wills, He does' from the claim that He actually wills, and demonstrates the latter leads to an infinite regress of wills and makes His essence a locus of temporal events—both impossible for the eternal God. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors and key terms.
Notice the author's structure: an initial observation, then a conditional, then a reduction ad absurdum.
2
Identify the key observation about power
The author observes that in both the state of acting and not acting, God is correctly described as powerful. This means power does not depend on actual willing.
The phrase 'في الحالتين يصدق عليه أنه قادر' is crucial.
3
Understand the conditional and its limits
The author affirms the conditional 'if He wills, He does', but clarifies that this does not imply He actually willed. The difference between 'if' and 'actual' is central.
See the shift from 'إن شاء الفعل فعل' to 'لا يصدق عليه البتة أنه شاء الترك'.
4
Trace the regress argument
The author argues that if God actually willed the leaving, that will would be created (muhdatha) and would require another created will, leading to an infinite chain without a first.
Note the terms 'محدَثة' and 'تسلسل'.
5
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion: the regress is impossible, and it would make God's essence a locus of temporal events, which is also impossible. This safeguards divine transcendence.
The final sentence 'وكل ذلك محال' sums up the impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : وهو أن حال كونه تاركاً للفعل، يستحيل أن يصدق عليه كونه فاعلاً له ، وحال كونه فاعلاً له يستحيل أن يصدق عليه : كونه تاركاً له . وفي الحالتين يصدق عليه : أنه قادر على الفعل . فيثبت : أن صدق كونه قادراً على الفعل ، لا يتوقف على صدق أنه شاء الفعل ، ففعله . أو شاء الترك ، فتركه . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إنه حق أنه سبحانه ، إن شاء الفعل فعل ، وإن شاء الترك ترك . لكن لا يلزم منه أن يقال : إنه شاء الترك فتركه . بل نقول : إنه يصدق عليه : أنه إن شاء الترك ، لتركه أنه لا يصدق عليه البتة : أنه شاء الترك. وإلا لكانت تلك المشيئة : محدثة . ولا افتقرت تلك المشيئة إلى مشيئة أخرى حادثة ، ولكان الكلام في تلك المشيئة كما في الأولى فيلزم أن يكون كل مشيئة حادثة مسبوقة بمشيئة أخرى حادثة ، لا إلى أول . ويلزم كون ذاته محلا للحوادث . وكل ذلك محال والله أعلم.
And the second aspect: that in the state of His leaving the action, it is impossible that He is described as doing it, and in the state of His doing it, it is impossible that He is described as leaving it. And in both states He is described as being powerful over the action. So it is established that the truth of His being powerful over the action does not depend on the truth that He willed the action and did it, or willed the leaving and left it. When this is established, we say: It is true that He, exalted, if He wills the action He does it, and if He wills the leaving He leaves it. But it does not follow from that that it is said that He willed the leaving and left it. Rather, we say: It is true of Him that if He willed the leaving, He would leave it—but it is not at all true that He willed the leaving. Otherwise that will would be created (muhdatha). And that will would not need another created will, and the discussion about that will would be as in the first, so that every created will would be preceded by another created will, without a first. And it would necessitate that His essence be a locus for events (mahall lil-hawadith). And all of that is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thani) against the claim that God's power necessarily implies actual alternation of will and action. The author observes that in the state of not doing an action, it is impossible to describe God as doing it, and vice versa, yet in both states He is correctly described as powerful (qadir) over the action. Thus, being powerful does not depend on actually willing and performing the action at a particular time. The author then affirms the conditional: it is true that if God wills an action, He does it, and if He wills to leave it, He leaves it. However, this does not imply that God ever actually willed the leaving. Otherwise, that will would be created (muhdatha), and it would require another created will to bring it into existence, leading to an infinite regress of wills (tasalsul). Moreover, this would make God's essence a locus of temporal events (mahall lil-hawadith), which is impossible for the eternal, unchanging God. The argument thus preserves divine transcendence by affirming that God's power and will are eternal and not subject to temporal succession or change.

Key Terms
قادر
مشيئة محدثة
تسلسل
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's power over an action implies He must have willed it at some point in time.
Correction: The author argues that God is described as powerful whether or not He actually wills an action; power does not depend on actual willing.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if we said God actually willed the leaving of an action?
A) It would be consistent with God's eternal will.
B) It would necessitate that God's will is created and lead to an infinite regress.
C) It would prove that God is not powerful.
D) It would imply that God does not exist.

Key Takeaway
God's power does not require Him to actually will every possible action; affirming a created will leads to an impossible infinite regress.

The Impossibility of an Infinite Chain of Divine Wills
Argument against infinite regress of divine wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of wills in God is impossible and why it would imply temporal events in His essence.

Core Idea
A will that requires another will leads to an endless chain, impossible for the Divine.

This passage from the Matalib addresses a subtle objection: if God's will to abandon something were itself willed, that would create a chain of wills without end. The author shows that such a regress is impossible because it would make God's essence a locus of temporal events. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and then the English translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
2
Follow the conditional argument
The author starts with a case: if God wills abandonment, it does not mean He willed the will itself. If He did, that will would be temporal.
This is the key turn.
3
Understand the infinite regress
If that will were temporal, it would require another will, and so on without end. This is impossible because it would make God's essence a locus of temporal events.
The conclusion is that the initial will is not itself willed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع الترك فتركه. بل نقول: إنه يصدق عليه: أنه إن شاء الترك، [لتركه] أنه لا يصدق عليه البتة: أنه شاء الترك. وإلا لكانت تلك المشيئة محدثة. ولا افتقرت تلك المشيئة إلى مشيئة أخرى حادثة، ولكان الكلام في تلك المشيئة كما في الأولى فيلزم أن يكون كل مشيئة حادثة مسبوقة بمشيئة أخرى حادثة، لا إلى أول. ويلزم كون ذاته محلا للحوادث. وكل ذلك محال. والله أعلم.
With abandonment, his abandonment. Rather we say: it is true of Him that if He wills abandonment, [for him to abandon it] that it is not true of Him at all that He wills abandonment. Otherwise, that will would be temporal. And that will would not require another temporal will, and the discussion about that will would be like the discussion about the first. Then it would necessitate that every temporal will is preceded by another temporal will, without end. And it would necessitate that His essence is a locus for temporal events. And all of that is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof by reductio ad absurdum. It begins with a statement about abandonment (tark): when something is abandoned, the act of abandonment is not itself willed in a separate sense. The author clarifies: if God wills to abandon something, it is not true that He willed abandonment in a way that the abandonment itself is a distinct object of will. Because if that were the case, that will (the will to abandon) would be a temporal (created) occurrence. And if that will were temporal, it would have to have been produced by another will, leading to an infinite chain of wills, each preceding the other without a first. This infinite regress is impossible because it would imply that God's essence contains temporal events (hawadith), which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The conclusion is that the will to abandon is not itself willed, breaking the regress. Thus, the divine will is uncreated and not subject to another will.

Key Terms
مع الترك
مشيئة
محدثة
حوادث
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every will must be willed by another will.
Correction: The author shows that leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible for God. Some wills (like the will to abandon) are not themselves willed.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude would happen if every will required a prior will?
A) An infinite regress of wills without a beginning
B) God’s will would be eternal
C) Will would be unnecessary
D) It would prove God’s power

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of wills is impossible because it would make God's essence a locus of temporal events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an infinite chain of wills is impossible for God, what does that reveal about your own tendency to endlessly deliberate without decisive action? Will you commit to trusting God's singular will in your next difficult decision?
?
This lesson shows that God's will is not caused by another will. How does this challenge your habit of overthinking and creating justifications for every choice? Can you take one step today to act on faith rather than waiting for an endless series of reasons?
?
Consider a past regret where you wished you could redo a choice. Did you treat it as if you could have an infinite chain of 'if onlys'? How does the impossibility of infinite regress call you to accept God's decree and move forward with trust?

The fourth article shifts the argument to proofs grounded in God's will.

Refutation of the Philosophers' Definition of Divine Will
The author refutes the philosophers' definition of will as applied to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' definition of will cannot apply to God and how the author critiques both that definition and the mutakallimun's alternative.

Core Idea
Human will implies need; God's will is free of need.

This lesson examines the philosophers' claim that will is a composite of belief, inclination, and power. The author argues that such a definition cannot apply to the Necessary Existence because it entails benefit and inclination, which are impossible for God. He also dismisses the mutakallimun's alternative as logically unsound.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' definition
Read the passage where the philosophers define will as belief in benefit/pleasure causing inclination combined with physical power.
Notice the three components: cognition, inclination, and power.
2
Identify the two refutations
The author gives two reasons why this definition cannot apply to God: benefit/harm are impossible for Him, and inclination implies need.
3
Consider the mutakallimun's view
The author mentions that mutakallimun define will as preponderance without a preponderating factor, but he dismisses this as unintelligible.
Ask yourself: why is preponderance without a cause impossible?
4
Reflect on divine will
Think about how divine will must be different from human will – it is eternal, not dependent on benefit, and does not involve inclination or change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدلائل المستنبطة من صفة الإرادة
قالت الفلاسفة : الذي نعقل من الإرادة هو أنا اعتقدنا في شيء أنه نافع ولذيذ ، صار هذا الاعتقاد موجبا لحصول الميل إلى تحصيل ذلك الشيء ، ذلك الاعتقاد منضماً إلى القدرة الموجودة في العضلات ، ثم إن ذلك الميل مع وهي المسماة بالقوة المحركة ، فيصير مجموع هذه الأشياء موجبا لحصول الفعل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذه الأحوال في حق واجب الوجود لذاته : محال . أما تصور كون الفعل المعين نافعاً له ، أو ضاراً له ، فهذا إنما يتصور في حق من يعقل في حقه الانتفاع بشيء [ أو التضرر بشيء ] آخر وذلك في حق واجب الوجود : محال . وأما حصول ذلك الميل فهو أيضاً : محال لأن الميل إلى جلب المنافع ، إنما يعقل في حق من يصح عليه النفع . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن حصول الإرادة في حق الله تعالى : محال . وأما الذي يقوله المتكلمون من أن الإرادة تقتضي رجحان أحد الميلين على الآخر لا لمرجح [ أصلاً ، فهذا أيضاً غير معقول البتة ، لأن الرجحان بدون المرجح ] محال غير
On the proofs derived from the attribute of will. The philosophers said: what we understand from will is that we believe something to be beneficial and pleasurable; this belief becomes a cause for the occurrence of inclination towards obtaining that thing, that belief being joined with the power existing in the muscles. Then that inclination, along with what is called the motive power, so the sum of these things becomes a cause for the occurrence of the act. If this is established, then we say: these states with respect to the Necessary Existence in Itself are impossible. As for conceiving that a particular act is beneficial to Him or harmful to Him, this is only conceivable with respect to one for whom benefiting by something [or being harmed by something] else is conceivable, and that with respect to the Necessary Existence is impossible. As for the occurrence of that inclination, it is also impossible because inclination towards obtaining benefits is only conceivable with respect to one for whom benefit is possible. And if this is established, then it becomes clear that the occurrence of will with respect to God Most High is impossible. As for what the mutakallimun say, that will necessitates the preponderance of one of the two inclinations over the other without any preponderating factor [at all, this also is utterly unintelligible, because preponderance without a preponderating factor] is impossible, except...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' definition of will (al-irada) as applied to God. The author begins by stating the topic: proofs derived from the attribute of will. He then quotes the philosophers' definition: will consists of an intellectual cognition that something is beneficial or pleasurable, which produces an inclination (mayl) to attain that thing, combined with physical power. The inclination together with the motive force results in action. The author argues that such a composite state cannot apply to the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud). First, conceiving a particular act as beneficial or harmful to God is impossible because God cannot be affected by benefit or harm. Second, the inclination to seek benefit is only conceivable for one who can benefit, which God cannot. Therefore, will as defined by philosophers cannot exist in God. The author then mentions the mutakallimun's alternative definition: will is a preponderance of one inclination over another without any external preponderating factor. However, he dismisses this as unintelligible because preponderance without a preponderating factor is impossible. The passage ends abruptly with 'impossible except...' likely continuing with the author's own positive definition.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existence)
ميل (inclination)
قوة محركة (motive power)
رجحان (preponderance)
مرجح (preponderating factor)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will in God is similar to human will.
Correction: Human will involves need and change; God's will is eternal and independent.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' definition, what components are necessary for will before an action occurs?
A) Cognition of benefit, inclination, and physical power
B) Random chance and divine decree
C) Only a command without any prior cognition
D) A preponderance without a preponderating factor

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' definition of will is incompatible with God's nature because it implies benefit and inclination, which are impossible for the Necessary Existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God 'desires' something in the same way you desire a treat or comfort—based on some perceived need? How does this lesson's refusal to attribute benefit-seeking to God challenge that assumption, and what can you do today to purify your concept of God's will?
?
The philosophers argued that if an act is not beneficial, there is no reason to choose it. In your life, do you only do things when you see a clear benefit for yourself? Where has God asked you to act without any obvious personal gain, and how does that reflect His transcendent will—free from need?
?
The author dismisses the idea that will is a preponderance without a cause as 'unintelligible'. Think of a time you assumed something happened without any deeper reason—maybe a 'coincidence' or 'luck'. How can you train yourself to see every event as stemming from God's wise will, which always has a perfect cause, even if hidden from you?

The Philosophers' Definition of Will
The philosophers define will analytically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of will as a composite process involving belief in benefit, inclination, power, and motive force that leads to action.

Core Idea
Will is a causal chain starting with belief in benefit.

The philosophers begin by defining what they mean by will. This definition is crucial because it sets the terms for their argument that God cannot possess will. In this lesson, we examine their precise definition and its components.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the philosophers break down will into distinct components.
This is a causal definition, not a mere description.
2
Identify the components
List the key elements: belief in benefit, inclination, power in muscles, motive force. Understand how each leads to the next.
3
Reflect on the role of belief
Consider why the philosophers start with belief. Why is it essential? This will matter when they argue against attributing will to God.
Belief in benefit is the initiating factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الذي نعقل من الإرادة هو أنا اعتقدنا في شيء أنه نافع ولذيذ ، صار هذا الاعتقاد موجبا حصول الميل إلى تحصيل ذلك الشيء ، ذلك الاعتقاد ، منضمان إلى القدرة الموجودة في العضلات ، ثم إن ذلك الميل مع وهي المسماة بالقوة المحركة ، فيصير مجموع هذه الأشياء موجبا لحصول الفعل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذه الأحوال في حق واجب الوجود لذاته : محال . أما تصور كون الفعل المعين نافعاً له ، أو ضاراً له ، فهذا إنما يتصور في حق من يعقل في حقه الانتفاع بشيء أو التضرر بشيء آخر وذلك في حق واجب الوجود : محال . وأما حصول ذلك الميل فهو أيضاً : محال لأن الميل إلى جلب المنافع ، إنما يعقل في حق من يصح عليه النفع . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن حصول الإرادة في حق الله تعالى : محال . وأما الذي يقوله المتكلمون من أن الإرادة تقتضي رجحان أحد الميلين على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلاً ، فهذا أيضاً غير معقول البتة ، لأن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال غير معقول . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لم يبق عند العقل من لفظ الإرادة ، معنى يمكن إثباته في حق الله تعالى . فكان القول به باطلاً .
The philosophers said: What we understand of will is that we believe in something that it is beneficial and pleasurable, this belief necessitates the occurrence of inclination to obtain that thing; that belief, combined with the power existing in muscles; then that inclination, along with what is called the motive force, and the sum of these things necessitates the occurrence of the act. If this is established, we say: These states are impossible with respect to the Necessary Existence (Wajib al-Wujud) in Himself. As for conceiving a particular act to be beneficial or harmful to Him, this is only conceivable for one who can be benefited by something or harmed by something else, and that is impossible with respect to the Necessary Existence. As for the occurrence of that inclination, it is also impossible, because inclination to attract benefits is only conceivable for one for whom benefit is possible. And if this is established, it has become clear that the occurrence of will (al-irada) with respect to God, exalted, is impossible. As for what the mutakallimun say, that will necessitates the preponderance of one of the two inclinations over the other with no preponderator at all, this is also absolutely unintelligible, because preponderance without a preponderator (al-murajjih) is impossible and unintelligible. So by this it is established: there remains in the intellect, from the word 'will', no meaning that can be affirmed with respect to God, exalted. So the affirmation of it is false.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' definition of will and their argument that it cannot be attributed to God. They begin by defining will as a combination of belief in benefit, inclination, and power, leading to action. They then argue that these components are impossible for God because He cannot be affected by benefit or harm, and inclination is only meaningful for one who can receive benefit. They also reject the mutakallimun's notion of will as preponderance without a preponderator, deeming it unintelligible. The conclusion is that no intelligible meaning of will remains that can be affirmed of God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is a simple, indivisible attribute.
Correction: The philosophers define will as a composite of belief, inclination, and power, not a simple entity.

Checkpoint
Which component initiates the process of will according to the philosophers?
A) Inclination
B) Power in muscles
C) Belief in benefit
D) Motive force

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define will as a composite process initiated by belief in benefit, leading through inclination and power to action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the philosophers define will as beginning with belief in benefit. Look at your own desires: Do you only want what you believe is beneficial? What about desires that persist even when you know they are harmful? What does that tension reveal about the philosophers' claim?
?
If will is caused by belief, then how accountable are you for your actions? Does this definition relieve you of responsibility, or does it shift the focus to the beliefs you hold? What false beliefs might be driving your will right now without your awareness?
?
The philosophers say will is a composite of cognition, inclination, and power. Does this description match your experience of making a difficult moral choice? If not, what is missing? What does your resistance to this definition reveal about your own understanding of the will?

Why Will Is Impossible for God
The philosophers argue the impossibility of will for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that the components of will—belief in benefit and inclination—are impossible for God.

Core Idea
God cannot be affected by benefit or harm.

After defining will, the philosophers apply this definition to God. They argue that each component—conceiving an act as beneficial, and having inclination—cannot exist in God because He is beyond need and change. This lesson explores their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the claim, then two sub-arguments.
2
Understand the first impossibility
The philosophers argue that conceiving a particular act as beneficial or harmful is impossible for God because He cannot be affected by anything.
This targets the belief component of will.
3
Understand the second impossibility
They then argue that inclination to attract benefits is also impossible, since benefit cannot apply to God.
This targets the inclination component.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الذي نعقل من الإرادة هو أنا اعتقدنا في شيء أنه نافع ولذيذ ، صار هذا الاعتقاد موجبا حصول الميل إلى تحصيل ذلك الشيء ، ذلك الاعتقاد ، منضمان إلى القدرة الموجودة في العضلات ، ثم إن ذلك الميل مع وهي المسماة بالقوة المحركة ، فيصير مجموع هذه الأشياء موجبا لحصول الفعل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذه الأحوال في حق واجب الوجود لذاته : محال . أما تصور كون الفعل المعين نافعاً له ، أو ضاراً له ، فهذا إنما يتصور في حق من يعقل في حقه الانتفاع بشيء أو التضرر بشيء آخر وذلك في حق واجب الوجود : محال . وأما حصول ذلك الميل فهو أيضاً : محال لأن الميل إلى جلب المنافع ، إنما يعقل في حق من يصح عليه النفع . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن حصول الإرادة في حق الله تعالى : محال . وأما الذي يقوله المتكلمون من أن الإرادة تقتضي رجحان أحد الميلين على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلاً ، فهذا أيضاً غير معقول البتة ، لأن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال غير معقول . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لم يبق عند العقل من لفظ الإرادة ، معنى يمكن إثباته في حق الله تعالى . فكان القول به باطلاً .

So by this it is established: there remains in the intellect, from the word 'will', no meaning that can be affirmed with respect to God, exalted. So the affirmation of it is false.

?

?

?
Think of a time you felt entitled to God’s help because you ‘deserved’ it. How does the reality that God is beyond benefit and harm reshape your understanding of gratitude and reliance on Him?

Refutation of the Mutakallimun's Concept of Will
The philosophers reject the mutakallimun's definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rejection of the mutakallimun's definition of will as preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
Preponderance without a preponderator is unintelligible.

After arguing that their own definition of will cannot apply to God, the philosophers address the alternative definition proposed by the mutakallimun (theologians of kalām). The mutakallimun defined will as the preponderance of one inclination over another without any external cause. The philosophers reject this as logically impossible, completing their case that no intelligible meaning of will can be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the rejection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The philosophers state the mutakallimun's view and then criticize it.
2
Understand the mutakallimun's claim
The mutakallimun say that will is preponderance (tarjīḥ) of one inclination over another with no preponderator (murjiḥ).
Preponderator means cause or reason for the preference.
3
Grasp the philosophers' objection
The philosophers argue that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible and unintelligible. They consider it a logical contradiction.
This is a foundational principle in philosophy: every effect needs a cause.
4
Consider the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that no meaning of 'will' remains that can be affirmed of God, so the claim that God has will is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الذي نعقل من الإرادة هو أنا اعتقدنا في شيء أنه نافع ولذيذ ، صار هذا الاعتقاد موجبا حصول الميل إلى تحصيل ذلك الشيء ، ذلك الاعتقاد ، منضمان إلى القدرة الموجودة في العضلات ، ثم إن ذلك الميل مع وهي المسماة بالقوة المحركة ، فيصير مجموع هذه الأشياء موجبا لحصول الفعل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذه الأحوال في حق واجب الوجود لذاته : محال . أما تصور كون الفعل المعين نافعاً له ، أو ضاراً له ، فهذا إنما يتصور في حق من يعقل في حقه الانتفاع بشيء أو التضرر بشيء آخر وذلك في حق واجب الوجود : محال . وأما حصول ذلك الميل فهو أيضاً : محال لأن الميل إلى جلب المنافع ، إنما يعقل في حق من يصح عليه النفع . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فقد ظهر أن حصول الإرادة في حق الله تعالى : محال . وأما الذي يقوله المتكلمون من أن الإرادة تقتضي رجحان أحد الميلين على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلاً ، فهذا أيضاً غير معقول البتة ، لأن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال غير معقول . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لم يبق عند العقل من لفظ الإرادة ، معنى يمكن إثباته في حق الله تعالى . فكان القول به باطلاً .

As for the occurrence of that inclination, it is also impossible, because inclination to attract benefits is only conceivable for one for whom benefit is possible. And if this is established, it has become clear that the occurrence of will (al-irada) with respect to God, exalted, is impossible. So by this it is established: there remains in the intellect, from the word 'will', no meaning that can be affirmed with respect to God, exalted. So the affirmation of it is false.

?
